HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
WITH SLOWING PC CHIP SALES, ZILOG WILL TRIM WORK FORCE

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Zilog Inc. said yesterday that it would cut 7.3 percent of its work force to cope with slowing sales of its specialty computer chips used in consumer electronics and personal computer products. Zilog said it would reduce its 1,650-member work force by eliminating 120 jobs at its manufacturing plant in Nampa, Idaho. Texas Pacific Group acquired Zilog and took it private earlier this year. The company eliminated 33 jobs in sales and at its headquarters last month to cut costs. Zilog, based in Campbell, Calif., said it would offer employees four to 10 weeks of severance pay, health benefits through December and assistance in finding new jobs.

HEADLINE: National News Briefs;
Arizona Seizes License Of Camp for Delinquents

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: PHOENIX, Aug. 26

 BODY:
A boot-camp-style center for juvenile delinquents lost its license today after an Arizona state investigation found that its employees had contributed to the death of a teen-ager.
The director of the Arizona Department of Economic Security, Linda J. Blessing, said investigators had found that 17 employees of the camp, Boys Ranch, abused and neglected Nicholaus Contreraz, 16, of Sacramento, Calif. 
 The teen-ager collapsed and died on March 2 at a Boys Ranch unit in Oracle after being forced to do exercises. An autopsy concluded that he had a massive lung infection.
A Department of Economic Security report released today said employees had abused Nicholaus and accused him of faking fevers, exhaustion, vomiting and other symptoms of the infection.
The president of Boys Ranch, Bob Thomas, said he would appeal the license removal.
Ms. Blessing said Boys Ranch could regain its license if substantial improvements were made.
The employees accused in Nicholaus's death have been dismissed, laid off or resigned.

HEADLINE: METRO BUSINESS;
New York Recruiter Lands 7 Businesses

BYLINE: By Nick Ravo 

 BODY:
The New York City Economic Development Corporation announced yesterday that its business recruitment division had attracted seven new high-technology companies to the city over the last six months.
The companies that the division said it helped bring to the city are Hotelnet USA Inc., an international computer services company; Silicon Alley Telecommunications Corporation, Icom, Jenkins Professional Services Net, Absolute Reality, RTFM Consulting and Ecentric Consulting. 
 Hotelnet furnishes hotel guest rooms with computers that provide Internet access, word processing, games and electronic mail, among other services. The company occupies 1,400 square feet at 90 William Street, which is designed and equipped for growing information technology companies. It is a so-called Plug 'n' Go building, part of a program created by the city last year to encourage the growth of the new media industry. The program is a public-private partnership among the city, the Alliance for Downtown New York and the owners of several downtown buildings.
The business recruitment division, which was created in 1996, promotes the advantages of doing business in New York City and helps companies with available services and programs, like financing and tax incentives. NICK RAVO

HEADLINE: THEATER REVIEW;
Brooklyn's Irish: a Clear-Eyed Toast

BYLINE: By PETER MARKS 

 BODY:
With the cruel relish of a bleak Irish wit, Colin Quinn recalls for us the peculiar narrow-minded characters of his Brooklyn youth: J. T., the scornful druggie; Aidan, the venomous alcoholic; Margaret, the perpetual martyr; Jimmy, the judgmental do-nothing. Yet for all the dark and finely drawn characters he introduces in the course of his one-man show, the most surprising character on the stage of the Helen Hayes Theater is Mr. Quinn himself.
Given his prominent television comedy job, as the new anchor of "Weekend Update" on "Saturday Night Live," you might have assumed that "Colin Quinn: An Irish Wake," which opened last night, would be a satirical spinoff of his late-night showcase. But if you were expecting a standup comic, a Seinfeldesque brush with Broadway, fuhgeddaboutit. What you get in Mr. Quinn instead is an honest-to-goodness seanachai. 
 A who? A seanachai, which is pronounced SHAWN-uh-kee: an Irish storyteller, a weaver of legends. At the foot of a set of steps, designed by Eugene Lee to resemble the stoops of Park Slope, Mr. Quinn spends a little more than an hour recreating the twisted universe of his late adolescence and invoking the small-town voices that echo on the insular blocks of the big city. He seems relieved to have blasted out of that universe, because the voices are harsh; there is not a scintilla of sentimentality in Mr. Quinn's admirably brutal, mostly unlovable portraits. But they certainly sound authentic, to the point where sections of "An Irish Wake" feel like documentary outtakes.
The strength of "An Irish Wake," which Mr. Quinn wrote with Lou DiMaggio, and which is directed by Bobby Moresco, is in its specificity and literacy. The vehemence of Aidan's attack on a boy who fails to bring him a proper Scotch and soda ("The little pimple-faced Judas has sold me out for 30 pieces of silver," Mr. Quinn declares in an Irish accent), the self-abnegating response of Margaret when a mourner stomps on her foot -- "No, it's O.K., honey, I'll offer it up" -- indicate a highly developed ear for the ironically funny detail, a pleasing awareness of the magnetic appeal of precision in language. He's a tale-spinner who chooses his words with the utmost care.
Still, there's the persistent sense of an assignment unfinished about "Colin Quinn: An Irish Wake," and it has more to do with Mr. Quinn the performer than the writer. Unlike other soloists who speak in multiple voices, like John Leguizamo and Anna Deavere Smith, Mr. Quinn is no chameleon. As is apparent in the roles he gets on "Saturday Night Live," he usually plays Colin Quinn. That's not a knock. He's extremely funny; his smart-alecky "Weekend Update" spots have reinvigorated that hoary feature. Although he makes no claim on the stage of the Helen Hayes to be an impressionist -- roundish and impish, he charts a neutral performance course in conjuring his neighborhood cronies, relying on subtler cues in the text to make the people come alive -- a bit of the mimic's craft here would not be amiss.
The trouble is, his characters are all specific in the same ways; it's difficult, at times, to figure out where one person begins and another ends. And the 39-year-old Mr. Quinn, who is a genial but not especially warm presence, makes the dangerous assumption that you arrive at the theater well-versed in the kinds of people and situations he knows so well; his shorthand discussions of the seven sacraments, around which he structures the stories, will mystify many a non-Roman Catholic. He provides so little context in this piece, in fact, it's as if you had been invited to a friend's office party, where everyone is telling jokes about people you've never heard of. You want to laugh with them, but mostly what you feel is left out.
The monologue is set on a day in 1976 when a beloved veteran of the neighborhood, Jackie Ryan, has died, and the community gathers for his wake. One by one, Mr. Quinn sizes up the working-class Irish characters who made up his world. The respect he pays them is that the portraits are unvarnished. These are people who fall easily into black moods, who complain and curse with equal fluency, who view love as a bitter pill and a life's progress as falling naturally into the regulatory path of the Catholic Church.
"Here you had a group of people who had spent their whole lives doing what they were told, kept their mouths shut, beaten by the nuns and told that sex was bad," one of the characters explains. "Then 20 years later, when our marriages were filled with silence, violence and bad sex, we blamed each other."
Mr. Quinn's people are ferociously devoted to their narrow ways; they are fatalistic and terribly hard on one another. And they're brawlers who lash out madly at ideas that frighten them. At Jackie's wake, his pal Jimmy notes, "There was the sober Jackie, who played stickball with the neighborhood kids, and there was the Jackie three beers later who chased an Econoline van with a broken-off car antenna, because he thought he saw a peace sign on the window."
The storyteller is not a benign presence. The stories he selects do not portray these people in the most favorable light. They are a carelessly nasty crew. Aidan hisses "Whore!" at the boy who serves him the wrong drink; J. T. has sex on the grave of his grandmother. What binds these souls is a duty to religion, a sneering mistrust of the rest of the world and a sense that the way things are is just the way things are. These are small stories about small people, and they get a bit swallowed up on a Broadway stage. Perhaps in more intimate surroundings, the clear-eyed Mr. Quinn's tormented characters would cast bigger shadows.
 
COLIN QUINN: AN IRISH WAKE
 
Written by Mr. Quinn and Lou DiMaggio; directed by Bobby Moresco; production designer, Eugene Lee; lighting by Roger Morgan; general manager, Paul Morer Productions; associate producer, Bobby Spillane; consulting producer, Michael Shoemaker. Presented by Delsener Slater/Lorne Michaels. At the Helen Hayes Theater, 240 West 44th Street, Manhattan.

HEADLINE: The Fed's Modest Agenda

BYLINE: By James K. Galbraith; James K. Galbraith, an economist at the University of Texas, is the author of "Created Unequal: The Crisis in American Pay."

DATELINE: AUSTIN, Tex.

 BODY:
Today is the last day of the Federal Reserve's annual retreat in Jackson Hole, Wyoming. And to my surprise, they have been discussing economic inequality.
Why surprise? Because in March 1997 the Fed's chairman, Alan Greenspan, declared to Congress that there was no relationship between Federal Reserve policy and the increase in the wage gap. 
 "It is a development which I feel uncomfortable with," Mr. Greenspan said. "There is nothing monetary policy can do to address that, and it is outside the scope, so far as I am concerned, of the issues with which we deal."
Have things changed? Has the Federal Reserve now decided that it can and should address inequality?
It ought to. Unemployment, most economists agree, is affected by Fed policies. And over a very long time, back to 1920 in my calculations, unemployment rates have affected pay inequality. When unemployment rises, so does wage inequality. And when unemployment rates decrease, wage inequality goes down too.
Indeed, from the early 1970's through the mid-1980's, and again in the early 1990's, the Federal Reserve's unstable and high interest rates produced high unemployment and drove wage inequality up.
The recent record is much better. Since 1995, Federal Reserve policies have kept interest rates largely stable and allowed unemployment to fall. As history predicts, these policies have finally started to close the wage gap between rich and poor. Last year, for the first time, Americans with the lowest incomes experienced the highest rates of wage gain.
Of course, a rising minimum wage helped. Still, Mr. Greenspan and his team can rightly take some credit for progress on the inequality front.
Unfortunately, that has never been the Federal Reserve's line. Recently the Federal Reserve Bank of New York published a report giving an experts' consensus on why earnings inequality rose in the 1980's and early 1990's. It blamed the rise on technology, trade, the decline in unions, immigration and the then-falling real minimum wage. Unemployment was not even offered as an explanation.
But most economists long ago agreed that rising trade with poor countries plays only a small role, since wage inequality also increased in sectors in which products are not traded internationally.
And even many of those who blame technology have been forced to back off. They had blamed computers for the wage gap, but the spread of computers was much too late and the rise in inequality was much too early, for this technology to have been the prime cause.
Today, the Asian and the Russian crises clearly threaten the American economy and our low rate of unemployment -- and the progress we've made on the wage front.
But the Federal Reserve isn't concerned enough yet to act. Its main focus is still on the phantom threat of inflation. At its meeting last week, the Fed decided properly not to raise rates, but apparently didn't consider cutting them. And unless there has been a radical shift in view, the virtues of full employment won't be high on the agenda in Jackson Hole.
At their mountain retreat, the Federal Reserve should forget about technology, trade and immigration, and focus on the policies it actually controls -- interest rates and their influence on economic growth and employment.
And then, at its next policy meeting, the Fed should make a sharp cut in interest rates. That would be the right first step.

HEADLINE: Time To Get Serious In Africa

BYLINE: By Chester A. Crocker; Chester A. Crocker, a professor of diplomacy at Georgetown University, was Assistant Secretary of State for African Affairs in the Reagan Administration.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
As we sift through the wreckage from terrorist bombs in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam, and mount retaliatory strikes against the bad guys, we are reminded once again of our uniquely American vulnerability to terrorism. But the current turmoil in Central Africa, where rival African alliances are fighting for and against Laurent Kabila's Congo regime, should also serve as a lesson. We must not let a natural preoccupation with our own physical security blind us to the need for realistic policies in Africa that will deter or check the spread of conflict and protect our growing interests in the region.
If you listened only to the prose from official Washington, however, you would miss this point. We are urged to think in terms of an African "renaissance," a term coined by Thabo Mbeki, Nelson Mandela's designated successor in South Africa, to capture the idealism of a new generation of African leaders. 
 Having devoted 12 days to a Presidential trip to the region in March, and with African trade legislation supported by both parties pending in Congress, the White House is at pains to move beyond the basket-case stereotypes and accentuate the positive in Africa. Our declared role is to engage with African nations as partners, not proxies or pawns, and to help bring them into the global mainstream.
Accordingly, we are stressing open markets as a way to forge economic links, we are promoting democracy and human rights, and we are trying to strengthen the ability of African countries to resolve their own conflicts. There is merit in some of this rhetorical repositioning. Africa is diversifying. From a history of authoritarianism and stagnation, the majority of African countries now have growing economies and liberalized political systems. The United States is poised like no other nation to enjoy the fruits of engagement with a successful Africa -- if such a thing emerges.
The problem is that 10 years after the Berlin Wall came down, the United States still has not decided how to attain these goals -- and ignores the bigger problems. State Department officials speak of the region's "remaining" conflicts as if they were an endangered species.
But nearly all of Africa's most important countries -- Algeria, Angola, Congo, Ethiopia, Nigeria and the Sudan -- are caught up in domestic strife or external conflict. The renewed fighting in Congo directly affects conflicts in six neighboring countries -- with potentially devastating consequences for the broader African region. There will be no African renaissance unless these conflicts are resolved and some new and reasonably predictable basis for stability and legitimate order emerges.
In this decade, the United States has tried to end conflict and to promote democracy in many different ways. We withdrew our support from cold war era autocrats, like Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire and Mohammed Siad Barre of Somalia, and tacitly encouraged their enemies and neighbors to seize power. But as we're seeing now in Congo, this led to factional strife over the carcass of the abandoned, "failed" state.
In Somalia, which was descending into clan warfare, we tried muscular humanitarian intervention in the form of United Nations-authorized peace enforcement. After 18 troops were killed, however, we spent much of the next five years discouraging, blocking and sabotaging proposals for robust military action in countries like Rwanda, where no other action could have stopped genocidal violence.
Instead, in Rwanda, we tried narrowly circumscribed humanitarian action, providing security for refugee camps in eastern Congo. This did nothing but make the problems worse, as Hutu militias, living off and among the refugees, mounted fresh attacks against Rwanda. In Angola, four years of diplomacy by the United States and peacekeeping by the United Nations have not yet brought an end to civil war.
In the mid-1990's, faced with a recalcitrant, Islamist regime in the Sudan engaged in terrorist alliances and a 40-year civil war, we encouraged the country's neighbors -- Eritrea, Ethiopia and Uganda -- to coalesce behind rebel movements and offered them token support like radios and boots. But however popular the United States posture of alignment against Khartoum may be, it has hardly proved to be a winning strategy, and now two of our allies -- Ethiopia and Eritrea -- are distracted from the Sudan front by their own border dispute.
Our African policy has advocated political reconciliation and negotiated solutions. Our envoys have crisscrossed the region when crises erupted -- a conspicuous demonstration of American concern. But we haven't had a strategic concept beyond supporting good guys, and we haven't followed up with anything more tangible that would give credibility to our rhetoric. Indeed, we have increasingly encouraged Africans to take the lead, which signals our own reluctance to do so.
Backing "African solutions" is fine where they have the possibility of leading to durable and legitimate outcomes. But the turmoil in Central and East Africa should remind us that the much acclaimed "new generation" of leaders in Eritrea, Ethiopia, Uganda, Rwanda, South Africa and Namibia may have sharply differing agendas. They have little in common apart from having gained power through struggle and having an inclination -- recklessly winked at by Washington -- to ignore established frontiers.
Admittedly, Africa is a messy and confusing place in which to engage in conflict management. It is not axiomatic that American leadership is appropriate in all cases, nor that it would be popular in Africa or at home. But that is not the point.
The point is that "African policy lite" isn't working very well. There have been few successes since Namibia, South Africa and Mozambique turned to peace and democracy earlier this decade.
Goodwill visits and innovative trade initiatives are not enough to assure Africa's economic turnaround. Enhanced stability and vigorous conflict management are prerequisites to a regional renaissance.
It is time to consider how we can make sure that all parties -- the good, the not-so-good and the downright awful -- take us more seriously and cooperate in making peace.
This will require leading by example, using all our considerable diplomatic resources to create a coherent way of controlling conflict. It means using our power to influence our closest allies and our most venal of enemies. In the final analysis, we should be on our side, backing our own concepts and principles.

SECTION: Section A; Page 24; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 318 words

HEADLINE: The Worldwide Stock Plunge

 BODY:
Panics are never pretty, and the one now going on in financial markets around the world is no exception. In virtually every stock market, prices fell sharply yesterday.
In recent years, plunges in world markets have often been halted in the United States, where individual investors have stepped in to buy because they were confident that stock prices would rebound. It appears they have been less eager to do so this summer, although there is no indication that there has been a lot of selling by such investors. 
 While the immediate cause of the panic is the default and financial chaos in Russia, it also comes at a time of growing economic weakness around the world. Recessions in Asia appear to be getting worse, and while the United States' economy has continued to grow, the Government reported yesterday that after-tax corporate profits were down in the second quarter from the same period a year ago. That is the first such decline since 1989, just before the country's last recession.
Over the past year there has been a gradual increase in concern among investors, which has shown itself in a flight to quality. The most established economies, in the United States and Western Europe, have until recently seen the prices of their biggest stocks rise, while shares of smaller companies did less well and other stock markets suffered. Now that flight to quality has turned into a stampede, and all stock markets look suspect to investors, while only the safest bonds seem attractive.
Whatever investors do in coming days, the important question is what will happen to the world's economies. Preventing a worldwide downturn will require a revitalized International Monetary Fund, and may require the Federal Reserve to reduce short-term interest rates in this country, despite its worries about inflation. With commodity prices at a 21-year low, inflation is not the major concern now.

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE BANKS;
Republic Says Loss in Russia Will Erase Quarter's Profits

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY L. O'BRIEN 

 BODY:
Adding to anxiety about the risk banks are facing around the world, the Republic New York Corporation, considered one of the most conservatively managed banks in the United States, said yesterday that its losses in Russia would wipe out all of its third-quarter earnings.
Although Republic, which trades in some of the world's most exotic markets, was the only bank to disclose losses in Russia yesterday, it was hardly the only bank stock to suffer from the fears of shareholders. Investors, reacting to a confluence of real or expected economic downturns in several regions, have concluded that the financial risks facing banks in the near future are too worrisome. They fled bank stocks with a vengeance yesterday. 
 "It's like the entire world just got a margin call and everyone is stampeding out of emerging markets," said David Berry, a banking analyst with Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc. "Everybody wants their money back at the same time. The 90's are over."
Banks were not the only companies feeling the heat. Shares of leading brokerage firms like Merrill Lynch and Morgan Stanley Dean Witter sank yesterday, with losses in the shares of Lehman Brothers outpacing the group. Lehman plunged $7.25, or 12.6 percent, to $50.4375.
An individual close to Lehman's trading operation said the firm expected a "several million dollar" loss on a Latin American swap transaction involving the Russian ruble. This person said that emerging-markets turmoil might reduce third-quarter earnings by about 11 percent from the company's earlier projections. A Lehman spokesman declined to comment.
Republic said it planned to take a $110 million pretax charge in the quarter for losses related to a sharp drop in the value of short-term Russian treasury bills known as GKO's.
Republic, which earned about $119 million in the second quarter, also said it would take a charge of $45 million against reserves because of "current and anticipated Russian counterparty defaults." Republic declined to specify what that charge involved, but analysts said the language suggested losses on derivatives.
In a separate announcement, Safra Republic Holdings S.A., a private European banking unit that is 49 percent owned by Republic, said it would take a third-quarter charge of $65.9 million because of losses in Russia.
Republic is controlled by Edmond J. Safra, a legendary banker whose family has been involved in the industry for more than 150 years. It is also one of the trading houses favored by George Soros, one of the world's leading currency speculators. Mr. Soros's investment company announced yesterday that it had lost about $2 billion in Russia over the last year. Republic and Mr. Soros's company declined to comment on any trading Mr. Soros may have done through the bank.
In trading on the New York Stock Exchange, shares of Republic plunged $4.625, or 9.23 percent, to $45.50. Other bank stocks were also battered, continuing more than a monthlong slide born out of concern about economic problems in Asia, Latin America and Eastern Europe.
Shares of J. P. Morgan plunged $13.1875, or 11.2 percent, to $104.75; Chase Manhattan fell $6.125, or 9.5 percent, to $58.125; Bankers Trust fell $8.3125, or 9 percent, to $83.8125; Citicorp fell $11.875, or 8.9 percent, to $122; BankBoston dropped $2.375, or 5.74 percent, to $39, and BankAmerica fell $3.875, or 5 percent, to $73.75.
The losses in Russia are an unaccustomed, though not unheard of, blow for Republic, the nation's 21st-largest bank, with about $60 billion in assets. Mr. Safra, 66, chairman of Safra Republic, owns 28.5 percent of Republic New York.
Although Republic maintains a traditional branch network in New York, its more intriguing businesses are in private banking, precious metals trading and financial services it performs for securities firms and highly volatile hedge funds. Many hedge funds, like those of Mr. Soros, have been battered recently in emerging markets like Russia, and their losses may have been felt by Republic.
"It's one thing for J. P. Morgan or Bankers Trust to make an announcement like this because they're usually much more active in those markets," said Gerard Cassidy, a banking analyst with Tucker Anthony, a research and brokerage firm, in Portland, Me. "But Republic, and rightfully so, has always been perceived as far more cautious and conservative. For this to happen to them is a real shock."
But this is not the first time Republic's trading operation has suffered a setback. A Federal judge in New York recently forced the bank to pay more than $13.5 million to two wealthy Pakistani investors, who contended that the bank had mishandled trading accounts involving complex currency transactions.
Despite the disastrous effect losses in Russia are having on Republic's earnings, most of the largest American banks have ample cushions to absorb any losses there.
Citicorp and Chase, for example, each earned more than $1 billion in this year's second quarter and said they had $420 million and about $500 million, respectively, in exposure to Russia.
Analysts said J. P. Morgan, which earned $402 million in the second quarter, had exposure of less than $400 million in Russia, but it is also one of the world's biggest traders and underwriters of emerging-markets debt and could be more severely hurt by problems in other countries.
Bankers Trust, which declined to comment on its earnings outlook, may be one of the most vulnerable big banks in Russia. Analysts said the bank, which earned $164 million in the second quarter, had exposure in Russia of roughly $1 billion, including about $500 million to hedge funds.
Russia is only part of the mix when it comes to investor worries about economic developments overseas, and those worries are not likely to be put to rest until banks and other companies begin reporting third-quarter earnings in October.

HEADLINE: Eliminating Hidden Weapons;
Illusory Inspections in Iraq

 BODY:
The resignation of Scott Ritter, a top American inspector on the United Nations team charged with penetrating Iraq's deadly-weapons program, is the latest and most dramatic warning that the Clinton Administration and the U.N. are abandoning a firm approach toward President Saddam Hussein. Richard Butler, chairman of the U.N. inspection team, and Mohammed el-Baradei, head of the International Atomic Energy Agency, have previously warned that the inspectors could no longer guarantee they had Iraq's weapons programs adequately policed. Now Mr. Ritter charges that the inspections designed to ferret out hidden weapons have become an illusion.
The United States has blocked aggressive inspections while it tries to build a consensus for firm action in the U.N. Security Council. But prolonged delay is dangerous, giving Iraq precious time to build biological or chemical weapons without being detected. Aggressive inspections must be resumed promptly, and if additional military forces are needed in the gulf region to lend credible support to the inspectors, they need to be provided. 
 From 1991 to last year, Washington fought off other nations' efforts to weaken the inspections. On several occasions when Iraq balked, America's threats brought it into line again. Now Mr. Ritter charges that American officials sought to stop him and others from carrying out sensitive missions and that Saddam Hussein has found a "witting partner" in the U.N. Security Council.
The Administration argues that a drop in political support for military intervention, both at home and abroad, has forced it to explore diplomatic options and seek support on the Security Council before considering the use of military threats and force. Meanwhile, Secretary of State Madeleine Albright has argued that Mr. Hussein is still losing the struggle because the economic sanctions on Iraq will remain.
But sanctions are not an end in themselves, merely a means to compel Mr. Hussein to cooperate. His willingness to live with sanctions is a measure of the importance he places on acquiring weapons of mass destruction. Sanctions and diplomacy cannot work unless backed by the threat of force -- if necessary, by the United States alone.
The Administration must not let its diplomacy drift into dangerous delay. Even a few more weeks free of inspections might allow Mr. Hussein to revive construction of a biological, chemical or nuclear weapon. As Washington contemplates an extended war against terrorism, it cannot give in to a man who specializes in the unthinkable.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS;
With Its Dollar Plummeting, Canada Raises Its Key Rate

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: TORONTO, Aug. 27

 BODY:
After weeks of hesitation, Canada's central bank raised its benchmark interest rate by a full percentage point today in an effort to halt the slide of the Canadian dollar, which has lost more than 10 percent of its value against the United States dollar this year.
Despite the increase in the overnight lending rate to 6 percent, the Canadian dollar swooned again, to 63.34 United States cents in New York, from 63.79 cents on Wednesday, leading many analysts to question the effectiveness of the central bank's actions and to worry about its effect on the national economy. 
 "I don't think it will help at all," said Jeffrey Cheah, a financial market analyst with Standard & Poor's MMS in Toronto. "Consumer confidence is already at very low levels because the currency is depreciating almost every day. This will just compound the lack of consumer confidence in Canada."
The stock market did not help matters either, with the Toronto Stock Exchange following the sharp decline of other markets. The exchange's index of 300 stocks fell 372.84 points, or 6.04 percent, to 5,799.46.
John McCallum, chief economist at the Royal Bank of Canada, said the central bank had wanted to ride out current conditions, thinking higher interest rates were unnecessary because the Canadian economy was slowing anyway.
The Canadian dollar -- called a loonie because the dollar coin bears the image of a common loon -- has been battered by contraction in the commodities market and problems in Asia and Russia. On Wednesday, it suffered its biggest one-day loss in three years, falling 0.73 cent, to 63.79 cents, an all-time low.
"That's when the Bank of Canada said: 'Enough is enough. We go,' " Mr. McCallum said.
Canada's largest banks quickly followed suit and raised prime lending rates one percentage point to match the central bank's move.
In a statement released today, the Bank of Canada indicated that the continuing slide in the value of the dollar represented a diminishing confidence in Canadian investments.
"Today's increase in interest rates is aimed at providing support for the Canadian dollar in order to bolster confidence, while preserving monetary conditions that will help to sustain the present noninflationary expansion of the economy," the bank's statement said.
The rate increase was the first in more than eight months.
Like many other analysts, Mr. McCallum sees raising interest rates as a fairly useless move in the face of so many other problems around the world. "We're in the eye of a storm, with Russia crumbling and Asia suffering through difficult times," Mr. McCallum said. "These are forces beyond our control. If we try to resist with interest rate hikes, what we will get are negative effects on our economy."
Until now, the Canadian economy has managed to retain much of its strength despite the currency troubles. With practically no inflation and a federal budget surplus, analysts had expected growth to be relatively brisk this year, until today's announcement.
"We will be revising our G.D.P. estimates downward," Mr. Cheah said. "The interest rate hike is not going to have an immediate impact but will be most strongly felt three to six months from now."
The economic effect of the higher interest rates will be offset somewhat by the advantages of a cheaper dollar, which helps exports and tourism, Mr. McCallum said.
Many analysts expect the loonie to continue losing value, but how far it will slide is anybody's guess.

HEADLINE: Metro Matters;
Big State, Big Dreams, Big Talk

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT 

 BODY:
I love NY as much as the next guy. But still I don't quite get it.
With Gov. George E. Pataki penning his memoirs and fund-raising around the country and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani wowing Iowans, what was for months a matter of speculation -- and in some circles incredulity -- now seems all but irrefutable. New York has not one but two Republican leaders with dreams of the White House dancing in their heads. 
 As the goal of their itineraries has in recent weeks become more explicit, so, too, has their message.
"Our approach works for this city, this state," Mr. Pataki said recently, addressing a group of national Republican leaders gathered in Manhattan, "and will work for the people of America as well."
John H. Gross, the Mayor's longtime campaign treasurer, meanwhile, has described Mr. Giuliani's travels as an opportunity to spread the Good News across the land. "The purpose of the trips is to tell people the story of the successes in New York City and to help influence the Republican Party to accept his views on economic and social issues."
That both Mr. Pataki and Mr. Giuliani are so busy taking credit for the same list of accomplishments -- reviving the economy, lowering taxes, cutting crime and reducing the welfare rolls -- is, of course, a little awkward. One can only imagine how an audience in Des Moines or Concord, N.H., will sort out their competing claims. But in some ways, that's the least of it.
If voters in most parts of the country were presented with some of New York's vital statistics, they would probably have a hard time figuring out what all the fuss is about.
CONSIDER the following figures. According to the most recent Federal surveys, New York now ranks 39th among states in job growth. It is 25th in personal income growth. By contrast, its outstanding publicly financed debt -- $35 billion and rising fast -- is far and away the largest of any state's and translates, on a per capita basis, into the country's fourth highest debt burden.
In terms of taxes, New York's record is even less boastworthy. Though the rates have dropped recently, New Yorkers are, experts say, still paying a higher proportion of their income in combined state and local taxes than anyone else in the country.
Thus, while the New York economy has improved, that improvement hardly seems much to crow about. As Hal Hovey, the editor of State Budget and Tax News, put it: "It doesn't take a rocket scientist to be associated with a national economic recovery. By all these measures, New York is doing worse than other states." All in all, Mr. Hovey, who has spent a lifetime compiling such comparative statistics, ranks New York 35th in a cumulative measurement he calls "economic momentum."
New York's drop in crime stands up a lot better in state-by-state comparisons. Still, it, too, reflects a national trend that appears to be part demographics, part tougher policing and part mystery.
At the same time, many of New York's other intractable problems continue to seem just that. The succession of New York City schools chancellors has, for example, in no way altered the fundamental fact that few who can afford not to -- including the Mayor himself -- send their children to public schools in the city.
Just this week, the City Comptroller, Alan G. Hevesi, estimated that it would cost the city $90 billion over the next decade to repair its neglected infrastructure, a figure almost twice what it is actually planning to spend. And even as the welfare rolls in New York have been substantially reduced, the income gap in the state remains one of the highest in the nation, a disparity that is obvious to anyone who has walked 20 blocks in Manhattan.
So what motivates all the grand talk about what New York has to offer the rest of the nation?
The most generous account would be that Mr. Giuliani and Mr. Pataki have simply been carried away by their own enthusiasm. Many good things have occurred in New York during their tenure, and in overestimating the global significance of their accomplishments, they are, well, typical New Yorkers.
Less charitably, there is room to wonder whether the exaggeration is calculated, a deliberate misrepresentation of the state of the state driven by personal ambition. Such things, after all, do happen.
Remember the "Massachusetts Miracle," anyone?

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE EMERGING ECONOMIES;
Already-Shaky Markets in Latin America Drop Even Further

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

 BODY:
Stock markets in Latin America plunged yesterday, raising more doubts about whether the region will be able to survive the economic crisis that has swamped Asia and is now pulling Russia to its knees. Currency declines in Mexico and the fear of a devaluation in Brazil are also adding pressure.
The answer is not only important to the Governments and citizens of Brazil, Mexico, and Argentina, who have fought back with economic reforms since the drubbing they took in the wake of the Mexican peso devaluation in 1994-95. 
 Latin America is also a region whose consumers are big buyers of American exports and if their pace slows down that will put another drag on the American economy.
Mexico alone is one of America's top three export markets and the peso's plunge to record lows is surely going to curb buying, as the weaker peso raises the cost of American goods. The peso has fallen 10 percent in value since the end of July.
"If emerging markets get weaker, that takes away a leg from under U.S. corporate earnings," said Christopher D. Alderson, head of emerging markets at Rowe Price Fleming in London.
In Brazil, the Bovespa index dropped 10 percent and is now down 39.5 percent since the end of July. In Mexico, the chief market index fell 6.1 percent and is off 34.6 percent since July 17, the day that the Dow Jones industrial average hit its recent record high. Argentina's Merval index fell 10.6 percent and the Venezuelan stock market index dropped 9.2 percent.
A lot of the equity selling in Latin America, analysts said yesterday, is being forced by the need of hedge funds to raise cash to pay off loans they took out to make big bets in Russia.
As fast as stock markets are falling, Latin American bond markets are keeping up, which is forcing interest rates higher. According to the J. P. Morgan emerging market bond index, the difference, or spread, between the rates on United States Treasuries and those on Latin American government bonds is 13 percentage points, putting rates on long-term Latin American bonds at more than 18 percent.
This surge in rates since the beginning of August will add to a country's borrowing costs and, more importantly, will make it harder for Latin American countries to roll over their current foreign debt as it comes due in the months ahead.
A lot of the focus in the near term will be on Brazil. Mr. Alderson said that Brazil has around $50 billion in short-term debt that has to be refinanced over the next several months. And as the time for rollovers approaches, international investors are likely to get more jittery.
Brazil is also critical to the outlook for all emerging markets because it is seen as the country that is the most vulnerable to a currency devaluation. The devaluation in Russia, which economically is less important than Brazil, has sent a shock wave through emerging markets. A Brazilian devaluation would probably do much more damange.
The rout in Latin America yesterday was mirrored in other emerging markets. In Eastern Europe, the stock market in Hungary plunged 14.4 percent, the market in Poland dropped 6.1 percent and the main index in the Czech Republic fell 6.8 percent. The RTS index in Russia plunged 17.1 percent and is off 84 percent for the year.
In Asia, the key emerging markets were off much less, falling in the 1 percent range. But that may have been because they were closed before the full brunt of the negative news from Russia yesterday.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Chaos reigned among traders on the floor of the stock market in Sao Paulo, Brazil, yesterday. The Bovespa index dropped 10 percent and is now down 39.5 percent since the end of July. (Associated Press)

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: MARKET PLACE;
NoRefuge in Plunging Commodity Prices

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

 BODY:
While global stock markets plunged, a major index of commodity prices, on products from oil to cotton, fell to its lowest level in 21 years yesterday. And that may be more worrisome for investors in the long run than yesterday's 4.19 percent decline in the Dow Jones industrial average.
Each tick down in the price of oil, copper, cotton and gold puts more economic pressure on the nations where they are produced. And many of the major producers are the emerging-market countries experiencing deep financial crisis, like Russia, which is a leading exporter of oil and precious metals, or countries considered vulnerable, like Mexico, which is also a big oil producer. 
 Already, commodity prices have fallen 20 percent in the last year and heightened concern about the economies and markets of developing nations that began with Asian currency selloffs in the second half of 1997. "It's a flu, it's catching; it's dominoes," said William Byers, director of futures research at Bear, Stearns & Company. "Not only is the psychology spreading, but the economic weakness is spreading."
Yesterday's drop in commodities came as markets around the world tumbled and Russia's economic crisis deepened. Millions of investors, seeking to flee the turmoil, rushed to the relative safety of United States Treasury securities, pushing long-term yields to their lowest level in 30 years or more.
For the American investor worried about the strength of the economy, which by all current signs is still strong, and corporate earnings, which have been weakening, so far the fall in commodity prices and the drop in interest rates have been good news.
The lower prices for commodities, while tough on emerging-market and other producing nations, have lowered corporate costs here and spurred consumer spending. And the damage that had been done to commodity producers in the United States has not had a big impact on the economy.
The fall in interest rates has also been positive for the economy, by bolstering housing and capital investment. Thirty-year mortgage rates have now been below 7 percent for more than two months. Yesterday, the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond fell to 5.34 percent, the lowest for long-term yields in three decades. The 10-year note's yield, a benchmark for home mortgage rates, fell to 5.06 percent, its lowest since 1967.
These forces have, so far, have more than offset the drag from the Asian economic crisis, which has led to a sharp drop in demand for American exports. And this is one reason the economy has slowed much less than many economists expected in the last six months.
But with the global economic and market turmoil spreading from Asia to Russia to Latin America, it appears less likely, some analysts said, that the positives of lower interest rates and low commodity prices would be sufficient to offset the drag from a global economic slowdown on American corporate profits.
Already, many economic forecasters who had bet on the offsetting effect from lower commodity prices and lower interest rates have been scaling back their economic forecasts for the second half of the year. And a steep fall in United States stock prices, which would dampen consumer spending, might force a further scaling back, which would not be good for corporate earnings.
"Unless you correct the problems" in Asia and Russia, said Richard Berner, chief economist of Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh, "the stimulus will be a minor offset."
The backwash from the Russian turmoil pushed the Commodity Research Bureau/Bridge Index of 17 commodities down 1.88 points yesterday, to 196.24, the lowest since December 1977, a year of sharp declines in agricultural prices that led to farmer protests.
The impact from the commodity price decline has gone beyond the emerging economies. In Canada, a major commodity producer, the currency dropped to another record low, forcing the central bank to raise its benchmark interest rate a full percentage point, to 6 percent. The increase also helped send the Toronto Stock Exchange index down more than 6 percent.
Two forces have been pushing commodity prices lower and will continue to do so, several analysts said yesterday. One is the falling demand from Asian nations, which, for example, were major consumers of copper for new construction. The other is that countries in deep trouble, like Russia, are expected to sell what they can to raise money, adding to the supply in an already weak market.
William O'Neill, director of futures research at Merrill Lynch, said there had been big sales of platinum and palladium by Russia in Japan and sales of gold and silver in Zurich and London. The crisis, he said, "inspires them to sell as much as possible." The Government controls much of the country's precious metals.
The selling by Russia had an odd twist. Gold had been thought of for years as refuge in the time of political and economic crises, and a few gold bugs still hold that belief. But yesterday, the price of gold plunged, dropping to a 19-year low on the spot market in London at $276.55 an ounce, down $5.70.
Commodity selling is also coming from countries like Australia whose currency also fell to a record low yesterday. But because most commodities are priced in dollars, the weaker Australian dollar means that gold and other commodity sellers can reap nice profits when the sales are translated back into Australian dollars.
This all means that commodity prices -- and the pain that they are bringing to already troubled economies -- still have a way to go before they hit bottom. "I don't think we are there yet," Mr. Berner of Mellon Bank said.

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: NEWS ANALYSIS;
Washington Stands By, Keeping Its Head Down

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
When Russia was the heartland of what Ronald Reagan called the Evil Empire, bent on spreading Communist dogma and power around the globe, the United States developed economic, military and diplomatic weapons to contain and eventually to help eliminate the danger.
Now Russia once again constitutes a threat to the rest of the world. Its inability to manage its economy has destabilized stock and currency markets from Moscow to Wall Street. 
 And the Russian Government itself is tottering, raising the possibility that Russia might turn away from its ill-starred experiment with democracy and return to its centuries-old tradition of authoritarian leadership. This, of course, is a nation that still maintains an enormous nuclear arsenal.
But there was no emergency mobilization in Washington, no metaphorical red light flashing at the White House, no public sense that the United States must take urgent steps to protect the interests of the industrialized nations.
Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright made only the briefest of comments, in an informal setting. Samuel R. Berger, the national security adviser, held a news conference in which he answered a number of questions about Russia, but the announced topic of the session was anti-American terrorism in Africa.
Neither President Clinton, who was making a speech in Worcester, Mass., about crime, nor Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, who was fishing in the West, dropped everything to rush back to Washington. Indeed, Mr. Clinton seemed a bit at sea when reporters called questions to him about reports that President Boris N. Yeltsin might resign soon.
That is not the way it was when the cold war heated up.
Vice President Al Gore was also out of town, as were most members of Congress. It is true that the Russian meltdown had the impertinence to happen in August, when Washington, Paris, London and Bonn become ghost towns, with politicians and bureaucrats taking to the hills or sunning by the seashore.
One larger truth here is that in the current Russian crisis, as in many other post-cold-war crises, there is not all that much the United States can do.
A second larger truth, also important, is the Monica Lewinsky matter.
For the record, the Administration has steadfastly denied, like all Administrations rocked by scandal, that the Lewinsky affair has in any way affected the workings of government. But individual government officials, high and low, tell a different story. For a month now, they -- and indeed much of Washington -- has been curiously detached from the ebb and flow of events worldwide.
"You put your head down, stay in your corner and hope none of this muck rubs off on you," a State Department official said last week. "It is not an atmosphere conducive to good staff morale or to creative policy making."
Priorities change in subtle ways. Little attention is paid, except by those directly involved in this fall's elections. With very few exceptions, such as medical care, domestic policy issues have fallen by the wayside.
Mr. Clinton's mind is focused, his aides have implied, not so much on affairs of state as on the urgent necessity to repair relationships with his wife and daughter and to rebuild his own political standing. Otherwise it is hard to believe that he would not have felt compelled to return to Washington from his vacation on Martha's Vineyard to show that he was engaged in the Russian problem, however limited his options.
Traditionally, Presidents are loath to be seen relaxing, however unreasonable this may be, when great events are under way in the world.
As it happens, Mr. Yeltsin has been as badly weakened as Mr. Clinton has by recent events. Which raises the question: In that situation, is there any real point to carrying on with the summit conference scheduled for next week? A number of Russian scholars said they doubted anything could be accomplished, but Spurgeon Keeny, president of the Arms Control Association, suggested that the meeting could provide "a critical opportunity for these two Presidents, who are hanging on the ropes, a unique opportunity to demonstrate to their countries and to the world that they retain the power of presidential leadership."
If so, that would be most welcome to Mr. Clinton, and not only because it would take people's minds off his sexual misadventures.
President Clinton and his aides have had to cope in recent weeks with a whole spectrum of frustrating issues where the United States, though by no means bereft of clout, lacks easy answers. Huff and puff as it might, Washington is no closer today than it was a year ago to ridding Iraq of weapons of mass destruction. For all the allied promises to end ethnic cleansing, it continues in Kosovo. And anti-terrorism measures without number did not prevent the sickening explosion outside the American Embassies in Kenya and Tanzania this month.
Circumstances differ from case to case, of course. In some situations, building or maintaining alliances has proved difficult. In others, the United States has tried to work through international organizations whose attempted solutions failed. In still others, existing problems have been compounded by errors or ineptitude in American policy. But whatever the specifics, the new reality -- economic crises in Asia and Russia, nasty local wars, often fueled by generations of hatred, and strikes by terrorists -- have proved a lot more difficult to handle than seemed likely in the euphoria at the cold war's end.
It is, of course, a richly paradoxical situation: helped by its allies, the United States wins the cold war, develops an economy of unprecedented productivity, emerges as the world's only superpower yet finds itself less powerful than before, not more so -- if one measures power in terms of capacity to influence the course of events abroad.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE OVERVIEW;
MARKETS JOLTED, DOW OFF 4.1% AS THE RUSSIAN ECONOMIC SLIDE ADDS TO PRESSURES ON YELTSIN

BYLINE: By GRETCHEN MORGENSON 

 BODY:
Stock market investors around the world, frightened by the depth of Russia's economic turmoil and its possibly contagious effect on Latin America, rushed for the exits yesterday in search of safer places to put their money.
In the worst trading day this year, the Dow Jones industrial average fell 4.19 percent, or 357.36 points, to close at 8,165.99. The Nasdaq fell 81.72 points to close down 4.62 percent. 
 With the Dow average now 12.5 percent below its July 17 high of 9,337.97, investors are well into what Wall Street calls a correction. Bear market territory -- defined as a stock price decline of 15 percent to 20 percent -- is at hand.
Investors struggled to absorb the full impact of Russia's economic woes -- its central bank has stopped supporting the ruble and has suspended payments on some debt. Institutions that had bought the country's debt saw the value of their holdings fall to around 10 percent of its worth just last week.
But the fear that the economic unraveling in Russia could spread to the emerging-market countries of Latin America acted as the greatest depressant on stocks worldwide. Indeed, investors everywhere wondered which economy would be the next to crumple.
Major markets fell, with the exception of Hong Kong. In Europe, key indexes fell 3 percent to 6 percent, with the DAX in Germany dropping 4.5 percent. Latin America's markets were punished even more, with Mexico's main index falling 6 percent and Brazil's nearly 10 percent.
Disappointment over Japan's inability to repair its broken-down economy drove the Nikkei 225 index down 3.04 percent long before trading began in the United States. Policy paralysis seems to have overtaken Japanese politicians, who appear unwilling to take steps to solve their country's well-documented economic and banking problems. The Nikkei has fallen 16.5 percent this year from its March high.
When the stock market opened in the United States, traders and money managers reported a flood of early selling, selling that continued in waves all day. Even though stock prices revived briefly several times, few, if any, buyers were willing to hunt for bargains. The market closed at its low, a sign of overwhelming market weakness and a sign of caution for the day. In percentage terms, the drop in the Dow was the largest since last October and the 15th-largest one-day decline since 1945.
Stocks of big international banks, which were down an average of 6.6 percent, were among the hardest hit on fears of Russia-related losses. But rampant selling ravaged even the most-favored stocks, such as Coca-Cola, General Electric and Microsoft, as well as the Internet darlings Excite Inc. and Amazon.com.
The suffering of the healthiest companies can be attributed in part to selling by investors who had to raise cash because of losses on their emerging-market investments. An investor who faces losses after borrowing heavily to make foreign investments that are plunging typically sells whatever he can. Often that means the biggest, most liquid investment. Or the investment that still shows a profit.
It is a vicious circle, said William Dudley, an economist at Goldman, Sachs in New York. "People are becoming increasingly risk averse, leading to lower equity prices, increasing pressure on emerging market economies. Absent right now is any mechanism to break that dynamic up." As a result, Mr. Dudley suggests that investors brace for more market declines.
The bad news started early. At 9:46 A.M., shares in the Republic New York Corporation, the parent of Republic National Bank of New York, were halted from trading. Minutes later, the bank announced it would take a $110 million charge in the third quarter for losses in Russian government bonds. The loss would virtually eliminate earnings for the period, the bank said.
Suddenly, it became clear that the ultimate impact of the Russian disaster was still unknown. If Republic Bank, known for its strong balance sheet and conservative lending practices, was hurt by Russia, what would happen to more aggressive lenders?
With that announcement, Republic Bank became the first domestic bank to enumerate its Russian losses. Market historians noted that Republic was also the first domestic bank, back in the mid-1980's, to reveal the extent of its exposure to lesser-developed country debt that later hammered Citicorp and many other American banks.
Investors in financial stocks yesterday found themselves in a nightmarish version of the child's game of hot potato. Exposure to Russia's ruble devaluation and debt is only starting to be defined. And since many of the losses will be taken by investors who bought or sold derivative securities, where the parties who took the other side of a given trade are not immediately identifiable, it could be weeks before it is known who is on the hook for what may be $100 billion in losses.
Some investors wondered why a country as economically inconsequential as Russia should play such a big role in world market turmoil. While Russia wields nuclear firepower, it accounts for just 1 percent of the world's trade, roughly equivalent to that of Denmark.
Analysts noted, though, that what happened in Russia could very well replay in the emerging markets of Latin America. When investors see their portfolios lose 90 percent of their value, as those holding Russian debt have, two things happen.
First, other emerging markets are likely to be dragged down as well, as investors revalue assets to what they see as the new economic reality. Then, as those assets are repriced, investors and lenders withdraw from the markets, cutting off companies' access to capital and making financing more expensive for governments.
Russia's default also made it painfully obvious that the International Monetary Fund is no longer ready or able to bail out countries in economic despair. Bailouts in Korea, Malaysia and Thailand had lulled investors into believing that the I.M.F. was effectively a guarantor of their investments.
So what investors are facing now is the very thing Wall Street fears the most: the unknown. The devil it knows, an imploding Russia in this case, is bad enough. The devil it doesn't know, what other economic disasters lie ahead, say, in Latin America, is considerably worse.
Indeed, all eyes are on Latin America. That's because a crisis in these nations would strike a severe blow to both American investors and corporations. Some 25 percent of Brazil's $300 billion in domestic debt, for example, is held by foreign investors. More important, around 20 percent of United States exports go to Latin America.
Even in the United States, the stock market carnage is worse than it first appears. Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette reported yesterday that the average stock was down 38 percent from its high.
One money manager said that the selling was overdone and the result of investors in the past buying stocks only because they are going up. "People have been investing by the direction of earnings and not looking at value," said David Eidelman, at Eidelman, Finger, Harris & Company in St. Louis. "If earnings are going higher, they buy. But when they start to see earnings come down, they look at the direction not the value. To paraphrase Oscar Wilde, these investors, so-called momentum players, know the price of everything and the value of nothing."
Contributing to the slide, brokerage firms asked investors who had borrowed to buy stocks to prop up their depleted portfolios with more cash, known as margin calls.
More than one professional trader said that yesterday's action in United States stocks was reminiscent of the beginning of the end of the great bull market of the late 1960's and early 1970's. These traders recalled that the 1973 bear market, which removed 45 percent from the Dow Jones industrial average, did not begin in earnest until stocks like Polaroid, Avon and I.B.M. crumbled.
This time, about 15 stocks -- including Dell Computer, Lucent Technologies and Cisco Systems -- had held up while the rest of the United States market had fallen. Yesterday, even these shares fell hard.

INTERNATIONAL A3-13
 
Pressure on Yeltsin to Quit Amid Economic Turmoil
Pressure mounted on President Boris N. Yeltsin to cede some of his powers or step aside as Russia's political leaders frantically negotiated over fundamental changes in the country's economic policies. A1
 
The Acting Prime Minister, Viktor Chernomyrdin, is trying to negotiate a sharing of power between the remains of the Communist Party and the tycoons who arose from the ashes of socialism. A1 

Ten of the country's 15 biggest banks have either combined, folded or been earmarked for Government takeover in the last two days. A12
 
The I.M.F. has apparently decided to resurrect key provisions of Russia's existing agreement rather than provide a quick infusion of money to support the ruble. A12
 
Bomb Suspect Brought to U.S.
Federal authorities brought a suspect in the embassy bombings in Africa to the United States, saying he had admitted that he was trained at terrorist camps in Afghanistan by Osama bin Laden. A1
 
New York City officials announced immediate plans to increase security in a section of lower Manhattan dominated by court and municipal buildings after learning that two suspects in the bombing would be detained and prosecuted in the city. A6
 
Police officers investigating the bombing of a Planet Hollywood restaurant in South Africa stopped an Air Egypt plane as it was to take off for Cairo and detained three people, a police spokesman said. A6
 
Bomb Raises Tensions in Israel
A pipe bomb exploded in a trash can on a busy Tel Aviv street, causing one serious injury and adding to a new wave of tension in Israel and the West Bank. The unrest has followed signs that Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu may be ready to make a deal with the Palestinians that would let peace negotiations resume. A8
 
Fighting in Congo Eases
Fighting just outside Congo's capital diminished, yet the Government severely tightened security, and evidence emerged that civilians and soldiers had killed several suspected rebel infiltrators. A3
 
Qaddafi Seeks Guarantees
Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi said Libya would have to negotiate certain guarantees before he would be inclined to turn over for trial two suspects in the 1988 bombing of a Pan Am jet. A3

Asylum for Colombia Politicians

NATIONAL A14-22
 
Bureaucratic Tangle Is Faulted in Air Crash
A crash of a commuter plane near Detroit last year, which took 29 lives, probably could have been prevented if the F.A.A. had put into effect the lessons learned from a similar crash two year earlier, Federal safety officials said. The officials also said the F.A.A. did not have enough information to have certified turboprop planes like the one that crashed for flying into ice. A1
 
Clinton Calls on Worcester
President Clinton, who is vacationing on Martha's Vineyard with his family, received a vote of confidence for his public policies but a mixed review of his personal conduct during a visit he made to Worcester, Mass. It was his first foray into a public arena since he admitted on Aug. 17 that he had had an improper relationship with Monica S. Lewinsky. A14
 
Miami Official Pleads Guilty
A former Miami City Commissioner, Humberto E. Hernandez, pleaded guilty to a Federal charge of conspiracy to commit bank, wire and mail fraud less than two weeks after a Florida judge sentenced him to a year in jail for a misdemeanor. Federal prosecutors, who had indicted Mr. Hernandez on 28 charges, said they would recommend that Mr. Hernandez serve four years in prison for the conspiracy conviction. A18
 
Hurricane Pulled Its Punch
Hurricane Bonnie seemed to treat the Carolinas better than feared, though state officials said it was too early to make a dollar assessment of the damage, and President Clinton declared part of North Carolina a disaster area. There were no reports of deaths or serious injuries. A20
 
Suit Links Tumors to Chemicals
Four former employees of an Amoco research center near Chicago and surviving relatives of three others filed suit against the company, saying it had "exhibited reckless disregard for the health and well-being" of the employees. Over the years, 19 current or former employees developed brain tumors, and four have died. The suit says Amoco had exposed the employees to "defective, unsafe and/or unreasonably dangerous chemicals" and failed to take adequate protective measures. A22
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-10
 
City Council Approves Campaign Fund Overhaul
The City Council voted to tighten the city's campaign finance rules, to reduce the role of money in elections and to free candidates from some fund-raising chores. The measures would reduce limits on contributions and enlarge the size of campaign subsidies that candidates could receive from the city treasury. The measures are intended to diminish the influence of big givers and lessen pressure on candidates to raise money. Mayor Giuliani criticized some of the provisions, but did not say if he would veto the measures. A1
 
March Leader Derides Giuliani
The chief organizer of the Million Youth March, Khalid Abdul Muhammad, derided Mayor Giuliani as "an ordinary cracker" who "chose to ignore the law" in his efforts to stop the march from taking place in Harlem on Sept. 5. The city, which has cited concerns for safety, asked a Federal appeals court to overturn a lower-court decision on Wednesday that said the march could take place as planned. A panel of three judges will hear arguments in the case on Tuesday, officials said. B1
 
Lack of Confidence in Schools
New York business leaders have little confidence in the city's public schools, with many saying that high school graduates lack the skills needed to succeed in the workplace, a survey of 450 employers found. B3

HEADLINE: Second-Quarter Economic Growth Faster Than Estimated

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The economy grew at a faster pace in the second quarter than previously estimated, even though it was weighed down by the strikes at the General Motors Corporation and slower exports to Asia.
Gross domestic product rose at a revised 1.6 percent annual rate in the second quarter, the Commerce Department said today. That was higher than the initial estimate of 1.4 percent released on July 31, although it was still well below the first quarter's stellar 5.5 percent growth rate. 
 Growth in the final two quarters of the year will probably average no better than 2.5 percent at an annual rate, analysts said. "We have two forces clashing" -- strong consumer demand and the slowdown from the Asian turmoil, said James Chessen, chief economist at the American Bankers Association in Washington. "One is going to have to dominate over the other, and I think it's going to be Asia."
The estimate for second-quarter economic growth was revised because more complete figures showed higher consumer spending on services and a narrower June trade deficit, which offset slower growth in business inventories, analysts said. The report also showed that corporate earnings rose and inflation remained under control.
Gross domestic product measures the total value of goods and service produced in the country.
Even after the revision, second-quarter growth in gross domestic product was the weakest since a rise of four-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter of 1995.
Exports fell 7.4 percent at an annual rate in the second quarter, the second consecutive quarterly drop. In the first quarter, exports declined 2.8 percent. Imports rose 10 percent after increasing 15.7 percent in the first quarter.
Taken together, the drops in exports and imports subtracted 2.1 percentage points from second-quarter economic growth.
Separately today, the Labor Department said the number of workers filing for jobless claims unexpectedly fell by 6,000 last week, to 297,000. That surprised analysts, who were forecasting a rise of 1,000, and suggested that employment growth had yet to slow.
What happens next to the economy will depend in large part on the spending patterns of consumers. An abundance of jobs, low interest rates, low inflation and falling import prices have so far kept consumers in a buying mood. But a slump in stock prices since the middle of July, along with an intensifying of economic problems overseas, contributed to a decline in consumer confidence this month.
"We're still seeing evidence of strong consumer spending, a booming housing market, and we're going to get a rebound from the G.M. strike," said Scott Brown, an economist at Raymond James & Associates in St. Petersburg, Fla. Offsetting those positives will be weakness in exports and an increase in imports, he said.
Inflation was subdued in the second quarter, today's G.D.P. report showed. The price deflator, a measure of price increases, grew at a revised eight-tenths of 1 percent, at an annual rate, in the quarter, down from an initial estimate of nine-tenths of 1 percent.
The economic slowdown from the first quarter took a toll on corporate profits. Second-quarter after-tax earnings, reported by the Commerce Department for the first time today, increased three-tenths of 1 percent.

HEADLINE: Youth March Organizer, Celebrating Ruling, Taunts Giuliani

BYLINE: By ABBY GOODNOUGH 

 BODY:
One day after a judge ruled that the Million Youth March could proceed next weekend in Harlem, the chief organizer attacked Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani for trying to block the event, calling him "an ordinary cracker" who "chose to ignore the law."
In a speech before reporters and about 20 supporters of the march, the organizer, Khalid Abdul Muhammad, said he wanted young black people who attend to be courteous and respectful. He said that above all, the march would be a show of "love and unity among black people." 
 But Mr. Muhammad took every opportunity during his 30-minute speech to bait Mr. Giuliani, who has condemned him in recent weeks for making numerous anti-Semitic and anti-white remarks.
"There's no need to be afraid of Rudy Giuliani any longer," Mr. Muhammad said, speaking at Sylvia's restaurant in Harlem. "This cracker knows the law, but he chose to ignore the law."
Mr. Giuliani was uncharacteristically silent after the speech, refusing to discuss it during an afternoon news conference at City Hall. But the city pressed on yesterday in its battle to stop the march, asking the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit to overturn the decision that Judge Lewis A. Kaplan of Federal District Court made on Wednesday. A panel of three judges will hear arguments in the case on Tuesday, said Florence Hutner, an assistant corporation counsel for the city.
Earlier this year, the city rejected Mr. Muhammad's request for a permit to hold the march in Harlem on Saturday, Sept. 5, saying the neighborhood could not safely accommodate the thousands of people expected to attend. The conflict between the city and the organizers has since become increasingly bitter, with each side accusing the other of racism and the organizers finally suing the city last week.
Lawyers for the march organizers argued in court that the city's refusal to grant them a permit was unconstitutional, and Judge Kaplan agreed. In his ruling, he said the city's rules for weighing permits for street events were too broad and its decisions too arbitrary. He said that the city had considered Mr. Muhammad's application "on an ad-hoc basis" and that the city was not "free to impose a ban like this without a persuasive showing" that the event threatened public safety.
Mr. Muhammad and other organizers have billed the march -- actually a stationary rally along Malcolm X Boulevard -- as an opportunity for black youth to unite and hear speeches about issues that affect them, like police brutality, racism, unemployment and drug abuse. They have invited young black people from around the country, but they now say attendance will be nowhere near the one million people originally expected.
In his speech yesterday, Mr. Muhammad praised Judge Kaplan for ruling that the city's actions had violated the Constitutional rights of the march organizers. But he went on to say that he did not believe in the United States Constitution, and that it was not "worth the paper it's printed on."
He also criticized black politicians who have expressed reservations about the march. Several black lawmakers from Harlem, including State Senator David A. Paterson and United States Representative Charles B. Rangel, have questioned why the organizers did not consult with Harlem residents before planning the event, and whether it will be safe.
"Stand up, be strong again," Mr. Muhammad said, addressing black elected officials who have criticized the march, none of whom attended the speech. "Stop flip-flopping on the side of the youth one day, then against them the next."
Senator Paterson issued a statement yesterday asking the march organizers to avoid making "bigoted" remarks about other racial and ethnic groups at the event and to focus instead on the problems facing black youth.
"I don't want it to be a contest to see who can make the most inflammatory statement in the most creative way," Mr. Paterson said.
But Mr. Muhammad shrugged off the request, saying after his speech that he could not make such a guarantee. "How are we going to prepare our youth," he said, "if we don't tell our youth who are our oppressors?"
In one of his attacks on Mr. Giuliani, Mr. Muhammad said the Mayor "never would've told Jewish youth in Crown Heights they couldn't march."
"He never would've said it to Jew mothers, or Jew youth," Mr. Muhammad said. "He never even would've tried it."
Although Mr. Giuliani would not respond to the speech, he did speak out against the march earlier in the day, at a news conference in the Bronx. "For the life of me, I can't figure out why people would voluntarily join a hate march," the Mayor said. "The person running this march has said the most hateful things that I have heard in this city in maybe a couple of decades."
At the same news conference, Police Commissioner Howard Safir said the march would severely strain police resources, since it falls on the busy Labor Day weekend. The West Indian Day Parade alone, scheduled to take place in Brooklyn on Labor Day, will require the supervision of at least 3,000 officers, he said. Mr. Safir said that the Police Department had started planning for the march, but that he did not yet know how many officers would be needed.
Because many officers are on vacation that weekend, it will be difficult to find enough to cover two major events, even though they are on different days, he said.
"If we're forced to deal with the march, we'll deal with it," Mr. Safir said. "But we're going to have to bring in all hands. We're going to have to bring in people who are off on overtime, and it's going to be a great cost to the city."
Mr. Muhammad issued a warning to the Mayor and the police force in his speech, saying violence could erupt if police officers mistreat anyone at the rally. "We could have one fool on the police force who could ignite the collapse of New York City," he said. "Mayor Giuliani, make sure you have no riots by police officers that day, because it could be catastrophic for the city."
But he also urged young people planning to attend the march to be "slow to anger, and not that quick to judge."
"If someone steps on your toe, apologize to him first," Mr. Muhammad said. "Keep smiles on our faces. Hug each other with tight embraces. Let's have a day of networking, organizing and galvanizing."
