HEADLINE: THE MARKETS;
Japan Stocks Fall 2%, but Rebound From a 12-Year Low

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Stock markets around Asia nose-dived in early trading today, as investors feared that the deep Russian economic crisis would hinder economic growth in the West and slow a recovery in this region.
The benchmark Nikkei index of 225 issues at one point plunged during morning trading to its lowest level since March 1986, down 621 points, or 4.3 percent, to 13,792.76. It then recouped much of those losses, to stand at 14,128.97 at the midday recess -- down 284.82 points, or nearly 2 percent, from Thursday. 
 Elsewhere, the key index in Singapore was off three-tenths of a percent, to 908.24; down 2.87 percent, to 305.55, in South Korea, and down 2.86 percent, to 2,487.40 in Australia.
"The most basic thing is not to panic," the Japanese Finance Minister, Kiichi Miyazawa, said at a regular news conference this morning. Prime Minister Keizo Obuchi said he hoped that Russia's problems would not have a major impact on the world economy.
The Japanese yen strengthened against the dollar, trading here at 140.6, as currency traders sold dollars in reaction to the sharp fall in New York stocks on Thursday.
This morning's early panic in Asian markets subsided a little in later trading. Funds linked to the government in Taiwan helped lift stocks there from lows, and institutional investors in Singapore began buying after an early selloff by retail investors.
In Hong Kong, monetary authorities again intervened to buy stocks. Still, at midmorning, the benchmark Hang Seng index was down 66.37 points, or eight-tenths of 1 percent, at 7,856.60.
Asian countries have been highly sensitive to troubles elsewhere because of the impact that outside economic shocks could have on fragile economic recoveries here. South Korea announced on Thursday that its economic growth for the second quarter ended on June 30 contracted by 6.6 percent on an annual basis.
Japan, too, is struggling to deal with an overwhelming banking crisis that has engulfed the nation, and Hong Kong is battling an economic contraction and a spike in interest rates.
"I'm sitting here watching the world falling to pieces," said Richard Jerram, an economist at ING Baring Securities Japan. Referring to governments in the region, he added, "The question is, what can they do?"
Investors in Asia are worried that exports, the bright prospect for the struggling economies here, may subside if markets in Europe and the United States suffer from a fallout from Russia. Western economies are sensitive to the Russian debt crisis through their commercial debt exposure and through investments in the capital markets, with Germany, Moscow's biggest trading partner, most directly exposed.
In addition, a weakening in Western stock markets could mean that Western buyers of goods would have less ability to absorb exports from Asia.
So in Tokyo, the shares of export manufacturers were hard hit, with even the favorite stocks like the Toyota Motor Company and the Sony Corporation tumbling. Some banking stocks had no buyers.

HEADLINE: President Goes Forth, To Mixed Reception

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: WORCESTER, Mass., Aug. 27

 BODY:
Wading into a public arena for the first time since he admitted 10 days ago to having had an affair with a White House intern, President Clinton today received a strong vote of confidence for his public policies but a mixed review of his personal conduct.
Mr. Clinton left his wife and daughter back at the First Family's vacation retreat on Martha's Vineyard and flew to this Democratic stronghold this morning to give a speech on school safety that his advisers hoped would help refocus the nation and the news media away from his sex life and onto policy matters. 
 He attracted a Presidential-campaign-size crowd, including hundreds of onlookers packed along a motorcade route of nearly six miles from the Worcester airport to the historic Mechanics Hall, where he addressed an audience of 1,200 people and never referred to his personal situation.
The speech was delivered before news of the plunging stock market and the precarious situation of the Russian President, Boris N. Yeltsin, although Mr. Clinton had no comment on either development after it had become clear that both were dominating the day's events. While meant to highlight school safety, the talk, which sounded more boilerplate than urgent, grazed over an assortment of policy items, including the economy, terrorism, Social Security, school construction and health care.
Mr. Clinton's sudden decision on Monday night to make Worcester the city of his post-confession debut sparked a burst of hometown pride. And those who heard his speech gave him sustained applause and standing ovations.
Representative Jim McGovern, the freshman Democrat who invited Mr. Clinton here months ago, declared from the stage at Mechanics Hall: "This is not a city of fair-weather friends. A friend from Worcester is a friend for life, and you, through your policies, Mr. President, have been a true friend of Worcester."
But behind the bunting and the resounding applause, many were careful to distinguish between the President's policies and his personal conduct. And signs along the motorcade route were mixed. While some said, "We support you," "We're proud of you, Mr. President" or "We're still with you, Mr. President," others ordered him to "Resign now!" or simply said, "Liar!" One placard offered a "Scarlet A."
Mary H. Whitney, the 63-year-old Democratic Mayor of nearby Fitchburg, who attended the speech after being called by the White House advance staff, said, "I have the highest respect for the office of President." But she added that the person in the office should be a role model for the nation and that Mr. Clinton "is not my kind of role model."
Another loyal Democrat refused to attend: Connie Lukes, a Worcester City Councilwoman, boycotted the event, condemning Mr. Clinton and saying, "The emperor has no clothes."
Senator John F. Kerry, part of the official delegation that greeted the President at the airport and traveled around with him, grew slightly testy when reporters pressed for his reaction to Mr. Clinton's personal situation.
"I don't have anything to say," the Senator replied, an uncharacteristic response from a politician who so enjoys the public eye that he is routinely referred to as John (Live Shot) Kerry.
"I support what the President is doing" on school safety, Mr. Kerry said.
Among those things, announced by Mr. Clinton today, is the distribution of a Government guide to every school in the country to help teachers and parents recognize children who may be prone to violence. According to the guide, such signs include social isolation, bad grades and "inappropriate access" to firearms.
Throughout the day, Mr. Clinton appeared by turns drained and relaxed. He shook nearly every hand in sight. And he apparently felt no need to rush back to Martha's Vineyard, lingering for more than 1 hour 45 minutes with Senator Edward M. Kennedy and other Democratic politicians at Scano's Restaurant here, where he shed his suit jacket and downed a "steak bomb" submarine sandwich with french fries.
The restaurant's owner, Mary Scano, a former member of the City Council, strongly supported Mr. Clinton. Asked about his admission of an improper relationship that he had denied for seven months, Ms. Scano said: "So he lied. I lie. When I was on the Council, I lied."

(Steve Lanava/Worcester Telegram via Associated Press)

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: IN WASHINGTON;
From the U.S.: No New Answers, No New Money

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER and STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
With Russia in economic free fall and only four days before a planned summit meeting between President Clinton and the Russian President, Boris Yeltsin, Clinton Administration officials said today that Mr. Clinton would go to Moscow with words of encouragement but no new money.
American officials were hastily rewriting the summit script even as they scrambled to devise a longer-range plan to prepare for a Russia without Mr. Yeltsin, who has been the focus of American policy for years. 
 While the Administration talked of credible fiscal policies for a new Russia, the United States seemed to be watching the events from the sidelines and with a hesitation that contrasted with the quickly offered monetary aid and statements of assurance during earlier, and less serious, crises in Russia.
American officials say they have no new answers to Russia's political and financial crises, and no new money to provide beyond what the International Monetary Fund has already pledged, and Mr. Clinton has already told Mr. Yeltsin that.
"We will not hit the ground with a bag of tricks," a senior official said today.
Highlighting this sense of uncertainty, another senior official for the first time suggested that the summit meeting might be delayed or canceled, after days of insistence that it would take place as scheduled despite the lack of a functioning government in Moscow.
The official, describing the situation in Russia as "extremely fluid," said the Administration was watching news reports with one eye and the financial ticker with the other. As rumors of Mr. Yeltsin's imminent resignation swirled, the value of the Russian currency tumbled and the United States stock market took a 357-point hit.
Samuel R. Berger, the national security adviser, repeated the Administration position that the United States-Russia relationship was built on policies, not personalities. Echoing Mr. Clinton's comments to Mr. Yeltsin in a telephone call on Tuesday, Mr. Berger urged the Russian President and his Acting Premier, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, to continue on the path of economic reform laid out by the previous Government of Sergei V. Kiriyenko, whom Mr. Yeltsin sacked on Monday.
"I think the composition of the Government is obviously for the Russians to decide," Mr. Berger said during a briefing on the capture of two suspects in the Aug. 7 embassy bombings in Africa. "What we were -- what we are concerned about and would be -- are extremely interested in is the policy direction that the new Government takes. Does the new Government stay essentially on course with respect to economic reform and with respect to democracy, or does it change course? If it changed course in any kind of fundamental way, that would be of serious concern to us."
Mr. Berger was the only senior American official to address the Russian crisis on the record today, a sign that the Administration was mystified by developments in Moscow and uncertain what to say about them.
Mr. Clinton ignored shouted questions about Russia from reporters during a visit to Worcester, Mass., for a speech on education policy, and later lashed out at staff members for not adequately briefing him on the situation or preparing him for questions on it.
But one White House aide said it was unlikely that Mr. Clinton would have said anything today on the unraveling situation in Russia in any case, for fear of roiling financial markets and adding to the instability in Moscow. "Anything you say, the words get parsed beyond all belief," the official said. "You say something and it gets picked apart, and it means whatever investors want it to mean."
Mr. Clinton received a detailed briefing book for the meeting at his vacation home on Martha's Vineyard on Wednesday night, but it was not clear whether he had read it. Glyn T. Davies, the executive secretary of the National Security Council, flew to the island earlier this week to help prepare Mr. Clinton for next week's summit meeting and to brief him on developments in Russia.
Mr. Clinton spoke with Tony Blair, the British Prime Minister, late this afternoon about the Russian crisis and Mr. Clinton's visit to Ireland and Northern Ireland next week, immediately following his two days in Moscow. The two leaders agreed that continued economic reform in Russia is "vital," a British spokeswoman said.
Lawrence H. Summers, the Deputy Treasury Secretary, returned early to Washington from a vacation to oversee economic preparations for the summit talks.
Strobe Talbott, the Deputy Secretary of State, was in Moscow in a series of urgent last-minute meetings to try to salvage some concrete results from next week's meeting and to gauge the climate for United States-Russian relations in months to come.
Mr. Yeltsin's departure could certainly scotch plans for the meeting, but American officials discounted reports that he was preparing to step down. "Obviously, the situation is tumultuous and day-to-day," one Administration official said. "We've heard the rumors, but no one has given us any hint as of yet that there is a change" in Mr. Yeltsin's status.
"It's still our expectation that the summit will go forward and be productive," the official said.
In a sense, the impending summit meeting is a gift for American officials. It gives them a short-term event to focus on and allows them to put off for another day the far more difficult question of how to deal with a foundering and virtually bankrupt nation in possession of thousands of nuclear weapons. Discussion of those questions has been put on hold, one official said, until it is clear who will be running Russia next week -- and after.
Also unclear is whether the I.M.F. will deliver the next $4.3 billion payment of a loan package, due Sept. 15. The payment depends on Russian legislative action to reform the tax and pension systems, votes that look increasingly unlikely. The Duma, the lower house of Parliament, must first confirm Mr. Chernomyrdin as the Prime Minister, and the debate could be contentious and long.
"We cannot save Russia, only Russia can do that," a senior official said.
"The President can't calm the markets," he said. "The markets will be affected only by clear evidence of Russian seriousness of purpose, from Yeltsin to the Duma, to fix the problems and insure that foreigners get a fair shake. But Clinton can engage Yeltsin in a discussion of the political imperatives of handling the economics."
In a telephone call to Mr. Yeltsin on Tuesday, Mr. Clinton pushed him in that direction, an official said. "Both Presidents reaffirmed the importance of a clear, decisive strategy for tackling Russia's economic crisis," said Barry Toiv, the deputy White House spokesman. "President Yeltsin stressed that this will be the top priority of the new Russian Government."
Senior American officials, in a sign that they believe the Yeltsin era is all but over, see the summit meeting as a way to begin to build bridges to the next generation of Russian leaders. Mr. Clinton's main public address of the two-day summit -- a speech next Tuesday to students and young business leaders -- is an explicit effort to speak over the heads of Russia's current leaders to its people.
Mr. Clinton will speak about the evolving relationship between the countries and offer sympathy for the pain of the transformation Russia is suffering, an official involved in summit preparations said.
On Wednesday, Mr. Clinton is scheduled to meet a dozen or more parliamentary and regional leaders, another effort to engage with a new generation of leaders not tainted by the Communist era or the difficult transitional years of Mr. Yeltsin's presidency.
Critics of American policy in the United States and in Russia have often questioned what they call Mr. Clinton's overly close relationship with Mr. Yeltsin, ignoring other potential Russian leaders. Administration officials respond that Presidents deal with Presidents, that Russia has only one President at a time, and that American officials are in contact with Russian politicians at all levels.
Peter Reddaway, a Russia scholar at George Washington University, said he thought Mr. Clinton should cancel his trip, in part because there will be no one of real authority to engage, and in part because Mr. Clinton may be greeted with "nasty demonstrations or comments" from Russians who resent his close relationship with Mr. Yeltsin, whom many Russians revile.
"It would be foolish to undertake any initiative, political or economic, in this volatile situation," Mr. Reddaway said. "You can't have a serious negotiation because there's no real government."

HEADLINE: Cablevision to Buy 20 Movie Theaters in Region From Loews Cineplex

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
Moving to grab a bigger piece of the region's movie theater market, Cablevision Systems Corporation announced yesterday that it would buy 20 theaters in the region, including the Ziegfeld and a dozen others in Manhattan, from Loews Cineplex Entertainment Corporation as part of a package worth $91.7 million.
The package includes two transactions: one for $85 million in cash for the 13 Manhattan theaters and 11 in Chicago, and another for $6.7 million in cash for 7 theaters in Long Island, Connecticut and New Jersey. 
 The acquisitions represent Cablevision's second purchase of a theater chain in the last two weeks. On Aug. 13, Cablevision paid $160 million in cash and stock to Clearview Cinema Group Inc., of Chatham, N.J., which has 45 theaters in suburban New York and New Jersey.
At the same time, the transactions reflect a recent push by Cablevision, based in Woodbury, N.Y., to make as much of a splash as possible in the business of local entertainment -- be it movies, television or sports.
Already, Cablevision offers cable service to more than 3.4 million customers in New York, Boston and Cleveland, and it owns a controlling interest in Radio City Entertainment, Madison Square Garden, the New York Knicks, the New York Rangers and the MSG Network, among other properties.
"A marketwide presence in movie theaters extends Cablevision's ability to offer metro area consumers a choice of live, film and televised entertainment," James L. Dolan, Cablevision's president and chief executive, said in a statement.
Last fall, the Sony Corporation agreed to merge its Loews Theaters unit with the Cineplex Odeon Corporation to create one of the world's largest movie theater companies, Loews Cineplex Entertainment Corporation, with more than 400 theaters nationwide and annual revenues of about $1 billion.
But after the Justice Department and the New York State Attorney General's office raised concerns that the combined theater chain might monopolize the market in Manhattan, the company agreed to sell about 14 of its movie theaters there.
Cablevision, meanwhile, has increasingly diversified its interests as part of a strategy to rely on a grab bag of entertainment and media forums to promote its products.
These include an alliance with the At Home Corporation to deliver high-speed Internet service through its cable systems and Cablevision's recent purchase of Nobody Beats the Wiz, the electronics retail chain.
But the movie theaters are an even more alluring opportunity, perhaps, to pump up Cablevision's brand name -- and the more movie theaters, the more powerful the leverage.
So yesterday's transactions fit neatly into the long-term strategies of both Loews and Cablevision, said Christopher Dixon, an entertainment analyst at Paine Webber.
"It's consistent for both companies," Mr. Dixon said.
"The theater business is all about the ability to create scale economies. That means you need to have a fairly large number of screens so that you can be assured that the studios will pay attention to you," he said.
Economies of scale are particularly important in the movie business.
While the number of movie screens has almost doubled to 31,000 nationwide since 1949, the number of people going to the movies has actually stayed the same, Mr. Dixon said.
Yesterday's agreement is still subject to approval by the Justice Department. But if approved, the movie theaters would be managed by Cablevision's Madison Square Garden unit, which is run by David W. Checketts, the president and chief executive.
In Manhattan, the group of 13 theaters, with a total of 35 screens, includes such marquee names as the Beekman and the Waverly.
Cablevision officials added that they expected to sell the Chicago movie theaters in order to concentrate on the New York region.
Mr. Dixon said the deal would have little effect on moviegoers; after all, few people probably knew that the Ziegfeld was run by the Loews Cineplex Entertainment Corporation.
But if Cablevision has its way, it may be able to offer its cable customers discounts on movie tickets, or combine the sale of tickets for sports events with movie ticket discounts.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
3M SAYS IT WILL CUT 500 MORE JOBS BY THE END OF 1999

BYLINE: AP 

 BODY:
The Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company said yesterday that it would eliminate 4,500 jobs by the end of next year, 500 more than the company announced in late July. The job cuts, which will reduce the 3M work force to about 71,000, are aimed at improving productivity. The company, based in Maplewood, Minn., also announced previously that it would consolidate some manufacturing operations worldwide. The company announced the additional 500 job cuts yesterday during a meeting with financial analysts. 3M expects to take a pretax charge of as much as $500 million for the revamping. The company's shares closed at $73.8125, down 31.25 cents, after hitting a 52-week low earlier in the day.

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE WEST;
Europe Gets A Bit Jumpy Over Russia

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, Aug. 27

 BODY:
With the monetary crisis in Russia creating turmoil in global financial markets, concern grew in European capitals today that the crisis could throttle economic growth in their own countries or weaken Russia's commitment to democracy.
But with Chancellor Helmut Kohl of Germany in the fight of his life for re-election a month from now and with President Clinton and President Boris N. Yeltsin both seen to be in weakened political positions, few here saw much chance of quick, decisive action during the United States-Russian summit meeting planned for next week in Moscow. 
 Indeed, Stanley Fischer, deputy managing director of the I.M.F., raised eyebrows with an interview Monday with the German daily newspaper Frankfurter Allgemeine Zeitung that was sharply critical of inaction by the Group of Seven earlier this month when Russia was forced to stop supporting the value of the ruble against foreign currencies.
"It was clear to me that the situation was untenable after the G-7 didn't rush to Russia's aid," the newspaper quoted him as saying. "I'm not sure Chancellor Helmut Kohl would not have behaved differently if the elections were taking place two years instead of weeks from now."
Mr. Kohl, campaigning in Berlin today, spoke by telephone with President Jacques Chirac of France, German officials said, and planned to call Mr. Clinton and Prime Minister Tony Blair of Britain.
Public advice from European political leaders to President Yeltsin and his Prime Minister-designate, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, was the same today as it has been all along: Without radical changes in the Russian financial and economic system, more Western help will not work, they said.
"It won't be possible to mobilize German and international aid without reforms," Mr. Kohl said. "Without sensible reforms, it won't turn out well."
European officials tried to reassure investors in their own financial markets today that Russia's economy was too small for a collapse to do serious damage to their own much larger economies.
The real concern of European leaders here is not about economic stability but political stability in Russia, which is still a nuclear power with an army bigger than all European armed forces put together. And all European leaders, for years, have backed Mr. Yeltsin as the bulwark of his country's commitment to democracy.
Fear that this commitment could come unraveled, some financial analysts suggested, also might explain the panic in European markets today. "Sidelining of Mr. Yeltsin Prepared," the French newspaper Le Monde reported today.
And after the stock market in Paris fell more than 4 percent, Peter Thorne, an analyst with Banque Paribas, said today, "Russia obviously has nuclear weapons, and I think the psychology is that Russia is more important than it really is."
French officials, just emerging from their annual August vacations, said something needed to be done to reinforce the International Monetary Fund, which is providing Russia with a $22.6 billion rescue package. Much of the latest installment of that money was spent by Russia's central bank in a failed attempt to defend the ruble.
Mr. Chirac, in a speech to French ambassadors from around the world, said Wednesday that the fund needed a decision-making political body at the ministerial level to strengthen its ability to deal with crises. International aid would only succeed, he said, "if the necessary internal reforms supported by President Yeltsin are resolutely put into effect by the new Government of Mr. Chernomyrdin."
Dominique Strauss-Kahn, the French Finance Minister, said the same thing today after a meeting here with his German counterpart, Theo Waigel.
Mr. Strauss-Kahn said he and Mr. Waigel were considering sending a letter to Mr. Chernomyrdin, who is widely seen in Europe as likely to go slow with the kind of fiscal and financial changes Western experts say it will take to nurse Russia's economy back to health.
Some economists wondered openly today why the Group of Seven industrial countries, led by the United States, had not taken a more active role in managing the crisis.
"For too long, Russia has been pretending to observe the conditions imposed by the I.M.F. and the I.M.F. has been pretending that it was doing so," Christian de Boissieu, an economics professor at the Sorbonne, told Le Monde. "The G-7 should take over," he said. The Group of Seven includes the United States, Canada, Germany, Japan, Britain, France and Italy.

HEADLINE: THINK TANK;
Studying How the Defeat of Heat Changed Nearly Everything

BYLINE: By Janny Scott 

 BODY:
Raymond Arsenault stumbled into his scholarly sideline back in 1982 when he was invited to speak on a panel on climate and history. "Just tell them about air-conditioning," his wife, a seventh-generation Floridian, suggested facetiously. And so began Mr. Arsenault's great air-conditioning adventure.
He turned his conference paper into an article, "The End of the Long Hot Summer: The Air-Conditioner and Southern Culture," which was published in The Journal of Southern History, then syndicated, then anthologized, then reprinted so often it has become the journal's most requested article for the last 15 years. 
 "I'm known jokingly among my friends as 'the air-conditioner man'," said Mr. Arsenault, a professor of history at the University of South Florida, who could be found recently discussing the cultural history of air-conditioning on ABC's "Nightline." "I feel like a typecast actor sometimes."
Air-conditioning, as it turns out, is a rich topic, something so ubiquitous and so easily taken for granted that it seems to have occurred to few scholars to examine its impact on everything from industrialization, migration patterns and public health to architecture, family life, the environment and attitudes toward nature.
"It's one of my favorite subjects," said Richard P. Nathan, a political economist and director of the Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany. "The civil rights revolution and air-conditioning are the two biggest factors that have changed demography and a lot of our politics in the last 30 years."
From the installation of the world's first air-conditioning system in a Brooklyn printing company in 1902, through its march through industrial plants, then movie theaters, then government buildings, hospitals, hotels and finally homes, air-conditioning was catapulted in the public perception "from luxury to amenity to necessity," as Mr. Arsenault recently put it.
The population of the South grew dramatically, as mortality dropped and in-migration grew. Air-conditioning proved crucial to the industrialization of the region. It improved productivity and encouraged economic growth, Mr. Arsenault concluded. It encouraged urbanization, in part by helping make skyscrapers and high-rise apartments more feasible.
At the same time, Mr. Arsenault wrote, it "weakened the bond between humanity and the natural environment" and "impinged upon a rich tradition of vernacular architecture" by making it possible to build inexpensive tract homes after World War II without the high ceilings, long breezeways, open porches, transoms and other features that once kept people cool.
For years Mr. Arsenault's 32-page article, replete with footnotes citing such sources as Air-Conditioning, Heating and Refrigeration News, has stood as the most comprehensive scholarly attempt to explore the history of air-conditioning, the growth of the industry and air-conditioning's impact on society and culture.
Now, Gail Cooper, a historian of technology and an associate professor at Lehigh University in Bethlehem, Pa., has published "Air-Conditioning America: Engineers and the Controlled Environment, 1900-1960" (Johns Hopkins, 1998), a study focusing on the engineering effort to create an "indoor climate."
In addition, the National Building Museum in Washington, which takes as its mission the celebration of American achievements in "the built environment," intends to open next May what is being billed as the first major exhibition to explore the transformative power of air-conditioning.
Ms. Cooper, who is 44, grew up with air-conditioning in California's Central Valley and remembers crawling out of her crib and into the hall to sleep on the floor under the air-conditioner. Now she lives in an 1890's house in the historic district of Bethlehem, where her summers are filled with ceiling fans, iced drinks and occasional escape to the movies.
"I see it as a complex subject," Ms. Cooper said. "I tried to describe how users feel: sometimes they love it; sometimes they don't." As for herself, she works in a windowless office. "It's the kind of room that is only possible with air-conditioning," she said. "I think a lot of people do miss windows."

HEADLINE: Even a Jihad Has Its Rules

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER 

 BODY:
Osama bin Laden, the renegade Saudi financier linked to the recent bombings of American Embassies in Kenya and Tanzania, has justified his call to kill Americans by citing Muslim holy law. "Allah ordered us in this religion to purify Muslim land of all nonbelievers," he said in an interview with ABC in May.
Libya, Iraq and militant Islamic groups have also applauded the bombings. Meanwhile, leaders in the Arab-American community and Islamic specialists have argued that such terrorists have distorted Islam's traditional teachings. And in a speech last week, President Clinton made a point of drawing a distinction between Muslim extremists and traditional Muslims -- the "hundreds of millions of good, peace-loving people all around the world," as he described them -- who condemn the killing of noncombatants in terrorist attacks. 
 But just what does Muslim holy law say about these kind of terrorist attacks?
Islamic holy law, or sharia, is based on the Koran and the precepts and practices of the Muslim prophet Mohammed and his seventh-century companions.
Islam, of course, is open to many interpretations. But several Islamic scholars who live in the West say that Mr. bin Laden has justified his jihad, or holy war, against the United States by a highly unorthodox, though increasingly common interpretation of Islamic law and political tradition.
Following in the tradition of other 20th-century radicals, like Sayyid Qutb, the Egyptian militant theorist executed in 1966, Mr. bin Laden argues that a war against the United States is obligatory because of American "crimes" against Islam.
These are often listed as America's presence on sacred Saudi Arabian soil, support for rulers throughout the Middle East whom the radicals say are "un-Islamic" and corrupt, Washington's "blind support" for what is characterized as Israel's "theft" of Muslim land and persecution of Palestinians, and supposed American indifference to the suffering of Bosnian Muslims. During his May interview, Mr. bin Laden made a point of saying, "After World War II, the Americans became more aggressive and oppressive, especially in the Muslim world," adding, "We do not differentiate between those dressed in military uniforms and civilians; they are all targets in this fatwa."
Many Islamic scholars, however, argue that the holy war Mr. bin Laden has declared violates not only the elaborate regulations of Islamic law as traditionally accepted but also Arab tribal custom.
Peter J. Awn, professor of religion and Middle Eastern studies at Columbia University, said that according to classical Islamic law, a jihad must be fought for the ultimate good of the Muslim faith. "It cannot be used simply for economic or political gain," Professor Awn said.
According to mainstream Muslim jurists and scholars, there are four kinds of legitimate warfare in Islam, including wars against infidels and apostates, which are both jihads; all must be declared and conducted by the Muslim ruler of a state, or failing that, Islamic wise men. Mr. bin Laden, of course, is not a leader of a state. Nor is he a qadi, a judge, or a mufti, a Muslim legal scholar, Professor Awn added, and thus, is unqualified to issue what his supporters call fatwas, his religious rulings or opinions justifying the anti-American campaign.
Quadir Amiryar, a political scientist at George Washington University, agrees with Professor Awn and notes that many of those who issue "fatwas" don't follow the procedures that Islamic law requires.
He also argues that spreading Islam "by the sword" is traditionally considered a last resort. While it might have been justified during the invasion of Afghanistan, he said, "that justification evaporated with the departure of the Soviet Union." True, many Muslim clerics in the Middle East and elsewhere have interpreted Muslim law to justify terrorism, but Professor Amiryar argues that those calls for jihad often stem from frustration with their own leadership: "They are using these Islamic concepts in other contexts which were originally not intended."
In his 1996 book on jihad, Rudolph Peters, a Dutch scholar, argues that while the umma, or Muslim community, has a duty to "expand the territory of the state," the doctrine of jihad forbids, among other things, the killing of noncombatants like children, women and old people. Thus, it would appear that even if Islamic law were stretched to consider women and children at the American Embassies combatants, the hundreds of Africans, many of whom are Muslim, who were killed near those embassies could not be considered combatants.
Muslim law also provides that ambassadors and their missions be respected as long as they do not engage in espionage. (That's why the Islamic Government in Teheran insisted on characterizing the American Embassy where hostages were seized in the 1970's as "a nest of spies").
Professor Peters says a "war against unbelievers may not be mounted without summoning them to Islam or submission before an attack." In other words, people should be given a chance to embrace Islam as their faith before they are killed.
Mr. bin Laden's defense of and reported ties to Egyptian and Palestinian suicide attacks on Israeli and American targets are also questionable under traditional Islamic precepts, scholars say. Islamic holy law forbids suicide. According to the traditions of the Prophet Mohammed, which believers claim are divinely inspired, the punishment is eternal repetition of the act by which a person dies. So if a man hangs himself, he'll spend an eternity choking.
But as Professor Awn points out, Islam endorses martyrdom -- fighting, and if necessary, dying for one's faith. And one Muslim's suicide might be another's martyrdom.

HEADLINE: Consumer Spending Declines For First Time in Two Years

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Consumer spending fell in July for the first time in more than two years as the strikes at the General Motors Corporation took a toll on auto sales. Analysts said falling stock prices could cause spending to weaken further.
The Commerce Department said today that spending declined two-tenths of 1 percent last month, in contrast to June's increase of six-tenths of 1 percent. The spending decline came even though Americans' incomes rose half a percent in July, exceeding June's rise of three-tenths of 1 percent -- a sign that jobs remained plentiful. 
Also today, figures compiled by the University of Michigan showed consumer sentiment weakened in August, falling to 104.4 on the university's index of sentiment from 105.5 in July. Because consumer spending accounts for about two-thirds of the nation's output of goods and services, any sign that consumers are not in a buying mood could be a signal that economic growth will slow.
"The strength of the stock market gave us some pretty spectacular spending numbers in the first half of the year," Christopher Low, an economist at First Tennessee Capital Markets in New York, said. "Now we're seeing confidence dented and spending weakened. The blow to the stock market is going to have the opposite effect in the third quarter."
July's drop in spending, the first since a decline of six-tenths of 1 percent in June 1996, reflected a decline in auto sales, the Commerce Department said. General Motors' sales decreased 38 percent last month because of two strikes that started in early June and shut down 26 assembly plants. By July, dealers were reporting shortages of some popular models.
In a sign consumer spending has some life left, consumption of nondurable goods rose 3.7 percent in July.
The report from the University of Michigan was in line with the findings of the Conference Board, which said earlier this week that its index of consumer confidence fell to 133.1 in August from 137.2 in July.

HEADLINE: Taking Action on Patients' Rights

 BODY:
The Senate will not have much time to approve legislation on patients' rights when it returns next week from its August recess. Its chances of enacting something worthwhile will be slim if Republicans and Democrats insist on turning the stalemate into a campaign issue with which to hammer each other. The blame strategy, however, could backfire with voters who want protection from unfair health insurance practices.
The House has already passed a bill that would guarantee emergency care coverage, allow consumers to appeal denials of coverage to an independent panel and permit women direct access to gynecologists and obstetricians, all of which the Democrats want. But President Clinton is likely to veto this plan because it expands medical savings accounts, a provision strongly opposed by the Democrats, and it does not change existing law that bars consumers from suing federally regulated health plans for damages related to the denial of benefits. The Senate Republican bill, sponsored by Don Nickles of Oklahoma, is unacceptable because it would apply only to the 48 million individuals covered by plans in which large employers act as their own insurers. 
 The Senate Democratic plan offers broader protections and would apply to an estimated 150 million privately insured individuals. It would also allow people to sue for damages when benefits are denied. But the Republicans look on any open-ended right to sue as a poison pill that will drive up insurance costs.
There is a workable, bipartisan compromise bill that could end this political standoff. Sponsored by Senators John Chafee, Joseph Lieberman, Bob Graham, Arlen Specter and Max Baucus, the plan adopts nearly all the protections favored by the Democrats and does not expand medical savings accounts. In benefit denial cases, it would allow suits in Federal court for recovery of economic damages and attorneys' fees only. While this would not allow compensation for pain and suffering or punitive damages, it is a step in the right direction and substantially better than the current law, which allows practically no recovery at all.
The measure also contains an important enforcement provision that no other bill offers. It authorizes the Departments of Labor and Health and Human Services to levy fines upwards of $250,000 against health plans that fail to comply with the requirements of the bill. That would include failure to provide benefits specified in the bill, as well as failure to meet disclosure and other obligations. This plan deserves the White House's support.

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: IN NEW YORK;
Russian Emigrants: Mixed Views on the Old Country

BYLINE: By JIM YARDLEY 

 BODY:
She only arrived in Brighton Beach, Brooklyn, from Moscow two weeks ago, but Natalia Kuznetsova has followed the crisis at home by telephoning friends. She knows that the banks are closed, that the stores are closed, that the ruble is practically worthless. Her friends tell her they pass the days drinking vodka.
But even as Ms. Kuznetsova in one breath compares daily life in Russia to a war, in the next breath she smiles and shrugs at the financial and political upheaval in Moscow that has rippled across the world. She said she hoped to stay in the United States for a year or longer, but the thought of returning home did not bother her. 
 "We had the same situation 5 years ago, 10 years ago," said Ms. Kuznetsova, who at 26 spoke with the resignation of someone older. "It's a normal situation in our country."
In Brighton Beach, the neighborhood that for two decades has absorbed layer upon layer of emigrants from the old Soviet Union, the news from Russia was greeted with alarm, but also with apathy. For every person who rushed to wire money to relatives in Russia, others claimed they could care less about life there. People who still own property there, or conduct business, stayed in close contact with friends, monitoring the latest changes. And still others considered the crisis about as realistic as the televised Russian soap operas playing at some of the stores on Brighton Beach Avenue.
"It's a great play by the Government to get money from the international financial system and to steal their money," scoffed Gennady Grigorovich, owner of Cafi Paris restaurant on Coney Island Avenue, an Ukrainian immigrant who has lived in Brooklyn for 20 years.
A few people at Cafi Volna on the Brighton Beach boardwalk watched Boris N. Yeltsin announce yesterday on Russian television that he would remain as President until 2000. Not long after the speech, the channel was quickly changed to the Weather Channel to follow a more immediate problem, the path of Hurricane Bonnie.
"He's going to stay?" asked Sopha Liebanov, manager of Feminik, a women's clothing storea few blocks away on Brighton Beach Avenue. "I don't think so. It doesn't matter if it's Yeltsin or Chernomyrdin. They stole all the money and put it in their pockets. The people don't work and everything is closed. They expect credits from America."
Ms. Liebanov blamed those in Brighton Beach who shrugged off the crisis, saying that many immigrants have lived in Brooklyn for so long that they have forgotten the hardship of their old lives. "The people don't understand," she said. "They think, 'It's so far, so who cares?' "
Boris Talis, executive director of Brighton Beach District Management Association, a coalition of local businesses, said several Russians visiting the United States on business have rushed back in the last two days as the fiscal crisis has deepened. As for Brighton Beach merchants, Mr. Talis said the crisis has had "no effect, only morally. This is a sad story. Nobody knows what can happen in the next three or four days."
Alexy Dementyev, a clerk at United Air and Parcel Service on Coney Island Avenue, said that on Thursday customers wired a total of about $10,000 to relatives in Russia and other former Soviet states, a significant increase. But he also said that the money may not be bringing much help.
"Western Union in some cities are paying in rubles," Mr. Dementyev said. "That's not very convenient because the exchange rate is not too positive. Nevertheless, people are sending."
Three years ago, Irena Chikantseva and her husband, Andrey Ahramov, came to Brighton Beach from Moscow and opened a gift store. They still own a four-bedroom apartment in downtown Moscow, but they said they can no longer find anyone able to pay the $2,000 monthly rent. Besides the financial crisis, the couple say that rising crime has kept the apartment empty; the last tenant moved out because the building had no doorman. Now, the couple regularly call friends and family in Moscow.
Ms. Chikantseva's sister in Moscow, whose son is approaching draft age, worries that the instability could lead to another war like those in Chechnya and Afghanistan. Mr. Ahramov's mother lives in Moscow, surviving on a pension, but he said she was not alarmed, perhaps because crisis is nothing new to the older generation.
"Nobody can fix the situation," Mr. Ahramov lamented.
At a barber shop, a Russian barber waved off questions, unimpressed with the latest crisis at home.
"It's life," he said. "People suffer."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Prices Move Up Slightly as Flight to Treasuries Continues

BYLINE: By Bridge News 

 BODY:
Treasury prices ended slightly higher yesterday, extending a rally sustained by investors fleeing turmoil on world markets. Some profit taking, however, held back the gains.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 4/32 , to 102 17/32 . The bond's yield -- an important benchmark for consumer and corporate interest rates -- slipped to 5.33 percent, from 5.34 percent on Thursday. (Bond prices and their yields move in opposite directions.) For the week, the yield fell 10 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point. 
 The yield on the 10-year note dropped to 5.05 percent yesterday from 5.06 percent on Thursday -- down 23 basis points for the week.
For most of the session, Treasury prices inversely tracked United States stocks, rising when share prices fell, as traders and investors focused on developments in Russia.
"The crisis is moving a little bit closer to home," said Bill Kirby, co-head of government securities trading at Prudential Securities in New York, "It's not in the whites of our eyes yet, but it looks like there's going to be some slowdown, and Treasuries are seeing the flight to quality."
Some market participants are beginning to speculate that the weakness in the stock market might cause Federal Reserve policy makers to consider cutting interest rates. But many analysts say the Fed will need to see a slowdown in the domestic economy before it will cut rates.
The Fed will "have to believe the situation is really critical" to cut rates between meetings, said Peter Kretzmer, an economist at Nationsbank. "We do think ultimately they will, and maybe the urgency did increase, but Fed policy needs to be guided by the domestic economy." Fed policy makers next meet on Sept. 29.
The members of the Fed are meeting in Jackson Hole, Wyo., at an annual conference.
The president of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, William Poole, told the conference that rates were low enough that the Fed could afford to bide its time on adjusting monetary policy. Traders thought this was significant because Mr. Poole had voted to raise rates at the Fed's policy meeting in May.

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE INVESTORS;
In New York, a Mixture of Denial and Optimism

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON and DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
The only way Dunstan Connell could have been more emphatic would have been if he had borrowed Alfred E. Neuman's shibboleth from Mad magazine: What, me worry?
"This?" Mr. Connell, a clerk for Charles Schwab & Company who works at the New York Stock Exchange, said as the Dow Jones industrial average was seesawing yesterday between a gain and a loss. "It's not a big deal." 
 He enumerated the milestones of his career. "I was down here for the crash," he said, as everyone on Wall Street refers to Oct. 19, 1987, when the Dow plunged 508 points, or 22 percent. "I was down here last year," when the Dow fell 554.26 points, or 7.2 percent, on Oct. 27.
"We've been through this before," he said. "Even Thursday wasn't that bad."
From the fashion industry's workrooms on Seventh Avenue to the exercise bicycles in private midtown clubs to the carpeted waiting rooms in storefront brokerage offices, New Yorkers watched as the markets dipped and climbed and dipped again yesterday. By the time the Dow finally settled at 8,051.68 -- down 114.31 points, or 1.4 percent -- most still seemed unworried by Wall Street's sudden turn toward bear market territory.
Like millions of other American investors who have ridden the stock market like a roller coaster the last few years, investors and stock market employees interviewed during the day acknowledged some queasiness about where the market was headed -- if not yesterday, then next week. But no one seemed to be using the p-word.
"I don't see anybody panicking," said Dean Rasi, a clerk at the New York Stock Exchange. "Some brokers are having a hard time. I hear them talking. You can hear guys go, 'This is killing me, this is killing me,' trying to rush their orders."
Other stock exchange workers reported hearing an unaccustomed edginess in brokers' voices, an unfamiliar uncertainty. But many small investors still sounded confident. Some said they had shifted their money into cash or money market funds, but most said they were not selling out. Not yet, anyway.
"I've inherited a lot of my stocks, and if you inherit blue chips, you hang onto them," Anne Murphy, a costume designer, said, smiling brightly yesterday afternoon in Bryant Park in New York City. "I figure it's all going to balance out. We went up so much last year, so if it went back to where it was a year ago that wouldn't be so bad."
Robert Banks, an engineer in St. Louis who specializes in high-definition television sets, was even more upbeat. Despite the economic meltdown in Russia and the tremors in Asia, he said the stock market was still a healthy outpost.
"I'm not all that concerned," he said early yesterday afternoon, when the Dow was down about 61 points. "Right now, our economy is strong. The rest of the world looks to us, so you have to be careful of knee-jerk reactions."
Staying the course has been a motto for many small investors throughout the long bull market of the 1990's, which many Wall Street analysts date to October 1990. It was the small investors who "bought the dips." They went bargain-hunting. They ignored all the noise about Asia and currency collapses. After all, last October's 13.2 percent drop in the Dow, the worst in the 1990's, was followed by a 30 percent rally.
Leonard Soriano of Trenton, who gave up managing health clubs to manage his portfolio from home, was convinced that pattern was still intact. "It can only go up," he said. "It will go down, but in the long term it's always gone up."
But yesterday he was selling. "I'm in front of my computer right now, trying to get out of stuff," he said. "I'm ready to jump off the Empire State Building, but they don't do that anymore," he added with a chuckle. "My net worth is down 50 percent in the last few weeks."
Mr. Soriano said he was "not trading money I need." He also said he had started to put aside cash as a cushion if prices tumbled more than they already had.
Like Mr. Soriano, millions of other investors are more attuned than ever to the stock market's every gyration. Labor officials talk about how even union halls have big-screen television sets tuned to CNBC and how the buzz in the room stops as the closing bell nears.
But perhaps nothing has brought the market closer to small investors than the Internet. Omar Wasow, who monitors on-line chat rooms for MSNBC, said yesterday that the chatter remained almost universally optimistic about the market's long-term prospects.
"I was really astonished, given the leading, critical questions I asked, that people were not concerned," Mr. Wasow said. "One person I talked to said you've got to be in it for the long term, and that sort of echoed the general consensus."
Among those in one Internet chat room Mr. Wasow plugged into was someone identified as Crabtown, who said that baby boomers could sustain the stock market through 2005, when boomers begin to retire and cash in their holdings. Crabtown, with the characteristic bravado of the 90's investor, predicted that the Dow would hit 15,000 before then.

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE OUTPOST;
It's a Dark and Stormy Night . . . and Here's Hong Kong Trying to Line the Clouds With Silver

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Amid the battering of stock markets around the world this week, Hong Kong has seemed strangely stable -- an oasis of calm at the eye of the storm. But appearances are deceiving.
In a climax to an unprecedented two-week-long direct intervention in the stock market, Hong Kong's government spent at least $8 billion today on a buying spree to prop up shares and foil speculators it accuses of manipulating its market. 
 On Thursday, an earlier round of intervention had managed to push the Hong Kong stock market up while every other market around the globe was down. Today, for all the money spent, the benchmark Hang Seng index still declined 93.23 points, or 1.2 percent, to 7,829.74.
Hong Kong has always been unusual in Asia, a rare outpost of free markets in a region of state-managed capitalism. The government, while committed to a fixed exchange rate with the United States dollar and prepared to support the property market the way South Africa's DeBeers regulates the price of diamonds, historically has gone to great lengths to avoid meddling in its markets.
So, in a city where tracking the Hang Seng is a favorite parlor game, today's wild ride -- by far the market's biggest trading volume ever -- left people slack-jawed. At moments during the morning trading session, the Hong Kong Stock Exchange seemed less a market than a giant clearance sale, with the government buying everything in sight.
"They did this at a time when there is gloom around the world," said Glenn Lesko, the director of regional sales and trading at ABN Amro Securities in Hong Kong. "I think they ran into far more sellers than they expected."
Government officials, however, claimed victory. They said the intervention had stabilized the Hang Seng, even as markets across Asia, Europe and the United States were plummeting because of fears about the financial turmoil in Russia and other emerging markets. That stymied traders who had taken short positions on shares and futures contracts on the bet that the Hong Kong market would tumble.
For Hong Kong, today's action was the boldest step yet in a costly effort to erect a wall between itself and the financial typhoon raging around it. Although Hong Kong has already been whipsawed by the Asian crisis, the territory's leaders are determined not to be sucked into an even more calamitous vortex of currency attacks followed by a stock market collapse.
Traders estimate that the Hong Kong Monetary Authority has spent at least $12 billion since it began buying shares and futures contracts on Aug. 14. Nonetheless, Sir Donald Tsang, Hong Kong's financial secretary, said the government was ready to continue intervening. Hong Kong has already spent more than 10 percent of its $100 billion in foreign reserves on the effort.
Tung Chee-hwa, the chief executive of Hong Kong, held open the possibility that China might support Hong Kong's campaign, with its $140 billion worth of reserves. "The central Government has said many, many times openly that they are very willing to support us if there is a need to do so," he said.
In fact, Hong Kong seems determined to drive out the currency traders, even if it means intervening indefinitely. It is unclear, however, whether the government has an end-game strategy. And at today's rate of buying, it will run out of reserves in about 13 trading sessions.
Hong Kong's tough stance came in the wake of more bad economic news. The government released data confirming that Hong Kong had slipped into a recession in the second quarter, as the economy contracted 5 percent. It also projected that the economy would shrink 4 percent in 1998 -- the first full year of contraction since Hong Kong began keeping records in 1961.
But it was the government's intervention that had analysts rattled. Several said the stock market would plummet as soon as the government stopped supporting it. And some argued that Hong Kong was squandering both its reserves and its reputation as the freest market in Asia.
"In the short run, if you pour billions of dollars into stocks, you can pump up the market," said Steve H. Hanke, a professor of applied economics at Johns Hopkins University. "But in the long run, this is doomed to fail because it convinces speculators that Hong Kong can't take the pain."
Indeed, no less prominent a currency trader than George Soros seems to view Hong Kong's intervention as a quixotic effort. Stanley Druckenmiller, the manager of Mr. Soros's Quantum Fund, said in an interview on Thursday with CNBC: "No matter what they do in their market, when they wake up on Monday morning, they're still going to be in a depression."
Hong Kong may not stop at simple intervention. Sir Donald announced that the government was considering measures to curb "manipulative practices." These could include restrictions on the short-selling of stocks and increased disclosure requirements in the trading of index futures.
Hong Kong would not be the first Asian market to place restrictions on certain kinds of trading. Japan, Korea and Malaysia all do it, just as they intervene in their markets. But Hong Kong has long prided itself as the New York of Asia -- a market where Wall Street openness meets Chinese entrepreneurialism.
As it happens, some analysts believe the government has been forced to act by Hong Kong's most prominent entrepreneurs -- the property tycoons -- who have seen their share holdings decline in value by 50 percent since last year. Two months ago, the government announced it would suspend sales of land, in an effort to halt the downward spiral of property prices.
"The irony is that the government is taking the people's money and using it to bail out the property developers," said Marc Faber, an investment adviser and managing director of his own brokerage firm.
Sir Donald dismissed the suggestion that the government was putting Hong Kong's reserves at risk. He noted that the Hang Seng index has risen 1,200 points since the intervention began, which means that the government's owns more in shares than it did in cash before it began buying.
Still, the government's buying spree has put it in the uncharacteristic -- and potentially awkward -- position of being a major shareholder in many blue-chip Hong Kong companies.
Sir Donald said he was not concerned about owning large stakes in Hong Kong companies, nor about the potential negative effect of dumping those positions on the open market once the intervention was over.
Hong Kong's battle with the currency traders has mutated from a guerrilla skirmish into a full-scale war of attrition. Several traders said the government had recruited an unidentified expert to help it plot moves to thwart even the shrewdest of hedge funds. Beyond buying blue-chip shares like HSBC Holdings and Hong Kong Telecom, the government has taken a complex set of long and short positions on August and September index futures contracts.
Beyond the mechanics of the market, Hong Kong's intervention has rekindled a debate about how much governments can -- and should -- intervene to protect themselves from the vagaries of the global marketplace.
Ronnie Chan, a prominent Hong Kong property developer, said the government had an obligation to protect its citizens from a wave of speculation that was threatening to swamp the stock market and the local economy.
"The total annual output of Hong Kong is dwarfed by the daily capital flow of the markets," Mr. Chan said. "At will, the market can destroy an economy overnight. Is Hong Kong being destroyed? No. But is it suffering hugely? You bet."
In Hong Kong's case, however, the debate is complicated by the fact that it has set itself up as a mecca for global capital. Last year, the Hang Seng index rocketed to 16,000 points as foreign investors poured money into shares of "red chips" -- Chinese companies with publicly listed Hong Kong subsidiaries.
The Hong Kong dollar is another example of the territory's supposed openness to the global economy. The currency is pegged to the United States dollar at a fixed rate. The price for preserving that link at a time when most Asian countries devalued their currencies has been a spike in interest rates, which drove down property prices and the stock market.
Until this summer, Hong Kong had seemed ready to absorb those blows. But then in June, the government announced it would stop selling land. And two weeks ago, it waded into the stock market. After today, some market experts began talking about the "nationalization" of Hong Kong.

HEADLINE: In Clinton's Remarks: A Focus on Interdependence and Forgiveness

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from comments President Clinton made yesterday at Union Chapel in Oak Bluff, Mass., at a ceremony on the 35th anniversary of the "I Have a Dream" speech by the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr.:
The summer of 1963 was a very eventful one for me: the summer I turned 17. What most people know about it now is the famous picture of me shaking hands with President Kennedy in July. It was a great moment. But I think the moment we commemorate today, a moment I experienced all alone, had a more profound impact on my life. 
 Most of us who are old enough remember exactly where we were on Aug. 28, 1963. I was in my living room in Hot Springs, Ark. I remember the chair I was sitting in. I remember exactly where it was in the room. I remember exactly the position of the chair when I sat and watched on national television the great March on Washington unfold.
I remember weeping uncontrollably during Martin Luther King's speech. And I remember thinking, when it was over, my country would never be the same and neither would I.
There are people all across this country who made a more intense commitment to the idea of racial equality and justice that day than they had ever made before. And so in very personal ways, all of us became better and bigger because of the work of those who brought that great day about. There are millions of people who John Lewis will never meet who are better and bigger because of what that day meant.
And the words continue to echo down to the present day, spoken to us today by children who were not even alive then. And, God willing, their grandchildren will also be inspired and moved and become better and bigger because of what happened on that increasingly distant summer day.
What I'd like to ask you to think about a little today, and to share with you -- and I'll try to do it without taking my spectacles out, but I don't write very well and I don't read too well as I get older -- is what I think this means for us today. I was trying to think about what John and Dr. King and others did and how they did it, and how it informs what I do and how I think about other things today.
And I want to ask, you all need to think about three things . . . .
No. 1, Dr. King used to speak about how we were all bound together in a web of mutuality, which was an elegant way of saying, whether we like it or not, we're all in this life together. We are interdependent. Well, what does that mean? Well, let me give you a specific example: We had some good news today. Incomes in America went up 5 percent last year. That's a big bump in a year. We have got the best economy in a generation. That's the good news.
But we are mutually interdependent with people far beyond our borders. Yesterday, there was some more news that was troubling out of Russia, some rumor, some fact about the decline in the economy. Our stock market dropped over 350 points. And in Latin America, our most fast-growing market for American exports, all the markets went down even though, as far as we know, most of those countries are doing everything right. Why? Because we're in a tighter and tighter and tighter web of mutuality.
Asia has these economic troubles. So even though we have got the best economy in a generation, our farm exports to Asia are down 30 percent from last year. And we have states in this country where farmers, the hardest-working people in this country, can't make their mortgage payments because of things that happened half a world away they didn't have any direct influence on at all. This world is being bound together more closely.
So what is the lesson from that? Well, I should go to Russia because, as John said, anybody can come see you when you're doing well. I should go there. And we should tell them that if they'll be strong and do the disciplined, hard things they have to do to reform their country, their economy, and get through this dark night, that we'll stick with them. . . .
The second thing. Even if you're not a pacifist, whenever possible, peace and nonviolence is always the right thing to do.
I remember so vividly in 1994 . . .I was trying to pass this crime bill, and all of the opposition to the crime bill that was in the newspapers, all the intense opposition was coming from the N.R.A. and the others that did not want us to ban assault weapons, didn't believe that we ought to have more community policemen walking the streets, and conservatives who thought we should just punish people more and not spend more money trying to keep kids out of trouble in the first place. And it was a huge fight.
And so they came to see me, and he said, "Well, John Lewis is not going to vote for this bill." And I said, "Why?" and they said, "Because it increases the number of crimes subject to the Federal death penalty and he's not for it. And he's not in bed with all those other people, he thinks they're wrong, but he can't vote for it." And I said, "Well, let him alone. There's no point in calling him" because he's lived a lifetime dedicated to an idea and while I may not be a pacifist, whenever possible, it's always the right thing to do to try to be peaceable and nonviolent.
What does that mean for today? Well, there's a lot of good news. It's like the economy: the crime rate's at a 25-year low, juvenile crime's finally coming down. . . .
Half a world away, terrorists trying to hurt Americans blow up two embassies in Africa, and they killed some of our people, some of our best people -- of, I might add, very many different racial and ethnic backgrounds, American citizens, including a distinguished career African-American diplomat and his son -- but they also killed almost 300 Africans and wounded 5,000 others.
We see their pictures in the morning paper, two of them who did that. We were bringing them home. And they look like active, confident young people. What happened inside them that made them feel so much hatred toward us that they could justify not only an act of violence against innocent diplomats and other public servants, but the collateral consequences to Africans whom they would never know? They had children, too.
So it is always best to remember that we have to try to work for peace in the Middle East, for peace in Northern Ireland, for an end to terrorism, for protections against biological and chemical weapons being used in the first place.
The night before we took action against the terrorist operations in Afghanistan and Sudan, I was here on this island up till 2:30 in the morning trying to make absolutely sure that at that chemical plant there was no night shift. I believed I had to take the action I did, but I didn't want some person who was a nobody to me, but who may have a family to feed and a life to live, and probably had no earthly idea what else was going on there, to die needlessly. I learned that, and it's another reason we ought to pay our debt to the United Nations, because if we can work together, together we can find more peaceful solutions. Now I didn't learn that when I became President; I learned it from John Lewis and the civil rights movement a long time ago.
And the last thing I learned from them on which all these other things depend, without which we cannot build a world of peace or one America in an increasingly peaceful world bound together in this web of mutuality, is that you can't get there unless you're willing to forgive your enemies. I never will forget one of the most -- I don't think I have ever spoken about this in public before -- but one of the most meaningful personal moments I have had as President was a conversation I had with Nelson Mandela.
And I said to him -- I said: "You know, I have read your book, and I have heard you speak. And you spent time with my wife and daughter, and you have talked about inviting your jailers to your inauguration." And I said, "It's very moving." And I said: "You're a shrewd as well as a great man. But come on now, how did you really do that? You can't make me believe you didn't hate those people who did that to you for 27 years?"
He said, "I did hate them for quite a long time. After all, they abused me physically and emotionally. They separated me from my wife, and it eventually broke my family up. They kept me from seeing my children grow up." He said, "For quite a long time, I hated them."
And then he said: "I realized one day, breaking rocks, that they could take everything away from me, everything, but my mind and heart. Now, those things I would have to give away, and I simply decided I would not give them away."
So as you look around the world, you see -- how do you explain these three children who were killed in Ireland or all the people who were killed in the square when the people were told to leave the City Hall, there was a bomb there, and then they walked out toward the bomb?
What about all those families in Africa? I don't know. I can't pick up the telephone and call them and say, "I am so sorry this happened." How do we find that spirit?
All of you know I'm having to become quite an expert in this business of asking for forgiveness. And I ----. It gets a little easier the more you do it. And if you have a family, an Administration, a Congress and a whole country to ask, you're going to get a lot of practice.
But I have to tell that in these last days it has come home to me again, something I first learned as President, but it wasn't burned in my bones -- and that is that in order to get it, you have to be willing to give it. And all of us -- the anger, the resentment, the bitterness, the desire for recrimination against people you believe have wronged you -- they harden the heart and deaden the spirit and lead to self-inflicted wounds.
And so it is important that we are able to forgive those we believe have wronged us, even as we ask for forgiveness from people we have wronged. And I heard that first -- first -- in the civil rights movement. "Love thy neighbor as thyself."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: FOREIGN AFFAIRS;
Beware The Ides Of August

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN; Frank Rich is on vacation.

 BODY:
Which do you think is scarier? Is it that Scott Ritter, the top U.N. weapons inspector in Iraq, quit his job, accusing the U.S. and the U.N. of surrendering to Saddam Hussein? Or is it the story told by Russian economists about a Russian soldier behind the Urals who drove his army tank to City Hall to demand months of back pay? The soldier said he drove the tank not because he wanted to blow up the municipality but because he didn't have any other way to get there and couldn't afford cab fare. Or is it reading The Washington Post, where a State Department spokesman, James Foley, was quoted as saying that Secretary of State Madeleine Albright couldn't possibly be going soft on Iraq because, after all, "Saddam Hussein has called Secretary Albright a snake and a witch, among other things"?
What these disturbing stories suggest is that the basic pillars that have stabilized the post-cold-war world are all either shaking or crumbling. And that's scary. 
 Pillar number one was the notion that Russia had made an irreversible leap from Communism to free-market capitalism, that it was basically on the right track, albeit bumpy, that Boris Yeltsin was basically the right man for the job, and that a little U.S., German and I.M.F. money could grease the way.
Today, financial experts are at a loss as to how to deal with Russia, whose future is anything but certain. Russia's crisis is primarily a governing crisis. Russia lacks the institutions to properly manage a modern economy linked to today's fast-paced global market, and the legitimacy of free markets there is still shallow. If Russia goes bad, it's going to go really fast, and if it gets better, it's going to take a really long time.
The second pillar was America's defeat of Saddam in the gulf war. That defeat resonated around the world. Those who supported Saddam, like Yasir Arafat, had to literally apologize to their neighbors after the war. Those who went on supporting him looked like fools, tilting against a Pax Americana. Arab moderation spurred Israeli moderation. But today the Arab center, not just the extremes, is again reconciling with Saddam. The Clinton team says it has no allies anymore to confront Saddam and no domestic support, and it's right. But neither did George Bush when he built the anti-Iraq coalition.
The third pillar was the American economic model -- that liberal, open, deregulated markets, with lots of free trade, will produce steadily rising stock markets around the world. That model is now facing its biggest challenge across the globe today, where investors and workers in every emerging market are discovering that while the American model may be the best thing going, it is much more difficult to reproduce than first thought.
"American credibility is being tested in every one of these areas," notes Robert Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International, "and the stability of the world will depend, in part, on whether we pass that test."
Which brings us to the fourth shaky pillar, the leaders who made this post-cold-war world. They are vanishing: Yitzhak Rabin was shot. Bill Clinton shot himself. Boris Yeltsin lost his way. Helmut Kohl lost his mandate. Japan is home alone. Who would have predicted that today's most stable leaders would be China's President, Jiang Zemin, and its Prime Minister, Zhu Rongji?
America is a superpower, but it's not omnipotent. It needs to decide where it can make a difference, where it can only coach and where it is just copping out. In Iraq, it is just copping out. In Asia the U.S. can make a difference by encouraging governments there to broaden their social safety nets to catch some of those brutalized and unemployed by the latest economic meltdowns, so this transition to real free-market capitalism is not derailed. In Russia, the U.S., Germany and the I.M.F. can offer aid, but only if the Communist-led Duma passes all the necessary reforms and Russia's greedy capitalist oligarchs are broken.
It's ironic: A strong Russia under Stalin could never bring the West to heel, but a weak Russia under capitalism can, if the West doesn't insulate itself. Which is why Bill Clinton needs to right himself fast, so that he is not just occupying the Presidency but exercising it. America needs a President. The world needs one even more.
Dear reader, this is my last regular column until January. I am taking a four-month sabbatical to finish a book about the post-cold-war world.
