HEADLINE: Judge Deals a Blow To City's Campaign Against Sex Shops

BYLINE: By DAVID ROHDE 

 BODY:
A state judge dealt a blow yesterday to the Giuliani administration's efforts to curtail the sex industry, ruling that Show World, a midtown Manhattan pornography emporium, and two other adult video stores could stay in business as long as no more than 40 percent of their floor space and inventory were dedicated to adult entertainment.
The ruling by Judge Stephen G. Crane of State Supreme Court in Manhattan breaks a long-running series of legal victories on the issue for the administration and suggests that Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani may achieve only a muted victory in his crackdown on sex shops. The city said it was considering appealing Judge Crane's ruling, and it vowed to continue trying to shut sex shops. 
 While several strip clubs have already been shut down under the new law, yesterday's court ruling indicates that some adult video stores may be able to remain open by selling nonsexual merchandise in their front portions and constructing back rooms for adult entertainment.
In trying to comply with the new zoning restrictions, Show World, a 20-year-old, three-story business on Eighth Avenue between 42d and 43d Streets, took advantage of vague language in the new law that defines an adult establishment as one devoting a "substantial portion" of its stock or floor space to adult entertainment.
Citing the City Department of Buildings's interpretation of "substantial portion" as more than 40 percent, Show World and other video stores replaced more than 60 percent of their "triple X" videos, "peep-a-live" booths and other sex merchandise with luggage, cameras, N.Y.P.D. T-shirts and classic films.
Lawyers for the city argued that the changes were a subterfuge by the stores to evade the new law, which bars adult video stores and strip clubs from operating in residential areas or within 500 feet of a school, house of worship or another sex shop.
But in his opinion Judge Crane ruled that the changes made by Show World and the two other businesses, NRS Video and Les Hommes, were substantial and had made them legally "nonadult businesses."
It remains unclear whether sex shops that eliminate more than 60 percent of their lucrative pornographic merchandise can remain economically viable, particularly in high-rent areas like Times Square, a city official, speaking on condition of anonymity, said. "It's not just the zoning regulation that's at play here," the official said. "There are the forces of the marketplace. We'll see how long businesses will be able to be economically viable."
But Herald Price Fahringer, the lawyer representing 107 of the 144 sex shops affected by the law, said yesterday that he believed that the profit margins of the shops might shrink if they eliminated most of their sex-related inventory, but predicted that many would remain open.
Scott Wexler, Show World's controller, said yesterday that the business had been forced to end all strip shows and lay off 20 people, including the dancers, since changing its inventory. "We're not operating in the red, but our bottom line has definitely been hurt," he said. "We're confident we'll be able to keep going."
For several reasons, Show World appears to be in a better position than smaller adult businesses. Show World's owner, Richard Basciano, owns the two 12-story buildings Show World is located in, and therefore has little to fear in terms of rising rents. The buildings have 50 other commercial tenants and 24 residential tenants to pay the $500,000-a-year property tax bill, Mr. Wexler said.
The owners of NRS Video, at 414 Eighth Avenue near 31st Street, did not return a call seeking comment. Les Hommes, at 217 West 80th Street near Broadway, does not have a listed telephone number.
Steven M. Fishner, the Mayor's criminal justice coordinator, said the ruling would not affect the city's enforcement of the law.
"To date, 13 illegal establishments are closed due to court orders and more than 20 others have voluntarily closed their doors," he said. "Judge Crane's decision applies to today's case only, and it does not present a legal impediment to future enforcement of the zoning law."
In his 23-page ruling, Judge Crane said the law explicitly mentioned only two criteria for judging whether a business is "adult," stock and floor space. He rejected the city's attempt to consider other issues like revenue. He said that having less than 40 percent of floor space and stock devoted to adult material did not automatically make a business "nonadult." But, citing such factors as Show World's investment of more than $100,000 in renovations, he concluded that the store appeared to have made good-faith efforts to comply. His decision suggests that if a video store makes major, permanent renovations to abide by the law, it may be able to remain open.
Judge Crane's ruling also suggested that the city's decision in these cases not to follow the typical procedure for closing businesses violating zoning laws may have backfired.
City officials who spoke on the condition of anonymity said that in most zoning violation cases, the city first informs a business that it is violating zoning laws and then discusses with it how it might comply. Mr. Fahringer said in court earlier this week that he had offered to meet with city officials after the law took effect on July 22 to discuss how the shops could comply with the new law, but was rebuffed.
Instead, city officials asked judges to padlock strip clubs and adult video stores under the city's nuisance abatement law -- the process normally used to close operations like crack houses. In his ruling, Judge Crane said the city must meet a higher standard of proof when trying to close stores under the nuisance abatement law than under the zoning law. He called the city's failure to respond to Mr. Fahringer's offer "unfortunate."
But a city official who spoke on the condition of anonymity said that using the more aggressive legal approach was appropriate, and vowed it would continue.

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: IN WASHINGTON;
Gingrich Opposes Summit, but President Vows to Press On

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Speaker of the House Newt Gingrich said today that President Clinton's plans to visit Moscow next week to meet the faltering President Boris N. Yeltsin were "imprudent" and should be canceled.
While some prominent legislators from both parties urged Mr. Clinton to proceed with the summit meeting, Mr. Gingrich added his own influential voice to those who have criticized the meeting, which comes in the midst of economic and political upheaval in Moscow. 
 "These are two weak Presidents trying to prop each other up," Mr. Gingrich said in a telephone interview today. "Who knows what kind of promises they'll make to each other, what they'll agree to? I think it's very imprudent for him to go."
But Mr. Clinton was undeterred by such criticism. In a speech on Martha's Vineyard about the importance of friendship in difficult times, he vowed to press ahead with the meeting and with an effort to help Russia if the new Government does "the disciplined, hard things they have to do to reform their country."
Defending his trip, Mr. Clinton said, "Anybody can come see you when you're doing well." He delivered his remarks shortly after Soviet television broadcast an interview in which a puffy Mr. Yeltsin announced that he would not resign.
Mr. Clinton received support for the summit meeting from Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, the New York Democrat, who said the India-Pakistan nuclear confrontation was reason enough for Mr. Clinton to go to Moscow. The Russians can help with the Indians, he said, "and we've not had that conversation with the Russians." A mutual collaboration to make South Asia stable "would cost a lot of money, but it's worth the world," he said. "That alone would be a good morning's discussion."
Senator John McCain, Republican of Arizona, also said that Mr. Clinton is right to go. "To cancel now would be a further blow to what little credibility Yeltsin has remaining," he said. "The President should obviously tell Yeltsin to get a grip on his country and recognize that we can't pour good money after bad without evidence of reforms."
Representative Lee H. Hamilton, Democrat of Indiana, said Mr. Clinton should stress the American desire for a prosperous Russia, fully integrated in the world. "There is a sour mood about Russia in Congress today, principally because of their missile and nuclear help to Iran," Mr. Hamilton said. "We have to engage Russia and build bridges when possible, but also draw red lines about acceptable behavior."
In a theme echoed today by his national security adviser, Samuel R. Berger, the President said the United States had to stand with the Russian people in their time of difficulty and be willing to help if the Russians themselves made the hard sacrifices required to get their house in order.
As Mr. Berger put it, "Although the key political and economic choices are up to the Russian people themselves, we need to engage actively, even -- or especially -- in a time of crisis."
Mr. Berger said that Mr. Clinton would urge Russians to hold fast "to the basic objectives of democracy and economic reform in the face of new challenges."
"The Russian people should know that, particularly in times of difficulty, the United States and the West will not turn away from cooperation," Mr. Berger said. "As they do the things necessary to restore stability and progress, we are prepared to support them."
Mr. Clinton's officials, struggling with themselves to prepare for a summit meeting in a land undergoing a political and economic earthquake, had encouraging news today.
The acting Prime Minister, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, told Clinton Administration officials privately that he has made no deal with the Russian Parliament that will undermine the general course of economic reform, senior American officials said.
Although members of Mr. Chernomyrdin's own party are saying "worrisome things" about a more Soviet-style state control over parts of the economy, one official said, Mr. Chernomyrdin "tells us he hasn't struck any deal on the policy stuff." At least not yet, the official acknowledged.
He spoke after a half-hour conference call with Mr. Clinton, Deputy Secretary of State Strobe Talbott, who is in Moscow, and Deputy Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers. Mr. Talbott emphasized that Mr. Yeltsin would not resign, that Russian officials of all stripes wanted Mr. Clinton to make the trip, and that the streets themselves were calm.
"They're not sacking kiosks to get crackers," the official said. "It's not like Clinton's walking into a maelstrom."
Mr. Chernomyrdin has been negotiating with parliamentary leaders, including some from the Communist Party, on a broad economic and political strategy for recovery. Mr. Chernomyrdin, who controls Parliament's second-largest faction, needs wider support to be confirmed as Prime Minister in a vote expected on Monday, as Mr. Clinton leaves Washington for Moscow.
But Mr. Chernomyrdin also needs Parliament to pass legislation to reform the tax and pension systems, at least if Moscow expects the next $4.3 billion installment of aid from the International Monetary Fund.
Senior officials said they remained deeply concerned that the Communists would demand a high price, and that Mr. Chernomyrdin may have to give some ground to the call for stricter state controls on the economy and the printing of more rubles to stimulate industry and pay workers, driving up inflation and further undermining the value of the ruble.
"Chernomyrdin can go pretty far toward the Communists," said another senior official. "We understand that he needs to stabilize the situation in terms of public reaction. He needs a coalition. That's the concern: he needs their support and they'll make him pay for it. But he's a lot smarter than they are."
Still, the official said, Washington had no real options. "We're prepared to be generous in our interpretation of what Chernomyrdin can put together," he said. "But frankly, we don't have any choice. We don't have any alternatives, nor is anybody asking us right now."
Mr. Berger conceded that Mr. Yeltsin's firing today of the reformers Anatoly B. Chubais and Boris Nemtsov was "not encouraging," but he continued to emphasize that the policies of any new Government mattered more than its personnel.
Mr. Berger and other officials continued to press their theme of fundamental Russian responsibility for the country's troubles and for their solutions. But Mr. Summers also emphasized that the I.M.F. would be prepared to be flexible, but dependent on the pace of reform. "That has to be put in the context of Russian economic policy, which is what we're waiting to see," Mr. Summers said. "And at this point, we don't have a Russian economic team, we don't have a Russian economic plan."
And Mr. Berger said important foreign policy and security issues could still be discussed, including nuclear stockpiles, the protection of fissile material, and Russia's policies toward Iraq, Iran and the conflict in the Serbian province of Kosovo.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
GLUT FORCES CHIP-EQUIPMENT MAKER TO CUT STAFF BY 20%

BYLINE: Dow Jones 

 BODY:
Ultratech Stepper Inc., a maker of semiconductor-manufacturing equipment based in San Jose, Calif., said yesterday that it had cut 120 full-time positions, or about 20 percent of its work force, in a move to reduce costs. The company also said it would require all employees to take two days off a month for the rest of the year. Ultratech linked the cuts to a worldwide glut of computer memory chips and uncertainty over Asia's financial turmoil, which have injured its operations. Costs associated with the moves will lower earnings for the third quarter ending Sept. 30, Ultratech said. In the quarter a year ago, Ultratech earned $5.4 million, or 25 cents a share, on sales of $36.8 million.

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: THE LENDER;
I.M.F. Chief Endorses Chernomyrdin's Goals

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The managing director of the International Monetary Fund, Michel Camdessus, threw his support today behind what he described as the attempts of Russia's acting Prime Minister, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, to resurrect an austerity plan for Russia.
"He accepts the challenge," Mr. Camdessus said, in his first public comment after a hastily arranged meeting with Mr. Chernomyrdin in Ukraine on Wednesday night. "Mr. Chernomyrdin has told me that he recognizes the validity" of the monetary fund's program for restoring the Russian economy. 
Mr. Camdessus and his chief deputy, Stanley Fischer, also suggested, at a news conference at the fund's headquarters here, that if the Russians failed to embrace the fund's approach, it would be fruitless for the fund or the major industrial nations to lend more money in an attempt to support the ruble and the Russian economy.
When the ruble began to slide in value earlier this month, "we had to decide how much further to go, once it was clear" that foreign lenders would no longer roll over short-term loans to Russia, Mr. Fischer said.
"The I.M.F. and its major shareholder decided that it would take more resources than were available," he said. The fund's major shareholder is the United States.
Mr. Camdessus's remarks seemed clearly aimed at giving Mr. Chernomyrdin public backing as the Prime Minister tries to persuade a recalcitrant Parliament to reject "populist" inflationary policies in favor of the conditions that the monetary fund has attached to a recent loan agreement. The fund is pushing Russia to move to a balanced budget, restrain borrowing and greatly improve its tax collections.
President Boris N. Yeltsin agreed to those terms last month and in return received $4.8 billion in loans from the I.M.F. to support the ruble. Another $4.3 billion loan was promised for late September, and it will be forthcoming, Mr. Camdessus said today, but only if the Russians show progress toward meeting the fund's conditions for the credits.
Mr. Chernomyrdin "indicated that he understood the severity" of the financial crisis in his country, Mr. Camdessus said, "and that the Russian Government was prepared to take the needed action in close consultation with the political parties and Parliament."
A political uproar over the monetary fund's terms played a role in Mr. Yeltsin's decision to dismiss his reformist Prime Minister, Sergei V. Kiriyenko, last Sunday and reappoint the more centrist Mr. Chernomyrdin, who had been the previous Prime Minister.
Mr. Camdessus and Mr. Fischer said they were operating in concert with the seven major industrial countries -- known as the Group of Seven, or G-7 -- that are the fund's major contributors.
Mr. Fischer was quoted in German and British newspapers in recent days as having criticized those nations for failing to provide additional money for Russia as several of them, particularly the United States, had for Mexico in 1994.
But today he suggested that he had been misquoted. "There was no disagreement between the fund managers and the G-7," Mr. Fischer said, "that the resources needed were more than were available to deal with the problem."
In a separate effort to reassure the financial markets, this time about Latin America, Mr. Camdessus announced that the monetary fund had invited the finance ministers and central bank chiefs of the Western Hemisphere to a meeting here late next week. The meeting, the first of its kind, will try to organize "regional surveillance," in which countries help each other maintain market-friendly economic policies.
Acknowledging that the fund's available money, depleted by the Asian and Russian crises, was unusually low, Mr. Camdessus insisted that the I.M.F. would not cut its assistance programs. The remark seemed aimed at getting member countries to come through with an $85 billion infusion -- including $18 billion from the United States, an amount that the Clinton Administration favors, but many in Congress oppose.
"If at some point we run out of resources," Mr. Camdessus said, "the international community will have to face its responsibilities and decide whether countries that are helping themselves should be abandoned by the international community."
The fund now has in the bank only about $13 billion not already committed to lending programs or to other commitments, fund officials say, one of the lowest levels in its history. The $13 billion does not include about $10 billion already earmarked for Russia but not yet disbursed.
The $4.3 billion loan scheduled for late September would come out of that $10 billion. That would leave $5.7 billion for future lending. Since 1992, after the collapse of the Soviet Union, Russia has received a total of $21 billion in loans from the monetary fund and has made repayments on time, fund officials say.

HEADLINE: Let Russia Slow Down Its Reforms

 BODY:

To the Editor:
You urge President Clinton to make it clear to Russian leaders at next week's summit meeting "that moving forward with economic reforms is the best prescription both for healing Russia and for maintaining strong American support" (editorial, Aug. 25). But 
 President Boris N. Yeltsin hasn't pursued any "economic reforms."
Mr. Yeltsin has done everything to keep himself in power. He has used the immense prerogatives of his office to let industries collapse and give his magnate allies a free hand to avoid taxes, privatize huge properties and invest profits abroad. He has lowered inflation by not paying millions of workers and driving up the number of starving and unemployed.
The West must disown Boris Yeltsin and help Russia find a way to saner economic policies and a more truly representative form of government.
 
ABRAHAM BRUMBERG
Chevy Chase, Md., Aug. 25, 1998

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: THE EUROPEANS;
Russia Told to Implement Tough I.M.F. Reform Plan

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The Finance Ministers of four economically powerful Western European countries warned Moscow today that the only way out of its current crisis was to make the tough economic changes long urged by the International Monetary Fund.
A letter by the four ministers -- from Germany, France, Italy and Britain --to Russia's acting Prime Minister, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, said those steps included enforcement of tax laws that were now widely ignored, and a monetary policy aimed at keeping inflation in check. 
 None of the countries offered Russia any emergency bilateral assistance, emphasizing instead that the I.M.F. would be the lead agency for dealing with the crisis.
The letter from the four European members of the Group of Seven leading industrial democracies was part of a concerted European effort to urge Mr. Chernomyrdin not to give in to pressure from the Communist opposition in Russia and drop unpopular economic programs.
"A return of confidence in financial markets will be possible only if Russia proceeds with its reform program in close relationship with the International Monetary Fund," a statement on the letter issued by the French Finance Ministry said.
Prime Minister Tony Blair of Britain spoke on Thursday with President Clinton, who plans to visit Russia next week, and officials in London said they had agreed on what Russia needed to do.
"The program and plan that has been put together over a long period of time by the international institutions does genuinely offer the only way out of this," Mr. Blair said during a visit to Scotland.
A high-ranking French official, however, said that the United States-Russian summit meeting would probably be "useless."
President Clinton is so weakened by the admission of having had an intimate relationship with a White House intern and Mr. Yeltsin is so stricken by Russia's economic and political turmoil that neither seems able to agree on a solution to the crisis, the official, who refused to be identified, said.
The German Finance Minister, Theo Waigel, said in Munich today, "Russia needs, like any other country, to work closely with international financial institutions, but Russia must solve its own problems."
He said that Germany and France would work together to try to help, but stressed that bilateral financial aid to Moscow from Germany, which has already lent or donated tens of billions of dollars in gratitude for Russia's agreement to its unification in 1990, was "out of the question."
As news accounts from Russia indicated that Mr. Chernomyrdin may be preparing to strengthen state control of the economy and let inflation run, authorities in Bonn and Paris expressed continuing concern about economic and political developments in Moscow.
"The finance ministers agree it is vital to strengthen the foundations of public finances with a fair tax system and to adopt measures to restore the stability of the financial system," the German Finance Ministry said. "It is also decisive that the Russian central bank is able to pursue an independent, stability-oriented monetary policy."
Mr. Waigel told the German financial daily Handelsblatt: "Turning up the printing presses is never a wise solution. Inflation is no way to escape the financial crisis. There is no way around rapid implementation of structural reforms."
The French Finance Minister, Dominique Strauss-Kahn, said: "It is clear that the solution is in the hands of the Russian Government. We appreciate that financial assistance from us is necessary, but not sufficient. In particular, the Russian Government has to put fiscal reforms in place."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The German Finance Minister, Theo Waigel, spoke at a party meeting yesterday in Munich. He has urged Russia to take harsh economic steps. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE POLICY MAKERS;
Fed's Focus Is Said to Shift To Possibility of a Rate Cut

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: JACKSON HOLE, Wyo., Aug. 28

 BODY:
The latest round of global financial turmoil is forcing the Federal Reserve to shift its sights from guarding against inflation through higher interest rates to the possibility that it might have to reduce rates to head off an economic downturn, central bank officials and economists said today.
Although it has left rates unchanged for nearly a year and a half, the Fed has consistently seen inflation, stemming from strong growth and low unemployment, as a greater threat than recession at home or a financial meltdown around the world. But the stock market's slide and the continued deterioration of most developing economies -- both of which accelerated this week amid Russia's crisis -- mean that the Fed will almost certainly not raise rates anytime soon and may seriously debate cutting rates in coming months, officials and economists said. 
 Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, made no substantive statements about the markets or monetary policy at a presentation here today on income inequality. But Fed officials, as well as economists who held informal discussions with them here during a symposium sponsored by the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, said recent events would inevitably reshape the debate in the Fed's policy-making panel.
One indication of the change in thinking came from William Poole, the president of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, who earlier this year was pushing for a rate increase. At the beginning of July, Mr. Poole dropped his call for a rate increase, and he told reporters here today that the best option for the Fed now was to wait and see how much the domestic economy was being hurt by what was happening around the world.
"Given what's been going on in the markets, there is more reason than before to wait and see how things play out," Mr. Poole told the Reuters news service.
Economists said, though, that any backing away from raising rates would still leave the Fed a long way from cutting them. "Clearly the events of the past few days make easing by the end of the year a possibility, though I wouldn't put it higher than 50 percent," said David Hale, chief economist at Scudder Kemper Investments.
Alan Blinder, a former Fed vice chairman, said that the fallout from the situation in Russia "can only push the spectrum of the debate in the dovish direction," but he said the most likely course would be for the Fed to hold rates steady unless Wall Street was plunged into a crisis.
As recently as its policy-making meeting on July 1, most Fed officials "felt that the risks continued to point on balance toward rising inflation," according to minutes of the meeting released last week. At that meeting, the Fed voted to keep a formal policy tilt toward raising rates. Minutes of the Fed's most recent meeting, on Aug. 18, will be released after the next Fed meeting, on Sept. 29.
But with conditions worsening, some Fed officials suggested that their assessments of the outlook were changing. The selloff in the stock market, they said, could be expected to help rein in demand as consumers grew less confident.
"Financial-market weakness will only underscore expectations of weakened growth and reassure policy makers of the remoteness of inflation as a risk," said John Lipsky, chief economist at Chase Manhattan Bank.
Moreover, the market reaction to Russia's woes has effectively brought about a severe tightening of credit conditions for nearly all developing nations, as investors are demanding sharply higher premiums over safe investments like United States Treasury bonds before they put their money just about anywhere outside the United States and Western Europe. Many investors suffered heavy losses in Russia and are wary of taking another hit.
The lack of credit and investment capital, as well as the continued turmoil in the markets, could bring a further slowdown to regions like South America and put further pressure on important economies like that of Hong Kong, where officials are battling a sharp slide in the stock market and pressures for a devaluation of the currency.
The implications for the United States of mounting problems in other countries, even apart from the effect the turmoil is having on the financial markets, could be considerable. Mexico and South America together, for example, account for nearly 20 percent of American trade, and a downturn there could have a pronounced impact on American jobs and corporate profits.
But Fed officials said they were by no means convinced that the economy would stall or even slow to a noninflationary pace. Although growth slowed to 1.6 percent in the second quarter after surging to a 5.4 percent annual rate in the first quarter, one Fed official noted that the average for the first half, at 3.4 percent, was still quite strong.
With unemployment remaining extremely low, there are also tentative signs that wage increases are beginning to bite into corporate profits, putting pressure on companies to raise prices and set off inflation.
There was widespread agreement among the experts here that one thing American monetary policy could not do was help Russia in any meaningful way. The loss of confidence in Russia among international investors is so profound that it will take wholesale changes there to woo them back, in this view.
E. Gerald Corrigan, a managing director of Goldman, Sachs & Company, said Russia's best course would be to make up for its mistake in unilaterally limiting debt repayments and try to negotiate with its creditors as a first step toward restoring stability and credibility.
"Russia and Russia alone must take the first steps to get this process back on track," Mr. Corrigan said.
But other economists said a rate cut could help economies like Hong Kong's, where the currency is tied directly to the value of the dollar and would therefore benefit from any reduction in dollar interest rates.

HEADLINE: Let Russia Slow Down Its Reforms

 BODY:

To the Editor:
President Boris N. Yeltsin's dismissal of the new pro-reform Government and the reappointment of Viktor S. Chernomyrdin as Prime Minister, whose ties to the industrial oligarchy are well established, point to yet another old aspect of Russian history (front page, Aug. 24). 
 The young reformers Sergei V. Kiriyenko and Boris Nemtsov, who are Jewish, have been sacked.
In times of crisis Russia returns to its historically unifying principles of nationalism and xenophobia and finds Jews or foreigners to blame for its failures. Alas, Russia is again on the brink of political and economic convulsions.
 
SUSAN GOLD
New York, Aug. 24, 1998
 
The writer is with the Harriman Institute at Columbia University.

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE BANKS;
BankAmerica and 2 Other Big Banks Suffer Russia Losses

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY L. O'BRIEN 

 BODY:
Adding to the list of American companies battered by economic turmoil in Russia, three more large banks reported trading losses there, but provided mixed signals about whether financial downturns elsewhere might lead to more widespread losses.
As the Russian crisis deepens, volatility in more economically significant countries like Brazil and Argentina is also eating into the profits of some financial concerns doing business there. Indeed, some analysts say there is now ample evidence that the current raft of financial problems extends far beyond Russia's toppling economy. 
 "That's the most important issue -- that markets are starting to overrun governments' abilities to contain them in many parts of the world -- and nobody wants to talk about them because it's a confidence game of sorts," said Charles Peabody, an analyst with Mitchell Securities Inc. in New York. "Banks and businesses don't want to create uncertainty that causes people not to repay one another."
Still, some analysts said they have yet to see signs indicating that serious losses will appear elsewhere in the world.
"The market's concern and what Moody's is concerned about is exposures in places like Latin America," said Gregory Bauer, a managing director and banking analyst with Moody's Investors Service in New York. "The banks are well positioned now to deal with potential losses."
Nonetheless, the debris from trading problems is piling up.
BankAmerica, the fifth-largest bank in the United States, said yesterday that it had trading losses of $220 million on a pretax basis over the last two months, largely as a result of soured trades in Russia. Given reports earlier this week of other companies' losses in Russia, many analysts and investors had been bracing themselves for BankAmerica to report a similar loss, so the figure did not come as a complete surprise.
But BankBoston, the 17th-largest bank in the country, announced a $30 million pretax trading loss, only $10 million of which was related to Russia. Most of the rest of the figure was a result of trading losses in Brazil and Argentina, according to the bank.
Although BankBoston is not in the top tier of large American banks doing business abroad, it has one of the oldest and largest United States banking franchises in South America and is something of a proxy for conditions there. And the announcement of its trading losses is the first solid indication this week that American companies may start to accumulate losses in that region.
Yet J. P. Morgan -- a more important bellwether for the well-being of emerging markets than BankBoston -- indicated yesterday that it might be faring well in South America and elsewhere, even as it too suffers trading losses in Russia.
Morgan, the nation's fourth-largest bank, announced yesterday that its exposure in Russia was $160 million, well below the exposure of $400 million that analysts had estimated the bank had. A Morgan spokesman said the $160 million figure was the result of trading losses the bank incurred in Russia, but declined to specify the size of the losses or what the bank's exposure was before the losses occurred.
Morgan said that its trading revenue so far in the current quarter was $300 million, which includes "losses from write-downs of Russian trading assets, partially offset by gains in other emerging markets, and lower revenues from trading activities in developed markets." Morgan's trading revenue is on track to fall well short of the $920 million it hauled in last quarter.
Yesterday's news followed disclosures of big Russian trading losses this week by Credit Suisse First Boston, the Republic New York Corporation, and the speculator George Soros's group of hedge funds.
Investors continued to punish bank stocks yesterday. Shares of BankAmerica fell $4.125, or 5.6 percent, to $69.625, while BankBoston's stock dropped $3, or 7.7 percent, to $36, a new 52-week low. J. P. Morgan, which released its statement after the stock market closed, saw its shares beaten down 6.7 percent, to $97.75. Among other banking stocks, Bankers Trust dropped 5.3 percent, to $79.375; Citicorp fell 4 percent, to $117.125, and Chase Manhattan fell 2.8 percent, to $56.50.
Large American banks have relatively little exposure in Russia given their size -- about $7.68 billion, according to Brown Brothers Harriman & Company. By comparison, the largest American banks have $25.7 billion in exposure to Brazil, $17.3 billion to Mexico, and $16.2 billion to Argentina, according to Brown Brothers. And in Latin America as a whole, the exposure is a whopping $98.9 billion.
Some analysts noted that even if banks face mounting losses around the globe, they are not the same companies that were brought to their knees a decade ago by shoddy lending practices to foreign governments and domestic real estate operators.
"Back in the 80's, these banks had less equity, less reserves and less earnings power than they do now," said James McDermott, chief executive of Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc., the bank research firm in New York. "Banks now have much more ability to withstand pain. They may be exposed to earnings shortfalls, but this is not a large, systemic economic threat."
BankAmerica said its exposure in Russia had been reduced to $100 million from $412 million on June 30, primarily because of the trading setbacks. BankAmerica's Moscow operation has been plagued by large accounting snafus. Recently, the bank was forced by what it described as an accounting error to restate its exposure in Russia at the end of the second quarter to $412 million from $668 million.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1535 words

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE STUMBLING GIANT;
A Lot of Talk About Russia, but the Numbers Shout Japan

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF and SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
One statistic stands out to explain the importance of Japan, along with its misery:
When trading ended today at the Tokyo Stock Exchange, Japanese stocks this week alone had dropped in value by $241 billion -- a sum that exceeds the size of the entire annual output of the Russian economy at present exchange rates. In other words, in one glum week's work, Japan managed the financial equivalent of erasing Russia from the world map for a year. 
 Japan is so monumental as to be critical to the prospects of the international economy, but for all its tremendous underlying strengths it is for now an economic mess -- one incomparably larger than Russia's.
A month after Prime Minister Keizo Obuchi took office, vowing "to deal with each issue in a dramatic, speedy way," Japan remains firmly in recession and largely paralyzed politically and economically.
The stock market fell 3.5 percent today to its lowest level since 1986, while long-term bond yields tumbled to 1.07 percent. They are the lowest long-term interest rates in recorded history, having recently fallen below the previous record low rate of 1.125 percent offered by the Italian city-state of Genoa in 1619.
The markets are tumbling because for all Mr. Obuchi's pledges, Japan is in even greater political gridlock than usual. Mr. Obuchi's ruling Liberal Democratic Party has never been described as energetic, except by itself, but these days even its modest plans to revive the banking system are being blocked by opposition parties in the Diet, or Parliament.
The upshot is that the six bills that represent the centerpiece of Mr. Obuchi's plans to revive the economy remain stuck in numbing Diet hearings. Officials acknowledge that it will be difficult to win passage of the bills by the time Mr. Obuchi meets President Clinton for a mini-summit meeting in New York planned for around Sept. 21, and the delays and stalemate are further dispiriting the markets here and around the world.
In short, expectations of Mr. Obuchi were low, and he has met them.
"In the long term, the Japanese situation is in some ways even worse than the Russian situation, particularly given the size of the Japanese economy and the seeming inability to take really serious action," said M. Y. Yoshino, a Harvard Business School professor who is an expert on Japan and is now visiting Tokyo.
Professor Yoshino noted that for all the vast scale of the bad bank debts in Japan -- perhaps five times the size of the entire Russian financial market before its collapse -- there is little move toward dramatic restructuring to restore economic growth.
A banking analyst at ING Barings Securities Japan Ltd. in Tokyo, James Fiorillo, penned a bit of verse that captures the mood here. It begins:
'Twas the night before crisis
And behind the Diet border
Not a creature was stirring,
To restore financial order.
Mr. Obuchi and his aides have been defending their conduct, but the defenses have only added to the impression that the Government does not have the same sense of urgency as the rest of the world.
Today, for example, Mr. Obuchi was scolded in Parliament by a prominent economist, Yoshio Suzuki, who accused the Government of adopting economic measures that were -- he switched to English -- "too little, too late."
Mr. Obuchi, who by all accounts is an exceptionally friendly and genial man, grinned and replied that if anything, his efforts had been -- here he switched to English -- "too much, too fast."
Finance Minister Kiichi Miyazawa told reporters after stocks tumbled today: "This isn't something to panic about. With the passage of time, the market will stabilize again."
Perhaps Mr. Miyazawa and Mr. Obuchi are trying to reassure the public, but to some it comes across as complacency. Officials have been reassuring ever since Japan's "bubble economy" burst in about 1991, and yet declining asset values have steadily erased more than $5 trillion in wealth, equivalent to wiping out half the entire value of all the stocks on the New York Stock Exchange.
"Right now, this is the worst scenario," warned Masaru Takagi, an economist at Meiji University, referring to the plodding approach by the Government and the stalemate in Parliament. Professor Takagi estimated that there is a 50 percent chance the Obuchi Government will collapse and be forced to call fresh elections, delaying any further moves to revive Japan's economy.
"There is no time to waste, so we must avoid a general election this year," he said.
Opposition parties, however, seeing blood in the water, are circling eagerly and insisting that this is just the time for general elections to break the deadlock, instead of waiting for two more years until elections are due.
"General elections should come fast," said Mr. Suzuki, the economist, who is now a member of Parliament for the opposition Liberal Party. "We're now in a standstill, and in that case no bills may pass the Diet, and economic and financial crises may occur."
Paradoxically, Mr. Obuchi's Government is weak in part because of constant complaining by critics at home and abroad, especially in the United States Treasury Department, who want Japan to act far more decisively to address its economic problems. But the criticism has so weakened the Government that it is less able than ever to push through economic measures.
A weakened Japan is a global problem because it exacerbates the Asian crisis, and because the plummeting yen has been squeezing China, South Korea and other countries. President Jiang Zemin of China this week appealed again to Japan to do more to help its economy, and many experts fear that China may eventually be pressured to devalue its currency, setting off a new round of devaluations and financial crises around Asia.
All that has added to the demands on Mr. Obuchi to be more aggressive in tackling Japan's problems.
"Some substantial initiative needs to be taken by the Government," said Takeshi Sasaki, a political scientist at Tokyo University. "It is the responsibility of the Government, or it can be the end of the Obuchi Government."
The opposition usually does not matter much in the Japanese Parliament, but the main opposition leader, Naoto Kan, has crafted a broad and relatively unified front that has blocked the Government's proposals and offered its own. Some economists think Mr. Kan's financial plan is better than the Government's, because it insists on more bank disclosure, but there are also criticisms that Mr. Kan is playing politics with Japan's economic recovery.
"The opposition sees a chance to force the Government down, even if it happens to bring the economy down," said Richard Jerram, an economist at ING Barings Securities Japan Ltd. "Politicians are oblivious to things in the real world, it's pretty clear."
Mr. Obuchi clearly wants to have the bills passed by the time he meets Mr. Clinton, and one Cabinet official said the Prime Minister would meet opposition leaders soon to work out a compromise. The fall in global markets may increase the pressure on both sides to work out a deal.
"If we were not a parliamentary democracy, of course we would be able to make progress on many issues, but that's not the case," said Akitaka Saiki, a spokesman for Mr. Obuchi. "If you call this paralysis, that could be one reason."
Asked if he thought the West was too impatient for results, considering that Mr. Obuchi has been in office for only one month, Mr. Saiki scarcely paused.
"Not just the West," he said glumly. "Everyone is a bit impatient."
Mr. Obuchi has taken some steps that are significant, even if they are less than his critics seek. He froze a fiscal austerity law, laying the groundwork for continued fiscal stimulus packages, and he prepared new budget guidelines and convened an economic advisory panel with coming up with creative ways to revive the economy.
Yet one indication of Mr. Obuchi's weakness and the perception that he will not survive long is that he has had trouble recruiting people. Sadako Ogata, the highly regarded United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, turned down an invitation to be foreign minister, one official said, and several prominent business leaders reportedly declined to serve on the advisory panel.
Whatever happens in Parliament, the situation is not entirely bleak. Above all, before Mr. Obuchi took office the Parliament had already passed a huge stimulus package, which will trickle through the economy this fall and generate some growth.
Though almost no one expects Mr. Obuchi to dramatically liberalize the economy, the stimulus itself and some associated tax cuts will lend a healthy boost.
Moreover, much of Japan's economic problem arises from a nearly universal gloom that makes everyone reluctant to buy or invest. That psychology is impossible to predict, but some optimists believe that the fiscal stimulus could restore growth, change the public mood, and bring the economy to life. That, if it happened, should revive the property and stock markets, and many bad loans would become good again.
"The pessimism," said Richard Koo, chief economist at Nomura Research Institute in Tokyo, "has been going too far."

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: THE SUMMIT MEETING;
Frazzled Clinton Team Set To Fly Into Heart of Storm

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The scramble was on again today among President Clinton's already seriously scrambled aides.
Three days before they are scheduled to leave for a summit meeting in Moscow, senior Administration officials wondered what Russians they would wind up facing across conference tables. Mr. Clinton's aides began rewriting his planned remarks on the future of United States-Russian relations. 
 "You've got an economy collapsing and a President on the verge of resignation, and we're about to go there for a summit," one Administration official said this afternoon, referring to Russia and Boris N. Yeltsin. "Of course they've been going crazy at the White House."
Samuel R. Berger, the President's national security adviser, gave a more measured assessment: "Well, I think that, obviously, one needs to make adjustments as the situation changes."
While Presidential trips abroad generally have the subtlety and delicacy of hurricanes, they are planned with the unstinting fussiness of Chinese operas.
The thick book laying out the President's schedule times his movements down to what minute he will enter which elevator past the startled maintenance staff in what dank basement (unless Mr. Clinton rewrites the official schedule as he goes).
Before the President went to Africa last spring, aides were dispatched as much as a month earlier to haggle over access and camera angles. With prompting from the Administration, Botswana even rushed a new cellular telephone system into service just ahead of Air Force One's arrival.
But this time, the only thing that some weary Presidential advisers seemed certain of was that the news media would blame the White House for Russian turmoil.
"It's our fault," one Administration official said, his sarcasm matched only by his exasperation. "If we'd been focused we would have improved the Russian tax collections system. Why argue with you guys anymore? Terrorism? Our fault."
The basic elements of the trip are unlikely to change, several officials said today. The prechewed matter dispensed on Presidential visits was prepared during weeks of talks. The Presidents will issue joint statements on weapons proliferation, Kosovo, Caspian oil reserves and the Persian Gulf. And Mr. Clinton's major stops -- including a joint news conference with Mr. Yeltsin, a speech, and a visit to a school -- are unlikely to be adjusted.
But the all-important tone of the trip, along with some of the President's meetings and his precise words, may be debated right up until he lands in Moscow on Tuesday morning -- or even longer.
"What the President says in public, where he goes, whom he meets with -- that's more of what we're reviewing right now," an Administration official said. "You want to project confidence and our ability to get the job done, but the fact is it's fluid."
The White House has been a busy place this August. For the most part, one set of aides has handled the biggest scandal of President Clinton's career -- his acknowledgment of an intimate relationship with Monica S. Lewinsky -- while another set managed the biggest counter-terrorist strike of his Presidency, the cruise missiles launched last week at Afghanistan and Sudan.
But there has been some crossover, including, of course, the President himself. Mr. Clinton will leave for Russia on Monday afternoon after what appears to have been one of the least restful family vacations imaginable.
Along with Chancellor Helmut Kohl of Germany, President Yeltsin is the only major non-Communist leader who was serving when Mr. Clinton was first elected in 1992. It is a shaky trio. Mr. Kohl is facing a tough re-election campaign this year, and Mr. Clinton turned a stop in Germany in May into a virtual campaign swing for his old friend.
The President has built an affectionate and trusting relationship with Mr. Yeltsin, his aides say. But even before this week's furor he had planned to hedge American bets in Russia. On Wednesday, he meets with leaders of the Russia's Parliament and regions. That meeting, Mr. Clinton's aides said today, now takes on new significance.
Administration officials said that aides will probably have to rejigger key points and questions that Mr. Clinton should raise with Russian presidential hopefuls like Aleksandr I. Lebed, a former general who is now governor of a Siberian region.
Indeed, the two-day summit meeting is starting to shape up as almost an intelligence-gathering mission, since the status of the Russian Government is so unclear. While Mr. Clinton will meet with Mr. Yeltsin, several Cabinet members will also sit down with their counterparts -- whoever those may be.
"It's very rare that the senior leadership of the United States is going into a crisis environment," an official said, glimpsing the bright side and calling it "a real opportunity for senior policy makers to get a first-hand look."
One adviser to Mr. Clinton said that, with Mr. Yeltsin's rejection of resignation today, summit planning had settled down. "We're sort of more on track today than we were yesterday," he said. Besides, he argued, the Administration had never expected the trip to Russia to be routine.
"It's not like a completely stable situation went completely unstable," he said. "There's been uncertainty on several fronts all along."

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE OVERVIEW;
Dow Off by 114 More Points, Ending Worst Week Since '89

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
Stock prices fell sharply again yesterday, as continued fears about instability in Russia and economic weakness closer to home produced the worst week for the American stock market in nine years.
The Dow Jones industrial average declined 114.31 points, or 1.4 percent, to close at 8,051.68. For the week, the Dow industrials were down 5.6 percent. Not since the so-called mini-crash of October 1989, when the Dow fell 7.8 percent, has the bellwether index fallen so far so fast. 
 The decline yesterday was a mere shadow of the 4.1 percent drop on Thursday. But a stream of global losses continued yesterday, with financial markets in Asia and Europe particularly hard hit.
In the course of the last week, American investors have begun shifting their attention away from Russia and other foreign economies to the weakening outlook for corporate profits at home.
Those concerns showed up yesterday in the loss in the Nasdaq composite index, which is chock-full of the computer and Internet stocks that are enormously popular with individual investors. Traders aggressively sold shares of Dell Computer, Cisco Systems, Yahoo and Amazon.com, stocks that have been among the biggest gainers of the year. The Nasdaq index fell 46.73 points, or 2.8 percent, to 1,639.68, and finished the week 8.8 percent lower.
Despite the recent losses, most investors in these technology stocks are sitting on enormous gains: Amazon.com, for example, is still up 251 percent this year.
Some investors, and a few analysts on Wall Street, are beginning to worry, though, that profits for these and other American companies might not grow as much as expected next year.
"The problem is not just in Asia anymore," James Paulsen, chief investment officer at Norwest Investment Management in Minneapolis, said. "Look to the north and south," where the Canadian and Mexican economies are struggling. That could more directly affect the profits of American companies, he said, keeping profit growth below 5 percent annually for the next few years after investors have come to expect growth of 10 percent or more. Others are even less optimistic. "We see no real earnings growth in 1998 or 1999, and we're uncertain about the year 2000," Peter Anderson, chief investment officer at American Express Financial Advisors, said. With that outlook, it is hard to justify current market prices, much less the far higher valuations of just a few weeks ago.
Since closing at a record high on July 17, the Dow industrials have lost 13.8 percent of their value, exceeding the 13.3 percent decline from August through October of last year. The major stock indexes are still showing gains for the year -- the Dow is up 1.8 percent and the Standard & Poor's 500 index is up 5.9 percent.
But individuals who invest through mutual funds are more likely to be in the red. According to Lipper Analytical Services, the average general stock fund was down 2.8 percent from the beginning of the year through Thursday, meaning the recent plunge has started to eat into the profits accumulated over the last three roaring years.
The stock market attempted to rally yesterday. In the first half-hour of trading, the Dow industrials jumped more than 78 points. But in 15 minutes, they lost all that and more, as sell orders flooded in. The rest of the day, virtually any rally in share prices was met with further bouts of heavy selling.
Some market analysts continue to be optimistic, chief among them Abby Joseph Cohen of Goldman, Sachs & Company. In a note to clients yesterday, Ms. Cohen said that the recent declines in equity prices "have been more closely linked to changes in sentiment than changes in the fundamental backdrop in the United States." She added that the Standard & Poor's 500 index, which fell 5 percent last week and is 13.4 percent below its high, "is now 7 percent to 10 percent undervalued."
She now favors technology and financial services stocks, which suffered sharp blows again yesterday. Information is beginning to dribble out about the exposure of banks and securities firms to losses in foreign markets from loans, securities trades and complex financial contracts known as derivatives.
The BankAmerica Corporation and the BankBoston Corporation each reported heavy losses from the trading of domestic and international assets. BankAmerica said it had lost $220 million this quarter, primarily because of Russia, leaving it with trading profits of $315 million for the year. BankBoston said its international and domestic trading operations had lost $30 million in this quarter, with only one-third of the loss related to Russia. Having focused on Russian losses until now, investors in financial stocks began to fret more about losses in other locales, including Latin America.
Worries about the Russian economy, and its possible ripple effects through much of the developing world, continued to weigh on foreign markets as well yesterday. Share prices fell more than 2 percent in Britain and 1.7 percent in Germany, while Japan's Nikkei index slid 3.5 percent and finished the day below 14,000, at its lowest level in 12 years.
But Latin American markets, which have been swamped by selling in recent weeks, took a breather. Mexico's major stock market index gained 3.2 percent, and Brazil's rose nearly 2 percent -- small gains, but important for markets that have lost more than one-third of their value since January.
Investors worldwide generally sought safety, and in the United States they snapped up shares of electric utilities, telecommunications companies and others that pay high, steady dividends. Shares of Consolidated Edison, for example, rose 1 3/16 , to 47 13/16 , their highest level in a year, as the Dow Jones utilities average rose nearly 1 percent.
The utilities index, in fact, has gained 3.9 percent since the beginning of January, more than twice the gain in the Dow industrials.
Stocks of regional telephone companies also rose yesterday, with Ameritech, BellSouth and Bell Atlantic among the winners. Bell Atlantic gained $2, to $45 3/16 .
The biggest losses were among the technology stocks that have a fervent following of individual investors. Dell Computer fell $6 5/16 , to $118 3/4, while Cisco Systems dropped $4 7/16 , to $94 11/16 ; meanwhile Amazon.com dropped $13 7/64 , to $105 57/64 , and Yahoo lost $8, to $831/16 .
Investors who have held those stocks for the year may be wincing at the latest declines but reminding themselves of their nice gains. And in general, the market's drop in recent weeks is still not considered severe by many Americans.
"We're not yet even back to where we started the year," Mr. Paulsen of Norwest said about the latest decline. "People are saying, 'Oh, isn't that interesting.' If we got down another 15 percent, to where their net worths were back to where they were in 1996, they may begin to worry."

HEADLINE: THE MARKET TURMOIL: THE HEMISPHERE'S CRISIS;
Even as Latin Markets Plunge, Drastic Remedies Are Avoided

BYLINE: By SAM DILLON 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The recent panic in world markets has weakened Latin American currencies and drastically reduced the wealth of many Latin corporations, raising prospects that governments in the region may be forced to take Draconian measures to contain the crisis.
Financial authorities in Brazil, Mexico and Argentina and other nations have allowed interest rates to rise, spent billions of dollars of reserves to support their currencies, and tinkered with other limited steps. But, perhaps hoping that market calm will eventually prevail, they have avoided more drastic steps such as aggressively squeezing cash out of circulation, analysts said. 
 Today, the Mexican peso crossed an unwelcome threshold, dropping in value to settle at a rate above 10 to the United States dollar.
"The Latin countries are using their second-stage defenses, hoping that these external shocks won't last very long," said Luis R. Luis, a managing director at Scudder Kemper Investments in Boston. "They want to avoid having to impose really Draconian monetary policies. But if this continues, they're really going to have to jack up the interest rates."
Most Latin markets got a breather today after a week of panicked trading that saw foreign investors flee from the region and local traders cash out of equities to buy dollars.
Mexican stocks, which have fallen by more than half in dollar terms since the beginning of the year, rose 3.22 percent, with confidence bolstered in part by a more modest rise in Brazil's larger stock market.
Argentina's benchmark index rose nearly 1 percent; the Chilean and Peruvian markets were off slightly. Venezuela, the sick man of the region, was the exception -- its stock index plummeted 4.52 percent.
But gloom among investors and business executives across Latin America continued to deepen. Fears persist that the collapse of the Russian ruble could play out here, either with a devaluation of the Venezuelan bolivar or, in what would be far worse, the Brazilian real.
Adding to the uncertainty was a belief that the International Monetary Fund, short of cash, is sitting on its hands, even though several Latin American economies are floundering. The fund has invited Latin finance ministers to meet next Thursday in Washington to discuss joint responses. Finance Minister Pedro Malan of Brazil and Economy Minister Roque Fernandez of Argentina have said they will attend. Mexico's Treasury Secretary, Jose Angel Gurria, and several others have not yet announced their intentions.
But because the I.M.F., after financing Asian and Russian rescue packages, has less than $10 billion available for new bailouts, its call for a meeting has aroused little hope.
Some analysts are starting to criticize what they see as a listless reaction to the financial chaos in Latin America by the Clinton Administration, too. "The U.S. is keeping monetary policy tight enough to slow our economy even though most developing economies sink," said David R. Malpass, chief international economist at Bear, Stearns. "I'm still hopeful that the U.S. will become more engaged, to calm things down and allow Latin America a rebound."
Since Asian financial crises broke out last year, international investors have become increasingly leery of emerging markets in general. And Russia's troubles have led to a hemorrhaging of capital out of Latin American securities.
In Caracas, where investors fear similarities between conditions in Russia's and Venezuela's oil-dependent and ill-managed economies, stocks are off 66 percent for the year in local-currency terms. Mexico's main stock index is down 40 percent, and if losses from the peso are taken into account, 51 percent. Brazil is 34 percent lower; Argentina 47 percent, and Chile 35 percent.
Authorities in the three largest Latin economies have confronted the crisis with relatively modest initial tactics, apparently hesitating before taking more painful steps, analysts said.
In Mexico, where the currency floats freely, the peso has dropped by 8.9 percent since the ruble's devaluation, settling here today at 10.020 to the dollar. Officials have attempted to slow the peso's decline by auctioning dollars, $200 million at a time, several times since the crisis began.
The Mexican central bank has also removed increasing amounts of currency from circulation as a way of indirectly raising interest rates. On Wednesday, it raised reserve requirements for commercial banks. In response, interest rates jumped to 27.2 percent on the 28-day Treasury notes that are used as a market reference, from 22 percent.
Still, Mexican rates are far below the 50 percent-plus levels reached in the peso crisis of 1994-95, when businesses were strangled and the economy plunged into a two-year recession.
In Brazil, where the currency trades within a fixed band and is under assault by speculators who consider it overvalued, the authorities have also defended its value. But there, too, they have acted with less vigor than during the previous crisis, a speculative attack last October.
As in October, Brazilian authorities have freely sold foreign reserves to defend the real. So far this month, economists estimate that some $8.5 billion of the country's $70 billion in foreign-exchange reserves have been sold to prop up the real. And the authorities have made it easier for Brazilians to borrow money abroad.
But in contrast with the moves last fall, the central bank has not yet raised interest rates from the current 19.75 percent, although offshore rates, set by commercial banks, have soared to 36 percent. Last fall, the authorities in Brasilia doubled official interest rates, to 41 percent, to defend the real.
The tactics in Brasilia are being watched closely in Buenos Aires. "The largest risk for Argentina is that the next victim could be Brazil," said Luis R. Secco, an economist with Estudio Broda, a consulting firm.
The Argentine peso is freely convertible with the dollar, and the central bank has $23.7 billion in foreign-exchange reserves. So far, authorities have made no changes in policy in reaction to the crisis, Mr. Secco said, adding, "Our authorities are viewing this calmly."
The opposite is true in Venezuela, where economists estimate that the bolivar may be overvalued by at least 30 percent and the budget deficit has ballooned as oil revenue plunged this year. The authorities have made deep spending cuts, allowed interest rates to soar above 100 percent, and last week sold nearly $500 million of the country's $14 billion in reserves. Nonetheless, fears of a devaluation are increasing.

HEADLINE: NORTHWEST PILOTS STRIKE AS TALKS FAIL ON CONTRACT

BYLINE: By LAURENCE ZUCKERMAN 

DATELINE: MINNEAPOLIS, Saturday, Aug. 29

 BODY:
More than 6,000 pilots at Northwest Airlines walked off the job last night, shutting down the country's fourth-largest airline in a strike that may cripple much of the Midwest.
"No Northwest pilot will fly until further notice from me," said Steve Zoller, the 747 captain who is chairman of the master executive council of the pilots' union, which reviewed Northwest's offer. 
 Despite 12 days of nearly nonstop negotiations under the direction of a Federal mediator, Northwest and the Air Line Pilots Association could not bridge their differences and reach a tentative contract.
President Clinton, who ordered striking pilots at American Airlines back to work last year, decided not to repeat the move.
In a statement, the White House said: "The President has decided not to exercise his authority to intervene and he urges the parties to stay at the table, work hard and resolve their differences. This dispute has dragged on for almost two years now and both sides should redouble their efforts to reach an agreement as soon as possible. They owe that to each other and to the American public."
Mr. Zoller said that the pilots union was fully committed to reaching a settlement. "After two years of negotiations, Northwest management has still not lived up to their responsibilities," he said. "I can assure you that our pilots would rather be in the cockpit than walking a picket line."
The union told all pilots to stay where they were until noon today. This appeared to be a sign that negotiations could resume soon, since it would take much more time to restart the airline if the pilots returned to their home bases.
Northwest said it was willing to keep negotiating. "We are committed to staying at the bargaining table for as long as it takes to get an agreement," said Donald J. Foley, vice president for communications for Northwest.
The strike grounded 1,700 of Northwest's daily flights and nearly 1,000 more flown by its two commuter affiliates, Mesaba Airlines and Express Airlines. Northwest is also one of the largest American carriers serving Asia with 119 international flights.
On Friday, many Northwest passengers were worried and confused as today's strike deadline of 12:01 A.M. Eastern time approached. Northwest canceled 170 of its flights on Friday, and some passengers were forced to seek other flights. Still, there were no signs of panic.
"Between Hurricane Bonnie and the threat of a Northwest strike, I figure I'm playing double jeopardy," said Tammy Wangler of Fargo, N.D., who was waiting Friday at the Hector International Airport in Fargo to board a Northwest flight to Raleigh, N.C.
A main sticking point in the negotiations between Northwest and the pilots, according to both sides, was the pilots' pay.
Northwest was offering a wage increase of 10 percent over the life of a new five-year contract, which it said would bring the pilots in line with their counterparts at American Airlines, which is the country's second-largest carrier.
But the pilots, many of whom earn more than $200,000 a year, wanted a 15 percent increase and a onetime payment of $25,000 each to make up for the sacrifices they made in 1993, when they took a 15.5 percent pay cut, along with other Northwest union workers, to bring Northwest back from the brink of bankruptcy.
"We shared the pain to help our airline survive, and now we want to share in the success we helped create," Mr. Zoller said last week.
Northwest officials said that the pilots received 14.4 million shares of stock, or 18 percent of the company's stock, in exchange for their wage cuts. They say those shares are now worth more than $500 million.
"We acknowledge that they deserve a raise," said Mr. Foley.
"That has never been the issue," he said. "The question is: How much can the No. 4 airline afford?"
Late Friday evening, Northwest disclosed its latest pay offer to the union that would give pilots 4.5 percent more money than pilots at other big airlines by the year 2000 and 7.7 percent by 2002. The company said it would also provide profit-sharing of up to 5 percent of each pilot's pay and a lump-sum payment equal to 3.5 percent of annual pay. It also said it would make changes in its two-tier pay scale, in which beginning pilots make 40 percent less than experienced ones, reducing the time needed to go to the higher tier to three years from five.
It was unclear how much of an increase the latest offer was over Northwest's initial offer of a 10 percent pay increase.
Another big issue in the contract negotiations has been job security. The airline also said that its latest offer included a no-layoffs proposal. The pilots union had been concerned about their status under a proposed alliance with Continental Airlines. It was also concerned about losing jobs to regional airlines allied with Northwest and to foreign airlines like KLM that also have an alliance with Northwest.
But the airline's proposal on job security failed to satisfy the pilots union. Paul G. Omodt, a spokesman for the pilots union, said, "We are not going to give away our jobs."
Five other unions, representing the lion's share of Northwest's workers, including flight attendants, maintenance workers and reservation clerks, are also negotiating new contracts and have pledged to support the strike. Two of those unions, the International Association of Machinists and the International Brotherhood of Teamsters, also received stock in Northwest in 1993 in exchange for wage cuts and have representatives on the company's 15-member board.
Northwest executives have been concerned that if they give too much to the pilots, they will have to do the same for other workers, leaving the airline with higher costs than its larger competitors.
But the unions point out that such concerns have not stopped company executives, led by president and chief executive John H. Dasburg, from enriching themselves with tens of thousands of stock options worth millions of dollars. Mr. Dasburg alone sold stock worth $19 million earlier this year.
The strike came as a blow to several upper Midwest states, including Michigan, Minnesota and north and South Dakota, that depend almost exclusively on Northwest for their jet service.
Northwest flies about 80 percent of the seats available in Detroit and Minneapolis-St. Paul, the country's 9th and 13th busiest airports. In many smaller cities in Michigan and the Dakotas, Northwest is the only airline offering service.
Governors in seven Midwest states and Tennessee had appealed to President Clinton to intervene to prevent a strike. But an equally influential group of Senators, Congressmen and union leaders, including Senator Edward M. Kennedy, Democrat of Massachusetts and House Democratic leader Representative Richard Gephardt of Missouri, urged the Mr. Clinton not to interfere.
Officials and travel agents in Minnesota, Michigan and other states said that there was little they could do to prevent the damage.
"Obviously, there is a great deal of concern in communities like Minneapolis and Detroit because the impact of a strike takes away 80 percent of the lift in the marketplace," said Brent Garback, the chief executive of Total Travel management, one of the largest corporate travel agencies in Michigan. "There aren't enough seats to be had for people to wander over to an another airline. From an economic standpoint a strike is devastating to the community."
Mr. Omodt added that after a few days of adjustment other airlines would begin sending in more planes.
However, carriers such as Trans World Airlines, United Airlines, Delta Airlines and others said that they did not have enough spare airplanes to make up for the loss of Northwest service in the upper Midwest. The airlines have said that they will accept tickets from Northwest passengers only if seats on their planes are available.

HEADLINE: Don't Knock California

 BODY:

To the Editor:
California bashing is a favorite Book Review hobby, of course, and must be comforting. But I can't let you get away with "the state's colleges and universities have evolved into economic aeries for the privileged children of the upper-middle-class taxpayer" (from Jack Hitt's June 14 review of "Cold New World: Growing Up in a Harder Country," by William Finnegan). 
 The University of California may not be free anymore, but with tuition nearing $5,000 a year it's still one of the greatest bargains in education; the California State universities are even cheaper, and the community colleges, at $13 a credit, are within reach of everybody. When black enrollment declined after affirmative action was canceled, the spaces did not go to rich white kids. A large portion went to young people of Asian heritage, many if not most coming from modest homes.
 
Cecelia Holland
Fortuna, Calif.
