SECTION: Section 2; Page 1; Column 5; Arts and Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 1174 words

HEADLINE: Gershwin Is Still Cause for Rhapsody;
An Inspired Borrower Of a Black Tradition

BYLINE: By STANLEY CROUCH; Stanley Crouch is an author, a critic and a columnist for The Daily News.

 BODY:
GEORGE GERSHWIN had a gargantuan impact on what the writer Albert Murray calls "the American soundtrack." But his music and the good life that it provided for its composer were inextricably connected to the racial terrain of show business. The finer esthetic elements of his music came into existence as a result of an interracial training ground that transcended all the balderdash of the national skin game. But the troubling ethnic components of show business aren't usually discussed, particularly when a man of Gershwin's Jewish background is the subject, for fear, in that case, of being seen as anti-Semitic. My intent is to provide an overview of Gershwin more substantial than the skeleton of the great marlin that Hemingway's fishermen returned home with in "The Old Man and the Sea."
Those who praise Gershwin see him as a number of things: a composer whose work epitomized the Golden Era of popular songwriting, a true example of the rags-to-riches story, a genius from the streets who pulled telling strands of American sound together and whose innovations earned him a position in the pantheon. 
 All of that is absolutely true. Gershwin was great. He did have a snap. He was a first-class popular melodist. His sound, part Negro and part everything else that was going on when he came to power, is one of the most important distillations of lyric and rhythmic American feeling to arrive in the first half of the 20th century. He was one of the musical poets of modern life. We cannot accurately think of urban American existence with any accuracy and leave him out.
His impact has been rightfully epic. We'll never know how many people have sung his songs professionally and unprofessionally, in public and in private, how many have danced to them in every circumstance from the grand ballroom to the sweaty house party. Those numbers are incalculable, just as are all the images that are summoned when one hears Gershwin music. Anyone whose music was used in so many films and as a catalyst for so many great dance numbers lives on that mythic side of our comprehension. He strides through and beyond his time like a colossus of urban moxie and celebration, of romantic sentiment, of U.S.A. syncopation. The brother had soul.
In fact, when Gershwin began contemplating the creation of an opera based in the nation's cultural vernacular, he expressed ambitions that were far greater than American literary innovators like T. S. Eliot, Ezra Pound, F. Scott Fitzgerald and Ernest Hemingway, as well as all of the great filmmakers of his country and his time. Gershwin aspired to create an expansive feeling of Americana. He told Isaac Goldberg of The New York World that he wanted to write an opera that bubbled up from "the melting pot of New York City, which is the symbolic and the actual blend of the native and immigrant strains."
"This would allow for many kinds of music, black and white, Eastern and Western, and would call for a style that would achieve artistic and esthetic unity."
In that sense, what Gershwin discussed -- and what might have arrived in musical composition had he lived and been able to come back to that idea again -- is instructive because it would have been far more adventurous than almost all the celebrated American fiction of his time other than that of William Faulkner, Richard Wright and Ralph Ellison. Unlike American writers, however, Gershwin was neither afraid of Negroes nor intimidated by the subjects of style and feeling that they presented in their ethnic version of American life. He spent his time up in Harlem, where he could hear jazz and listen to the masters of stride piano. He realized, as did most of the songwriters of his time, that Negroes had put something together that could supply fresh melodic, harmonic and rhythmic directions through the literal and implied elements of blues, idiomatic syncopation and swing. As early as 1922, Gershwin wrote "Blue Monday," an opera about the Harlem that was on his mind. It was orchestrated by Will Vodery, a prominent Negro musician of the time who worked as an orchestrator for Ziegfeld's follies and was an accomplished conductor.
Gershwin's time uptown had put him in the same situation as the developing black musicians who were there: he was learning from the best people he could get next to, since all artists learn from others. In particular, however, the exchanges or the homemade academies of jam sessions and rent parties and private lessons from men like James P. Johnson resulted in an extraordinary irony: with the exception of Duke Ellington, Gershwin went on to become a finer songwriter than any of the Negroes who were his contemporaries or who influenced him.
That is where we get the rub and where the attacks on Gershwin for "stealing" from Negroes falls flat on its lewdy duty. Given his talent, why is Gershwin also paddled so often for being something less than he actually was? Primarily because Gershwin was Jewish, and there is an extremely complicated history fundamental to the tale of Negroes and Jews in show business. It is a story of alliance, animosity, success, failure, exploitation transcending friendships and ongoing shifts of attitude and economic position. Show business was an open frontier for Jewish immigrants who -- often battling one another every step of the way -- rose to higher and higher positions as entrepreneurs, performers, agents, critics and so on. Where Indians complain of their land being stolen by Northern Europeans, Negroes have made similar complaints in cultural terms. Those complaints steam up from the fact that Jewish journeymen, in and out of blackface, were able to make use of Negro culture while benefiting from a support system capable of neutralizing the religious status of a minority in favor of its acceptably white skin. Jewish or not, white skin or not, George Gershwin was nobody's journeyman.
To go a little deeper, there is another irony when the subject of "stealing" comes around to Gershwin. That irony is found in the work of all the jazz musicians who have used his songs as jumping off points for classic improvisations, either on his themes or on the chord sequences he devised. The uncopyrightable chords of "I Got Rhythm," by the way, have been used by so many jazz musicians that we have no idea how much larger the Gershwin fortune would be if his estate could lay claim to every use of that harmonic material. So one could argue that black musicians have done much more profitable, literal stealing from Gershwin than he did from them, which, in his case, amounts to nothing more than inspired borrowing.
Finally, like all giants of American art, culture and entertainment, George Gershwin belongs to everyone and should be celebrated by all. His achievement goes far beyond race and far beyond the squabbles our nation's racial mess continues to guarantee. When they line up the work of those who spoke across the barriers of American life, you can believe George Gershwin will be highly placed in that number.

HEADLINE: Q&A/Lorna J. Wendt;
Marriage, and Divorce, as 50-50 Propositions

BYLINE: By NANCY POLK 

 BODY:
DIVORCE is usually a private matter. But when Gary and Lorna Wendt of North Stamford went through the process, the public got an earful.
The Wendts were high school sweethearts in Wisconsin who had a traditional marriage for more than 30 years, until, in 1995, Mr. Wendt, chairman of the GE Capital Corporation, sought a divorce. 
 In the subsequent proceedings, Ms. Wendt, a stay-at-home spouse, declared herself an equal partner, entitled to half the family's assets.
Although the Wendts' considerable assets put them in a narrow sliver of Americans economically, the issue of the financial worth of spouses who do not work outside the home affects many of America's divorcing couples. Ms. Wendt's claims also sent shivers through the corporate ranks, where the thought is that who made the money keeps most of it.
In the ensuing dispute, Ms. Wendt was pictured on the cover of Fortune Magazine under the headline, "What's a Corporate Wife Worth?" She has appeared on television and been widely interviewed, and she has established the Institute for Equality in Marriage in Stamford, a clearinghouse for information about divorce. On Sept. 23 she will speak at the Ridgefield Library.
"This is not just a divorce between two rich people," she says emphatically. It is about how wives, and sometimes husbands, are treated by their spouses and the courts.
She urges women to know their family finances thoroughly, as she said she did. "Knowledge is powerful," she explained. "Just look out for yourselves." Since about half of American marriages end in divorce, Ms. Wendt believes that preparation for independent living is essential. "I believe in marriage contracts," she says. "With that sad statistic in mind, we should go into marriage with our eyes open. Marriage may end in divorce. What's going to happen if that happens?"
Last December, the Wendt marriage was dissolved by Judge Kevin Tierney of Connecticut Superior Court. He awarded Ms. Wendt about $20 million in property and money as well as stock and pension options, out of total Wendt holdings estimated by Ms. Wendt at between $45 million and $130 million. Both sides are appealing the award.
Recently Ms. Wendt met to discuss her belief in marriage as a 50-50 partnership. Following are excerpts from that conversation.
 
Q. When your husband decided to divorce you, what were your financial expectations?
A. I assumed we would split everything 50-50. Our marriage was a partnership. If he wanted to dissolve the marriage I just assumed I would get 50 percent.
I was looking for an even distribution of assets accrued by both of us during our marriage such as stock options, restricted stock units and a supplemental pension. A lot of things have payoffs after the divorce but were earned during the marriage.
 
Q. Were you business partners?
A. I was his wife. I was in the business of creating a happy home. I was the mother of his children, the C.E.O. of our house, that was my business. I gave up my career. We decided together to have a family and it was our understanding that that is what I would do. I did those things extremely well. He went on to only do what he was very good at and that's what got him to where he is today.
 
Q. Were you tempted to just put it all behind you?
A. The first thing for me was shock that he wanted to end the marriage. Unlike many women, I did not let him put me down as a human being, devalue what I brought to our marriage. When he offered me 10 percent, that was a real personal affront. I am not a 10 percent person or a 20 percent. I gave a 100 percent or more to this partnership. If he wanted to dissolve it, I expect to be compensated for the years as a partner and that to me is 50-50.
Would anyone go into marriage today or advise anyone to go into marriage if somewhere down the line -- the courts or your spouse -- could say you're only worth 30 percent or 10 percent?
 
Q. Where is Connecticut in all this?
A. Connecticut is not a community property state. It is an equitable distribution state. Even though the root word is equal, it's up to a judge to decide how much the little lady should get. That's the "enough is enough" theory that is prevalent in the court system.
We all have different judges in family court. They all come in with preconceived ideas or notions. How was their family life? How is their life with their wife? All these things affect a human being. And what a person gets.
I would like to see that changed. We are challenging the courts on some things we thought were fairly gender-biased. One is the "enough is enough" rule. That's when a judge says -- here's a judge making maybe $125,000 a year -- "This lady has been offered $10 million. Isn't that enough?" That isn't the issue. I never said that wasn't enough. The issue is that we were partners. Who are you to decide I am a 10 percent person?
 
Q. Is that what inspired you to establish the Institute for Equality in Marriage?
A. I realized this was a bigger issue than just Gary and myself. This has become a national and international issue. It is because of the principle that when you marry you are equal, not unequal partners.
As a society, we can't have young women and men going into a marriage expecting it to be a partnership, working their whole lives side by side, then have to say "What I did at home was worth less than the person who brings home the paycheck." That's not right. How can you say that of the love that you have for a family, your children, all the things that have been consumed during a marriage, all the meals that were cooked, all the doctors' trips, all the excuses for why Dad is never home?
The institute's main mission is to educate society as to what we mean by marriage today. One of the things we are hoping to do is judicial education. That is not lobbying. That is bringing together learned scholars for the judges, for the lawyers and showing them what is going on around the state and around the United States and this is what the latest thinking is on family law.
 
Q. Are you an advocate of pre-nuptial agreements?
A. Since marriage is the biggest social contract that two people will ever make, I think they should talk about it, maybe have something in writing, a marriage contract, that doesn't say anything about money. I think it should say, "I value you as a human being, I respect you and what you will bring to our marriage. If we should go our separate ways it's been a 50-50 partnership." What if your partner balks at that? Shouldn't a red light go off? Beforehand?
I think that's being a little practical. I guess it's unfortunate the courts are not finding that marriage is a partnership, so maybe we had better have that understanding before we enter into marriage.
You can have postnuptial agreements, although it is a little harder. You could sit down with your husband and say, "Now let's have some things in writing." How do you think that would go over after 5 or 10 or 20 years of marriage? That's a little scary.
 
Q. When divorcing, is it unusual to receive less than half of the assets of a marriage?
A. Most high-asset divorce cases are settled, but they are not settled equally. Women are browbeaten and threatened verbally. So they settle for less than 50 percent. I was the one to say publicly, "Wait a minute, I gave my life to our family and to us willingly, lovingly and now you are saying that's no good."
The 30-year-olds don't understand what this is all about because to them marriage is a partnership. It is my generation that is the hardest hit because they have spent 35 years in a marriage, all of a sudden to find out he wants someone younger, or whatever, and thinks he can walk away and say, "Thank you, here's drivel." Women of divorce are the new poor class in the United States.
 
Q. What changes would you like to see in divorce laws?
A. What I would like to see happen 25 years down the road may never happen. I would like to see some uniform, basic divorce laws that affect everybody no matter what the income, across all 50 states. Every state is different. If I were to lobby now I would have to have 50 different lobbyists for 50 different states. All that has to be changed.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Lorna J. Wendt: Challenging the courts on the "enough is enough" theory of financial settlement in divorce. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Q&A/Dr. Constance Ward Iervolino;
Task of Applying Standards to Classrooms

BYLINE: By DONNA GREENE 

 BODY:
THE pressure is on for schools to have higher standards for all students -- pressure from the State Board of Regents, the business community and parents, among others. It will be the task of people like Dr. Constance Ward Iervolino, the new assistant superintendent of curriculum and instruction for the White Plains public schools, to make sure that all the tough talk about higher standards is not just talk, not just the latest educational fad.
Dr. Iervolino, a resident of the Bronx with a doctorate in education, was an assistant principal in Ardsley from 1988-90 before becoming principal of two schools in the Rye Neck School District: the Daniel Warren School (kindergarten through first grade) and the F. E. Bellows School (grades two through four). 
 In her new job, which she assumed last month, she oversees the curriculum for kindergartners through 12th graders in White Plains, which, with 6,200 students, is one of the county's largest school districts. Here are excerpts from a recent conversation with her:
 
Q. Will the directives on new standards coming from the state make a difference?
A. I think the standards now will really influence and shape classroom practice in every classroom. It will be aligned with curriculum, and it will be aligned with the assessment. For the very first time in New York State there is a parallel and a very close match between what the standard says in the curriculum and the assessment that is going to test the results. That was not always true.
Also, in each of the grade levels, from the elementary to the middle to the high school, if you look at any of the assessments that will now be used -- say the 4th-grade language arts test, the 8th-grade test and the 11th-grade Regents test -- they will use the same standards. That is very new.
 
Q. What are those standards?
A. The standards in English and language arts, for example, deal with listening, speaking, reading and writing -- to be able to read for information, to access information, to apply information; to be able to read for reflection; to be able to listen, read and write for response to literature; to be able to read, write, listen and speak for critical analysis and to read, write, listen and speak for social interaction.
And every single one of those standards are the same at 4th, 8th and 11th grade. Of course, the tests will look different at each grade level. But the components will be similar. White Plains has done a lot of work at looking at the standards and asking, What do the standards say? Now we're looking to make sure we're aligning all the curriculum to the standards and curriculum and doing a gap analysis -- saying, What's missing?
In every school district we will find there are areas where we will put in more support so that we will be able to meet those very challenging standards. And the idea is, of course, that there will be Regents exams for everyone at the end -- so it's raising expectations for all students.
 
Q. How do we know this is not just the latest fad?
A. I think the unfortunate thing is that people have perceived a lot of what has gone on as fads and bandwagons, and in truth there have been innovations that have gone nowhere.
But this is the state acting -- for the very first time. At the end of the 1980's, the different subject area groups, like the National Council of Teachers of Math and the National Council of Science Teachers, had looked at content area curriculum and had worked really closely and looked at the kinds of reforms that were needed in those subject areas to bring kids to the millennium. They did a lot of work. The wonderful thing is that the state is now embracing all the work that has gone on before. In other words, they didn't start from scratch. There is a whole body of research out there that tells us what works.
 
Q. Will the new assessments look at individual children's performance or evaluate tht of school districts?
A. The test lends itself to doing both. The state began getting away from just meeting minimal standards when it began two years ago to report test results that differentiated between how many advanced readers there were, not just how many were at grade level. That's important because the goal of schools has to be to add value -- not just have children reach their potential, but to maximize it. Otherwise, children who come to school accomplished will succeed in spite of us, but we need to take them beyond what they could otherwise do without us.
 
Q. How is the question of "gifted" children factored in? Are there separate standards for them?
A. The standards are the same standards but we need to differentiate instruction in the classroom so we can accommodate all students. The level at which students achieve is only limited by their own effort and their own ability. We need to continue to add to that. I think there are definitely ways within the classroom to differentiate instruction, and we will be working on that as well. We need to make sure that the gifted kids, the advanced child, the child who comes in reading and comes in with that knowledge base will be able to take off and go beyond and not feel constrained because they are beyond their grade levels.
 
Q. But, as you know, that is the ideal. Some are gifted teachers who can exceed curriculum guidelines, others can be trained to do so and others will never be able to do it.
A. Yes, but there are lots of things that you can do still. There can be flexible grouping. There can be additional programs, there can be programs within the programs. Within the standards that are given there is enough room to do things, to make accommodations. That's a very important part. That also brings me to something that I think is new about the standards. We've learned a lot about teaching and learning in the last 15 to 20 years and about how people learn. We don't learn for keeps when we are learning discreet, isolated pieces of information. And that's the way testing was until recently. It was really an accrual of facts and information. We're now being asked to go beyond that. Now we have an integration between math, science and technology, between English language arts and social studies. The eighth-grade English language arts test will actually ask students to write, weaving in the social studies curriculum.
 
Q. White Plains has many non-English-speaking people. How do you factor that in?
A. In Westchester County there is a growing population of limited and non-English-speaking children. We're very fortunate to have very fine English as a Second Language programs throughout the county. About six years ago, I visited Japan with a group of educators invited by the Japanese Chamber of Commerce. One of the things the Japanese were so amazed and gratified about were the English as a Second Language programs we have here. There is nothing like that there or in Europe. But more than just having the program, the English as a Second Language teacher needs to work with the classroom teacher so that accommodations can be made for the child during the time the child spends in the classroom. That can be done through different materials, tape recorders and classroom volunteers. Through this early identification, that program becomes one that follows the child and the child develops more expertise.
 
Q. And how does the socio-economic perspective come into this?
A. Again, every class is heterogeneous, and particularly within the elementary schools we see the diversity of students as really an advantage as well as a challenge. It's what the real world is like, and students need to learn -- and they do learn -- from the different cultures that they encompass. We need to provide for everyone the background that will enable them to be literate, articulate and educated.

HEADLINE: Alan King's Love-Hate Relationship

 BODY:
In the 1950's and 60's, the comedian Alan King became a kind of social critic of the suburbs. He poked fun at life in places like Long Island -- where he had lived since the late 1940's -- on national television when national television was just coming of age and particularly on programs like the "Ed Sullivan Show," which originated in New York.
In a recent conversation with James Barron of The New York Times, Mr. King looked back at his life and career, and how a sense of place influenced both. 
 Q. Long Island was a part of your act for a long time. Isn't it fairly unusual for a comedian to become so closely identified with a place? How did it happen?
A. I was quoted on the "Huntley-Brinkley Report." They quoted me as saying the Long Island Expressway was the longest parking lot in the world. That was the line that did it. I was given names like the General de Gaulle of the crab-grass set. It was terrible.
Q. Seriously, though: how did you come to do the kind of material you did about Long Island?
A. It was an evolution that followed my actual life. I came out of Williamsburg when I got married 51 years ago. From Williamsburg we moved to a summer apartment in Long Beach, but we lived in it all year long. It was a little tough taking a shower outdoors in December.
At just about that time, my career started to take a little upturn. I was discovered in a sense by Ed Sullivan, and I bought a house in Rockville Centre, a $22,000 ranch house in a cul-de-sac. It was an upscale Levittown, but I thought I was in San Simeon. What happened was, when I moved into this house, there were so many crazy things going on that I used to relate to everybody. All the guys would say, "Alan, these are things you should be talking about."
Q. So, really you were just living your life, but you went on national television and talked about it.
A. I was talking about my life, an exaggeration of my own life. I wrote a book, "Anybody Who Owns His Own Home Deserves It," that was the retelling of a lot of the monologues that I started doing on the Sullivan show, like I lived on the Baldwin-Rockville Centre line. The line went right through my house. I had a Rockville Centre address, but my children had to go to the Baldwin schools. They tried to explain to me that if my kids would sleep in the garage, they could go to the Rockville Centre schools.
I did 90-some-odd appearances on the Sullivan show. In the middle of that, I started doing Garry Moore. What made the suburban thing work was the anger. I'm innately angry. What I was saying was what everybody else wanted to say about this suburban community -- instead of keeping up with the Joneses, it was keeping with the Cohens. I was talking about something I lived every day.
Q. So you were just poking fun at the manners and mores of your neighbors.
A. The competition between the neighbors -- who had the best lawns. That's when I started screaming about the crab grass. What's wrong with crab grass and just cutting it short? They put out petitions in the neighborhood; I was being burned in effigy. I said, "Leave me alone, I'm not trying to compete with my neighbors."
Q. So in a way your career paralleled the growth and development of Long Island. What about what happened to the houses people lived in? The houses started out pretty basic, like in Levittown, and then --
A. Finished basements. I said, it's so wonderful to walk into a house with an eight-foot ceiling and a basement with a four-foot ceiling and spend an evening like Quasimodo or Toulouse-Lautrec. This started to take off, with my exposure on the Sullivan show. Then everybody started giving me a title: "Here he is, the uncrowned king of the crab-grass set."
Then they started adding rooms on. That was the next thing. The open porch became a closed-in porch. The garage became a study. I had a $22,000 house and lived there for 11 years. We figured out I put $50,000 into that $22,000 house. And you know how much I sold it for? Twenty-two thousand dollars. That's absolutely true.
Q. And then you moved.
A. Then I moved into Oscar Hammerstein's quote-unquote mansion, which I'm in 40 years now. According to the press, if you have a four-bedroom house, it's a mansion. Six bedrooms, it's an estate. I'm looking at my rose gardens right now. My rose gardens are on more land than my house in Rockville Centre was on.
You know, when I was growing up in the tenements of New York, there were eight million kids on every corner. Now, all of a sudden, I had to take my kids with a station wagon to find someone to play with.
These are routines I did 40 years ago.
Q. But it sounds as if you really liked it on Long Island.
A. I had the excitement of New York and within the hour I had Bel Air and Beverly Hills right here on Long Island. I played tennis and golf for so many years. I had everything I wanted, and I had the city. That's why I never moved. It was a conscious choice because my business was moving to California. I said I was the best comedian in New York by default. California, half of it is burning down, the other half is choking to death.
Q. If your office was in the city, why didn't you just move there after your kids were grown?
A. I never chose to live in the city. I think that living in the city, you have the opportunity to be more isolated. You can live in the city and not know the guy next door. There's something about living in Long Island that's about a sense of community.
It's never been a free ride. You make a peace with yourself, you weigh the pluses versus the minuses. There are more pluses here. I lived in the city, and I couldn't wait to get to the suburbs. All my kids wanted to live in Greenwich Village and live on a fire escape, which is what I wanted to escape. I remember taking my son through Williamsburg, the old neighborhood. Here I was in a limousine car, and he said, "Gee, Dad, you were such a lucky kid." I looked at him, I was stunned.
Q. But even Long Island has a weird, dark side, no?
A. Every place does, but you have such an expanse of economics, of culture here. You go from the bowling league to the James Joyce club. It's so diverse. Don't forget, a lot of people forget how big Long Island is. It starts at Queens and it ends at Montauk. It's a state, practically.
Q. So how do you explain the barge, which sailed up and down the East Coast looking for a place to dump trash from Islip?
A. You know the expression "only in America"? Only in Long Island is garbage worth more than in its original form. You know what it reminds me of in a more dramatic sense? When we became aware of the Woodstock generation, the drug problem, they had a lot of meetings and everybody became so civic minded. They said we've got to build these halfway houses -- halfway to your house, not my house. People in Great Neck said build them in Roslyn, people in Roslyn said build them in Hewlett, and then the temples said the churches.
Q. A few years ago, a lot of comedians made a lot out of the whole Amy Fisher-Joey Buttafuocco thing. How do you explain it?
A. I don't have any bodywork done.
I don't even think about it as a Long Island happening. It's just something you read about. Somehow, I can't relate. First of all, it started out tragic because a woman was nearly killed, and then it got funny. You see this guy, and all of a sudden he's making pictures in Hollywood. I could understand if he went on the wrestling programs. I could never identify.
And you know something? I'm sure that every suburban area has its Mary Jos. You go to another town, you read about a schoolteacher having a baby by a student. No place is immune to weirdos.
Q. What kind of advice would you give people who are thinking of coming to Long Island?
A. I've been living here for 50 years. There's no greater ad. I'm the great commercial. In spite of everything I said, I'm still here and I'm not about to go down to Palm Beach and wear out my velvet slippers. I'm going to end up walking slow in Long Island.
Q. In all this you haven't said a word about the Hamptons.
A. The Hamptons don't belong to Long Island. To me, all the people I see at Elaine's and the "21" club and Le Cirque, Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday, I really don't want to see after a three-and-a-half-hour drive on Saturday and Sunday. You go to a cocktail party in New York on Wednesday, then you go out on Friday in the Hamptons. Same people, only not as well dressed.
And there's a game they play in the Hamptons: how long did it take you to get here? Two hours. Two hours if they shoot you out of a cannon, you lying you-know-what.
The Hamptons have been taking it on the chin for years, but rightfully so. Where else can you buy a loaf of bread for $41?

GRAPHIC: Photo: Alan King, the comedian, outside his Tudor-style home on the North Shore. (Vic DeLucia/The New York Times)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Interview

SECTION: Section 11; Page 7; Column 1; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 1477 words

HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Office Buildings;
Some Owners Are Finding It's a Good Time to Sell

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
THE 42-story office building at 1185 Avenue of the Americas, between 46th and 47th Streets, has to be considered a trophy property because of its location, its roll of high quality tenants and its direct underground connections to Rockefeller Center and several subway lines.
In addition, the leases on large portions of the building are due to expire over the next few years, with the new leases at higher current market rates replacing those signed when the real estate market was soft and tenants commanded low rates and big concessions packages. It is almost completely rented, with only one 25,000-square-foot block of space available, and rents are expected to keep rising in Manhattan for several more years because of the booming economy and demand for space. 
 Which is why the family that controls the building has put it up for sale.
Indeed, with the run-up in values over the last few years, some investors who bought distressed properties in the early part of this decade are looking to lock in their profits by selling or refinancing their buildings.
As James D. Kuhn, the president of Newmark Realty Advisors, put it recently, "We are advising our clients that if they are not long term holders of real estate, now is a great time to take a few chips off the table."
He noted that office vacancies are declining, interest rates are low, capital is available and capital gains tax treatment is less onerous with the repeal of New York State's so-called Cuomo tax of 10 percent on sales of over $1 million and the lowering of Federal tax rates, making transactions more attractive.
The state gains tax was considered particularly onerous, real estate executives said, because it was triggered by events not normally taxable, such as the acquisition of property by an investment trust shielded from Federal taxes.
Mr. Kuhn said it would be foolish for a property owner to try to wait to sell at the very peak of the market, because peaks are hard to spot and the downside is steep. "When the market corrects, it corrects quickly," he said.
"Yes, this is a good time to sell, but it is also a good time to buy, depending on your investment objectives," said Jeffrey Cooper, a senior director at Cushman & Wakefield's financial services group. "With capital available at 7 percent, it is a good time to sell. But with rents headed up at good buildings in midtown and downtown, it is a good time to invest in Class A and high B buildings if you are a long term investor."
"There is no one right answer about when to buy and sell," said Charles L. Goldenberg, the president of the Sylvan Lawrence Company. "Indeed, there is a theory that well-located real estate should never be sold, but passed down to subsequent generations."
However, he conceded that this was a luxury theory and that harsh reality often does not permit such smooth transfers. He said his company would probably have not sold the 128 properties in Mr. Lawrence's portfolio, including the block-square former Port Authority building at 111 Eighth Avenue, except for litigation arising from the Lawrence estate.
The 1.1 million-square-foot building at 1185 Avenue of the Americas that is now up for sale was purchased in 1977 by a private investors' syndicate, whose general partners are Peter and Anthony Malkin, and it has been managed and marketed since by the family's operating company, W & M Properties.
Starting in 1995, the owners began a major upgrade to the building, replacing the roof, installing new controls on the high-rise elevators and refurbishing all the elevator cabs. The lobby was upgraded and the bathrooms were revised to meet standards for the disabled and the facade was examined and repaired as needed.
Behind the scenes, the air-conditioning and electrical capacity were increased and fiber-optic telecommunications lines installed. Although it was erected in 1972 -- to be the headquarters of the company that is now Westpoint Stevens -- W & M is marketing it as Class A space because of the improvements.
Anthony Malkin, the president of W & M, said the decision to offer the building for sale was strategic. Because there is a clear opportunity to increase income from rents, he said, the building should be attractive to real estate investment trusts and other institutional investors seeking secure income. "We anticipate that people will pay us a portion of the value they will be able to create," he said.
The biggest leases are expiring between 2003 and 2005, W & M officials said, with 14 1/2 floors becoming available for releasing.
SELLING the building, which could fetch several hundred dollars a square foot if recent sales are any indication, will enable the Malkin family to redeploy its capital after syndicate partners have been paid their proportionate shares.
"For the last 10 years we have been buying distressed assets, fixing them up, renting them and financing them," he said. In the process, W & M has built up an in-house engineering, construction, acquisition and finance capability, which would not be of much use in a building that has already been upgraded and is almost fully occupied.
"We can take the capital from this building and invest it in five or six other properties," he said.
Although sales prices of top quality office buildings have recovered sharply in recent years, they are still below those of the 1980's, said Daniel J. McNulty, the president of Rockwood Realty Associates, which is marketing 1185. "Nine years ago, Class A space was selling for about $400 a square foot," he said. "Now it is around $300 a foot not adjusted for inflation, so we are well south of the past peak."
He also noted that the lack of construction for most of this decade would provide support for rental rates and building sales that was not present in the last market cycle. During that time, 30 million square feet of office space was added to the Manhattan market, he said. "That is not going to happen in the next 15-year cycle."
But actually selling a Manhattan skyscraper is trickier than making the decision to sell. There are questions about the ability of a prospective buyer to obtain financing and actually close the transaction, regardless of how big a number may be initially proposed. Should an owner demand cash and face capital gains tax? Or would selling to a REIT in exchange for rights to shares be preferable because it postpones taxes -- but leaves the sellers vulnerable to declines in a REIT's stock price? Swapping for another property might avoid both pitfalls, but does not meet the objective of turning real estate into liquid capital.
Nevertheless, "a lot more people are talking about tax-free exchanges than in a long time," observed Kevin F. Haggarty, executive managing director of Insignia/ESG's capital advisers group.
Mr. Malkin made his thinking clear last week by issuing an announcement that investors at 1185 are interested in making a large number of acquisitions quickly to take advantage of tax-deferred exchange provisions of the law.
Another way of realizing cash from a building is refinancing, an alternative that allows an owner to capitalize on the value of a property, but adds a big obligation to the lender that might be hard to sustain if the market weakens and rents start to slip. That, essentially, is what happened at Rockefeller Center, real estate executives say. The companies operating the center were unable to meet debt payments and filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy in 1995. A group led by Tishman Speyer Properties bought control of the buildings east of Avenue of the Americas in 1996.
The recent weakness in the stock price of many REIT's may curb their free-spending ways, industry executives say. "The softness of REIT stocks has made them less aggressive," said Howard L. Michaels, the chairman of the Carleton Group, a brokerage and financing company. "Six months ago, if a high-profile property was offered to sales, the REIT's would get it. Now individuals can compete with REIT's."
Part of the reason REIT's are reluctant to issue the tax-free operating partnership units that are convertible into common stock after a period of time is they don't like the current terms of such a transaction. . "REIT's don't want to do O.P. units these days." Mr. Haggarty said. "They view their stocks as undervalued and they don't want to pay too much."
Adding the risk of stock market fluctuations to the usual complications of doing real estate transactions means that added brain power is needed to evaluate proposals, Mr. Malkin said. "The reason we went with Rockwood is their analytic capability and the capabilities of their associates," he said. "If some REIT we have never heard of comes in, we need to be able to analyze it to figure out whether its stock price is likely to decline 30 to 40 percent in the next year."

HEADLINE: For Every New Long Islander, Familiar Hopes and Dreams

BYLINE: By MARCELLE S. FISCHLER 

 BODY:
WHEN David Grunberger first broached the possibility of moving to New York three years ago, his wife, Claudia, wasn't exactly thrilled. She had recently relocated from London to Antwerp. And she hated the month they spent in a Manhattan apartment hotel. Her husband, however, a Belgian businessman, was tired of his trans-Atlantic commute every other month. And their future, he said, was in the United States.
As it turned out, changing countries was easy. Figuring out where to live in the metropolitan area was not. 
 On a visit to New York a year and a half ago, the couple rented a car and toured neighborhoods in Westchester, Queens and Nassau Counties. This weekend, after renting a home for 10 months to make sure they liked the area, they are moving into the five-bedroom Tudor on half an acre that they bought near a park in Great Neck.
The Grunbergers are among scores of recent arrivals, a polyglot, group of homeowners seeking a suburban setting in which to raise their children.
"I didn't know what the suburbs looked like," said Mrs. Grunberger, 29, who has also lived in Paris, Venice and Florence. "When I saw Great Neck, I loved it. It was so close to the city. Great Neck has a life of its own. The main street is buzzing. London has similar high streets. I liked the Jewish feeling. It reminded me of Israel."
Mrs. Grunberger's early experiences mirrored those of many newcomers. She joined the library, enrolled her daughter in nursery school, checked out supermarkets, got lost on the parkways, and -- not knowing a soul except for her landlord and real estate agent -- called her mother in London to complain.
Welcome to Long Island. From entrepreneurs to postal workers and retirees, newcomers to the more than 260 communities in Nassau and Suffolk counties expressed personal, occupational and economic reasons for choosing to settle where they have. Some could live almost anywhere but opted for proximity to family or based their choice on schools, shopping, beaches, parks, commuting and taxes.
Following the natural pattern of neighborhoods, where empty nesters downsized, younger folks moved up and in.
The Grunbergers, for example, bought their 4,200-square-foot home from a couple in their 60's who relocated to a townhouse in White Plains to be closer to the only one of their four grown children still in the same state.
According to the Census Bureau, about 1,303,700 people lived in Nassau County in 1997, an increase of 1.3 percent since the 1990 census. The figures for Suffolk County were about 1,362,600, an increase of 3.1 percent. People from Asia and Spanish-speaking countries represented the largest increases in both counties.
"The demand for residential housing is extremely strong," said Dr. Pearl M. Kamer, chief economist for the Long Island Association, the Island's major business group.
"The market for luxury housing on Long Island is particularly strong. The housing market has changed dramatically in the last 12 to 18 months. It's a seller's market, where in the past 10 years it was a buyer's market. We know young families are replacing retirees. Some are undoubtedly coming from the city as they become more affluent and move out here."
Changes in the tax code, allowing older residents who settled on Long Island in the 1950's and 60's to shelter more on the gains from the sale of their homes, Dr. Kamer said, "has certainly unfrozen the market and allowed younger families to move in, retaining the people who grew up here."
Homes in upscale neighborhoods, she continued, are being bought up and demolished except for a standing wall or chimney and rebuilt up to the lot line. And estates are being built on former farm acreage, with Wall Streeters commuting on a daily basis to the East End.
Retirees, however, Dr. Kamer said, are not relocating to the sunbelt, but moving to local retirement, life-care and new assisted-living developments. And low mortgage rates are causing an influx of young families who can afford to buy more home.
In July, Kwang Min, an entrepreneur with a shoe business in Manhattan and a shipping enterprise at Kennedy Airport, moved from a three-bedroom co-op in Flushing to Port Washington in search of a topnotch education for his two children, ages 10 and 14. In Queens, the businessman said, his children went to Catholic school. What he spent on tuition there, he said, he wll apply to his $500 monthly property tax bill on a $460,000, four-bedroom house with a two-car garage on a 60 by 120 lot and send the children to public school.
Mr. Min, whose extended family is still in Korea, learned about the community through a friend. When he bought the property, he said, he had seen the town harbor. He had no clue, however, as to the best shopping or parks. He was depending on his real- estate broker -- coupled with daily jogs and bicycle rides -- to learn his way around.
For his weekly soccer game, however, Mr. Min still goes back to Flushing.
"The Asian population is really growing," said Ran Sook Cho, an associate broker at Coldwell Banker Sammis in Port Washington. "Mainly Chinese, Indian and Korean doctors and lawyers and computer business and top professionals, because the schools are excellent and the train line is wonderful. A lot of people move to Flushing and then to Bayside and then Douglaston and Little Neck and then finally settle in Port Washington, Searingtown and Manhasset Hills. Some who can afford it move to Sands Point and Brookville and Old Westbury and to gated communities. If they can't afford it, they go to New Hyde Park."
It was the love of family and friends that drew Patricia and John Carusi back to Long Island after 10 years in Texas when Mr. Carusi, 62, retired last year.
While they had planned to move to New Hampshire, where they already owned a second home -- and had even moved most of their belongings to the mountaintop property -- their daughter convinced them it was time to come back to Long Island.
"If my husband hadn't been transferred, we never would have left New York to begin with," said Mrs. Carusi, 60, who was born in Manhattan, raised in Queens and moved with her husband to a colonial in Commack in 1967. "We expected todie in that house. We all had our friends and we were all established in our community. To transplant lifelong New Yorkers like we were was quite an adjustment. It's a different culture in the South."
The five-bedroom, four-and-a-half-bath home on the fairway of a golf course that they had in Texas, however, could not be duplicated for under seven figures on the Island, more than triple their selling price. So the Carusis downsized on their return to a two- bedroom condominium a mile from their daughter's Kings Park home. They are still trying to figure out what to do with all the furniture.
"I missed our family and our friends, that was the biggest thing," Mrs. Carusi said.
Harold and Cheryl Spector were pretty familiar with the area when they bought a $215,000 split-level house in North Bellmore last fall. Although the couple had lived in Bay Terrace almost their entire lives, Mrs. Spector, 32, has an uncle and cousins in the South Shore neighborhood. Mr. Spector's brother lives a mile away in East Meadow.
"We thought it would be a better life to raise our child," said Mr. Spector, 39, a customer service analyst for the United States Postal Service. Their son, Evan, was born a month after they moved in.
"This is one of the best school districts," said Mrs. Spector, who checked out school ratings in the neighborhood. "East Meadow was our first choice, but we just couldn't find the house we were looking for."
Mrs. Spector wanted multiple bathrooms. Her husband wanted an entranceway. "We looked in Plainview but the taxes were higher and you get much more here for the price."
"Anything closer to Queens we couldn't afford," said Mrs. Spector, who used to live five minutes from her job as a third-grade teacher and now drives an hour each way. "Anything further out made the commute too long."
Besides, her husband said, this house was close to Roosevelt Field and Massapequa malls, where they had always liked to shop. Using contractors they found in local newspapers, they painted, stripped floors and started replacing windows, adding a glass circle top over the front door. Doing work on the house, they have noticed, is a sure way to meet the neighbors.
Mrs. Spector visited a local mothers' center. They joined the library, found a park nearby with swings for the baby and went to the town pool. Just strolling the neighborhood with the baby in the carriage, Mrs. Spector found friends.
"Our neighbors are new, too," she said. "They just moved in a few weeks after us. We stay in the backyard and talk."
Since they moved in, four homes in the neighborhood have turned over."The old people are moving out and younger people are moving in," she said.

LENGTH: 1209 words

HEADLINE: Coffee's Hot, Bread's Fresh, the Bookstore Goes Social

BYLINE: By JACKIE FITZPATRICK 

 BODY:
EACH morning, not long after waking up, Sharmila Sen heads to her home away from home or, rather, her secondary office. She goes to the Atticus Book Store-Cafe on Chapel Street in New Haven, in search of a strong, hot cup of coffee and quiet.
"It's a great place to go when I have a lot of papers to grade. Their hours are perfect for someone like me -- open early, open late," she said. She often returns to the store in the evening, to find a good new book or meet friends. 
 Ms. Sen is a graduate student working on her Ph.D in English at Yale University. On a recent afternoon, she shared a late lunch with Helen Lawrence, also a Yale Ph.D. candidate and a frequent bookstore visitor. She comes "to be surrounded by books. I love that. And it's a relaxing, fun place."
They are not alone. A bookstore is no longer simply to pick up the latest techno-thriller by Tom Clancy or mystery by Mary Higgins Clark. They are places for people to sip espresso and nibble lemon bars, to hear big-name authors or aspiring folk singers, to join a monthly book club, to take their children for a pajama party complete with bedtime stories and milk and cookies.
Bookstores have become social gathering places in the 1990's, places where, as Virginia Meyrat, a manager at Borders Books in Stamford tells it, people can "browse around in search of a great book with their cookies and coffee . . . and teen-agers can sit around together on the rug, talk and read."
Some bookstores remain steadfastly devoted solely to books but many others, both independents and large chains, have branched out.
"We weren't the first bookstore to have a cafe but we were one of the earlier ones and we did it because we felt food and coffee and books go well together," said Diarmud Kennedy, manager of Atticus. "Now we have a bakery so people can pick up a book and some fresh baked bread. We're like a human fishbowl here on Chapel Street with our big, wide windows. People like looking in and people inside like watching people outside. I think people like to have a place to go where they feel welcome."
At the Whistle Stop Book Shop in Stratford, children arrive with piles of books they've just read, place them on the counter for the store owner, Bonnie Lee, and dash off to the children's section where they plop themselves on the floor.
There they hunt through shelves of used books. They receive store credit for each book they return and often leave with a paper bag full of Babysitter Club books, Judy Blume or Beverly Cleary. "We want children to be readers, that's so important," Mrs. Lee said. Youngsters can also try their hand at acting in plays from the American Girl series or participate in numerous other events at the shop.
The Whistle Stop Book Shop, tucked in near the train station in Stratford, is full of used and new books for adults and young people to explore. A grandparent might show up with a grandchild, both leaving with a few books. "We have lots of older people who can go through a mystery a day and they might not have the money to buy new, so they come in with what they've read and then get more," Mrs. Lee said.
Antiques dot the store's landscape and a bench with a quilt draped over it beckons a reader who thumbs through a few pages before making a selection. Mrs. Lee owns the store with her daughter, Pam Robertson, both book lovers. Customers have started several reading groups including a mystery and classic group. Recently the owners held a coffeehouse for local poets, singers and comics to showcase their talents. "We feel like a real part of the community here and we love that," Mrs. Lee said.
Suzy Staubach, manager of the General Books Division at the UConn Coop in Storrs, said the profile of a typical customer at a bookstore "fits the profile of a shoplifter at another store."
As she explained it, "They hang around. They browse. They pick up a book, read a bit, put it down, try another. But we love that. That's what a good customer does."
While many customers at the Coop are UConn students, in search of that text for economics 101 or biochemistry, "It's actually a very cosmopolitan place. We have a big international community and people from our region love books." Ms. Stauback said.
Early on, recognizing that people like to be connected to the authors they love, the bookstore began offering readings and signings. One of the most popular readings was by Arundhati Roy, the author of "The God of Small Things." It was slated for a day after school was out, in the middle of the day. But the place was packed. "The Indian community turned out in large numbers, as did people who had fallen in love with her book," Ms. Staubach said.
In recent years, the Coop has added a cafe and it continues to build on its signings and readings. On Sept. 17 there will be a party for Wally Lamb, author of "I Know This Much Is True," a bestseller and that became his second book selected by Oprah Winfrey's influential book club.
Mr. Lamb, who still lives in the area, wrote his first book, "She's Come Undone," in the wee hours of the morning just next door at the UConn library. He retains his status as a kind of local hero.
The R.J. Julia bookstore in Madison will also turn its attention to local authors with its first Connecticut Authors Day on Oct. 24. Roxanne Coady, owner of R.J. Julia, said she's always felt it is important to bring the writer to his or her readers. Three personalities who had drawn huge crowds at previous signings were Don Imus of WFAN Radio; Frank McCourt, author of "Angela's Ashes," and Anna Quindlen, the best-selling author and former New York Times columnist. This fall, among others, Fannie Flagg will sign her new book, "Welcome to the World Baby Girl."
Because of their ties to people in their communities, many bookstores have helped build a following for a book. Mr. Kennedy, for example, said he's been on a mission to promote William Maxwell's book "So Long, See You Tomorrow."
"When you feel passionate about a book you can share it with people who love to read and that's such a good feeling," Mr. Kennedy said.
At Borders in Stamford, poets and singers have a chance to build an audience at regular sessions in in its Espresso Bar. Poets sometimes take their words, jotted down on lined paper or just off the computer printer, and share them with listeners. "That's been very popular," Ms. Meyrat said.
It hasn't hurt bookstores, several managers have chuckled, that America is in the midst of a love affair with coffee and the java goes well with poetry and song.
"I really like it when I walk around, helping people find a book, and I see other people sitting on the floor or walking around, tapping their feet to music we have on in the background or talking to a friend over coffee," Ms. Meyrat said. "It's the kind of atmosphere that I think brings people back and back."
At Atticus, the aroma of fresh bread and cappucino mingled in the air. Robin Greenwood, a graduate student at Harvard, was in town to see his girlfriend, a Yale student. They were meeting in the bookstore's cafe. "I like the fact that they have big tables and you can spread everything out and get some work done," he said, digging into a piece of cake.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Teale Caliendo, a comedian, holding forth at the Whistle Stop Book Shop in Stratford. Elsewhere, readings and book signings are increasingly common, as are refreshments. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

SECTION: Section 14WC; Page 6; Column 3; Westchester Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1242 words

HEADLINE: With Transfers, Relocation Business Thrives

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER 

 BODY:
WHEN Ann Wheatley learned that her husband's promotion would mean leaving London for New York, she immediately started looking for houses in the New York area by way of the Internet.
"I came across the Web site of a local realtor, Houlihan/Lawrence, and printed out a couple of their houses that looked interesting," Mrs. Wheatley said. "But after we arrived in New York it was actually a realtor in New Jersey, introduced to us by Saville, our London realtor, who put us together with the Houlihan/Lawrence Company in Westchester." 
 Because Mr. Wheatley now has a permanent position with an American company, with headquarters in Manhattan, Mrs. Wheatley said the couple sold their Victorian house in England and have their one-bedroom apartment on the Thames River in London on the market.
"I saw a number of houses in April, when I was here for a long weekend," she recalled. "Actually, there wasn't much on the market. Prices were high, but no higher than at home, so we weren't shocked."
Eventually, the search was narrowed to two houses, and the Wheatleys finally bought a five-bedroom, three-and-a-half-bath, contemporary with a view on a pretty acre, for $570,000 in Briarcliff Manor.
"It's very lovely and reminds us a bit of England, and the commute is good," Mrs. Wheatley said. "Our first look at the area was when we took a train trip up the Hudson to Cold Spring. A friend advised us on taking a jaunt outside New York City to see some of the countryside, and we thought the Hudson River surroundings were beautiful."
During the lean years marked by corporate downsizing in the late 80's and early 90's, the relocation business was dormant. Executive moves like that by the Wheatleys were few and far between. Now, thanks to a robust economy and an influx of new companies in the region, the relocation market is thriving, despite the record-breaking high prices of houses.
"Yes, people who are being transferred from lower-priced areas are experiencing sticker shock when they see what houses cost here, but we're prepared," said Elizabeth N. Demey, director of Corporate Services for Houlihan/Lawrence Relocation Management in Armonk. "Housing costs and cost-of-living differences are explained to transferees, as are various choices of communities, life style and commuting distances. Following an introductory telephone interview, we match each relocating employee with an agent in one of our 22 offices."
One of the largest relocation moves in recent years involves the corporate consolidation of about 200 employees of Starwood Hotels and Resorts Worldwide to White Plains, where the company is temporarily situated while it looks for permanent headquarters in the Westchester-Fairfield area.
"We were competing with the large relocation divisions of the national franchisee real estate companies across the nation for this account, which we've been wooing since we first heard Starwood was considering a move to Westchester earlier this year," Stephen R. Meyers, president of Houlihan/Lawrence, said. "We were absolutely delighted that they chose to go with a local company. We're members of RELO, which is the national network of independent real estate firms providing relocation services, so our clients around the country can deal directly with a realtor in any location, eliminating the middleman."
Ms. Demey said Starwood employees from Boston, Seattle, Phoenix and Atlanta have already bought homes in Westchester, Putnam and Fairfield Counties.
"In Fairfield, we work with the William Pitt agency," she said. "To date, 24 families have either rented or bought houses, and we hope to have all 200 settled by the end of the year."
Initially, the out-of-towners in their hunt for housing were dismayed not only by the high price tags on houses but also by the high real estate taxes that went with them, Ms. Demey reported. "It is understandable, coming from a place like Seattle or Atlanta where the dollar buys a lot more house," she said.
Yet, she observed, within a 10-mile radius and less than 45 minutes from White Plains, houses for $300,000 and under do exist.
"That's the average price this relocating group is comfortable with," Ms. Demey said. "And there are some good buys in this price category in northern Westchester and southern Putnam Counties, under 45 minutes from White Plains, but some buyers, like a couple who formerly lived outside of Boston, have had to downsize. They wanted to buy a single-family home but ended up with a town house in the Brewster area, which was what they could afford."
Resale values are very important to transferees, Ms. Demey emphasized. "Most of our corporate clients insist on having a prepurchase appraisal," she said. "They want to be sure the house isn't overpriced and that it will retain its value. Moving is never easy, but relocation is even harder than a regular move, so we supply a one-stop shop for transferees through our contacts with career counselors, moving companies, mortgage companies and the like. We schedule meetings with schools and provide up-to-date school reports. What's more, we keep in contact with the family from three to six months after the move to see how they've settled in."
The company is working with a dozen major corporations and multinational companies in the Westchester-Fairfield area that have operations in various parts of the world, Ms. Demey said. "The U.S. is the top destination for global relocation," she said. "We're seeing increasing numbers of transferees from Europe and South America. And in order to make the move easier for them, we have multilingual agents who accompany the transferee and the family on their house-hunting tour, acting as a translator and giving them assistance with schools, bank accounts, grocery shopping and whatever else is needed."
Before moving, transferees going or coming from overseas receive a package full of information like local real estate practices, housing options, a home buyer's handbook, information on utilities, a measurement-conversion chart, local airport information, educational and cultural opportunities, places of interest, climate and seasonal facts and a suggested reading list.
The company is working with a corporation that is transferring a number of employees to the United States from Brazil. Anita Kowarick, an international sales agent in the Bronxville office who speaks fluent Portuguese, French and Spanish, recently helped relocate a Brazilian family.
"I've lived in Brazil, although I was born here to a Brazilian mother and a Norwegian father," Ms. Kowarick said. "I draw on my experience in Brazil to help transferees by recognizing the cultural differences and explaining American customs to them in a way they will understand."
Before she started the Brazilian family on their house-hunting quest, Ms. Kowarick escorted their two daughters, 11 and 14, on a tour of schools.
"Schools were a priority, so we looked at private schools and public schools, and eventually the girls selected a good public school," she said. "And then we found the family a house in the area. They were two very frightened girls when they arrived, anxious about the schools, but as it turned out, the thing they were most frightened of was the cold. They didn't know how they would deal with it.
"They cheered up quickly when I told them, Not to worry, the houses here were heated. You see, in Brazil they are not."

HEADLINE: In the Region/New Jersey;
Englewood Lures Investors to Battle West Side Blight

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

DATELINE: ENGLEWOOD

 BODY:
THE wave of redevelopment that rolled across downtown Englewood during the last decade has kept the blocks along Palisade Avenue, east of the unused railroad tracks, a vibrant shopping district. But the activity was never fully successful on the West Side, which still shows signs of deterioration and blight.
Rather than leave that neighborhood's future to fate, this Bergen County city started a campaign three years ago to turn the West Side around and in the process attract national retailers, who have long been unwilling to cross the tracks. Since then it has invested $3.5 million in city, state and Federal grants and loans and $1 million more in private money The money was used to buy run-down houses from absentee landlords, rehabilitate them and sell them; increase patrols aimed at ridding the streets of crime; remove graffiti, and spruce up the 30-acre MacKay Park, among other projects. 
 "We had to fix the quality-of-life issues before we could set the stage for economic development," said Peter M. Beronio, the city's director of community services.
By doing so, the city has succeeded in attracting Group U.S.A., a national discount women's clothing chain, to the West Side. During the next year, Group U.S.A., based in Secaucus, is scheduled to develop a 31,000-square-foot, two-story building and open a 6,000-square-foot store. A tenant, Mikasa, a china and flatware retailer, is to occupy 5,500 square feet. Group U.S.A. hopes to find a third retailer and lease 15,000 square feet of second-story professional office space, said Mahyar Amirsaleh, president of Group U.S.A.
Before the $2.5 million Group U.S.A. project gets under way, the city will demolish seven vacant and dilapidated storefronts on the site at James Street and West Palisade Avenue. Demolition began on Thursday.
The hope is that the private investment that has eluded the neighborhood for years will finally take hold, said Robert L. Benecke, Englewood's City Manager and chief financial officer. He said he is optimistic because the city is discussing two other West Side projects with developers. One involves housing above retail shops; the other focuses on an entertainment complex.
Edward T. Baczewski, who has run Herbert's Camera House on East Palisade Avenue for 25 years, said, "If you entered the city on the West Side it gives the wrong image and so, if nothing was done, all businesses would suffer."
Mr. Baczewski, who is also vice president of the city's Economic Development Corporation, said there is some concern about increased competition. But bringing in "high-quality merchants" to the West Side could strengthen, not hurt, existing businesses, as past competition has proved, he said.
Albert King, a former New Jersey Nets basketball player who has run a Wendy's franchise on West Palisade Avenue for three years, agreed, saying: "Competition is good. It keeps everyone on their toes. Putting in the Group U.S.A. project a half-block away can't do anything but help."
Englewood's downtown comprises six blocks along Palisade Avenue and the side streets in between. East of the tracks, there are about 200 restaurants and stores, including antiques shops and boutiques, like Ann Taylor. Occupancy is high, with a vacancy rate of less than 1 percent, Mr. Beronio said, and rents average $28 a square foot.
On the West Side, the streetscape is a mix of houses and shops. It contains 100 stores, including long-established retailers like Eagle Paint, as well as the John Harms Center for the Arts. Still, the lack of investment in the last decade, along with drug trafficking, repair shops forbidden in residentially zoned areas and the poor condition of the housing, particularly in and around Armory Street, city officials said, has kept the retail streets pocked with empty stores. Rents are low, averaging $13 a square foot.
"Improving the West Side benefits the entire city in terms of both increased quality of life and tax revenue," Mr. Benecke said. Every $1 million invested will generate $30,000 in annual tax revenue, he said. He added that an additional $5 million in public and private money, including Group U.S.A.'s investment, will be funneled into the area, for a total of $9.5 million. The additional money will be used to add parking, landscaping and street lights and transform a worn-out lot off Palisade Avenue into three two-family houses that the city will sell for around $170,000.
THE city's campaign reflects the approach New Jersey encourages through its Neighborhood Preservation Program, which provides money to municipalities to rebuild entire neighborhoods rather than make quick-fix investments in a single project. Rebuilding a neighborhood "takes more than bricks and mortar," said Anthony Cancro, director of the Division of Housing and Community Resources of the State Department of Community Affairs, which administers the program.
Englewood is one of 130 communities that receives money from the program. But the city used the $400,000 it received to attract $1.3 million in other public and private money to improve the neighborhood, Mr. Cancro said.
Previous attempts to revive the West Side have fallen short of expectations. In the early 90's, a grubby, seven-acre wedge along North Van Brunt Street was transformed into a 115,000-square-foot shopping center called Palisade Court, but the $25 million project was not extensive enough to lure other private investors to the neighborhood.
As a result, plans to attract private developers to build a housing project in the troubled Armory Street area never materialized. In 1995, the plans, which envisioned 400 condominium units on 11 acres, were abandoned.
That failure prompted Englewood to redevelop the Armory Street neighborhood. At that point, in 1995, the city had the financial stability and political will to pursue such a plan, Mr. Benecke said.
The city purchased 5 of the 17 one- and two-family houses on Armory Street from absentee landlords for $85,000 each. It worked with contractors, who invested their own money, to rehabilitate those houses. The houses were then sold for $160,000 each and city and the contractors reaped the profits.
The city also helped four homeowners secure state money to renovate their homes. Now the landlords of the remaining eight houses, many of them vacant, are sprucing them up for lease or sale.
The city received a $200,000 grant from Bergen County to put in new sidewalks, landscaping and lighting in the neighborhood, and now has received another $700,000 for the rest of the West Side.
Those improvements influenced Group U.S.A.'s decision to proceed with its project. "We are taking a chance going in there," said Mr. Amirsaleh, the retailer's president. "But we believe in the potential of the area and the city's no-nonsense vision of its future." The site also provides the company with the Bergen County location it sought "to be closer to our customer base," he said, noting that working with the city also made it easier to secure building permits.
Its partnership with the city also saved the company money because Englewood set up a special 15-year land lease arrangement. Under it, the city will lease the land under the new building from the landowner, John Krantz, for 12 1/2 years, paying $65,000 for the first three years with increases tied to the rate of inflation for the remaining 9 1/2 years. When the lease expires, the owner will donate the land to the city, which in turn will sell it to Group U.S.A. for $1.
The city will also improve the drainage canal under the buildings, Mr. Beronio said, noting that the city's payback will come from increased taxes. The property owner now pays $13,000 in annual taxes; once the project is complete it will generate up to $100,000 a year in taxes, Mr. Beronio said.
Mr. Amirsaleh said that without the city's financial incentives the project would have cost $4 million, making it too expensive, since current rents are too low to provide an adequate return. "With it, our risk is reasonable," he said. The building is expected to open next August.
When the city's campaign is complete, Mr. Beronio said, "It will go a long way in erasing the tracks as a psychological barrier to shopping on the West Side."
