SECTION: Section 9; Page 1; Column 1; Style Desk 

LENGTH: 1225 words

HEADLINE: VIEW;
One Riddle Fits All

BYLINE: By ALEX KUCZYNSKI 

 BODY:
LET me begin by saying that I'm not a Lilly Pulitzer kind of woman. But in July, I found myself on Martha's Vineyard with an hour on my hands, checked out the competition and thought, "When in Rome . . . "
I followed a group of women with sun-worn faces and plaid hair bands into the closest boutique, and there I found it: a short, flirty, flowered Lilly Pulitzer, printed with roses and frogs and prancing children. The fit was miraculous, as if a team of steely-eyed couture seamstresses had spent weeks plotting each angle and line and dip of my body. I was ready to unburden myself of $130 when I stepped out of the dress and saw the label. The magical phrase I read there changed my life: size 6. 
 I had unwittingly stumbled upon the holy grail of womanhood. Sucking in my stomach, I decided to go for a size 4 plaid skirt. The headband set listened as I struggled, wriggled and zipped. And as a soft white light suffused the dressing room and the distant strains of a divine choir (actually, it was Hanson) seemed to drift past the swinging wicker doors, I took a long breath. Victory -- size 4 -- was mine.
Women's apparel sizing is eternally mystifying. I am a broad-shouldered athlete, close to six feet tall and weighing many, many dozens of pounds. How I fit into a 6, the size of wraithlike supermodels and desiccated society waifs who eat grapes for lunch, or a positively puny size 4, is a riddle wrapped in an enigma wrapped in, well, a size 4 wrap dress.
And it's become a lightning-rod issue for American clothing manufacturers. I described the incredible shrinking act to Dr. Lenda Jo Anderson, a professor of consumer affairs at Auburn University in Alabama, who at the behest of the Commerce Department studies size in women's clothing.
"We define ourselves in our minds by size, and designers play to that notion," she said in a demure Southern accent. "It makes us feel good to fit into a 6, but it also makes us feel good to be able to say to our friends that we fit into a size 6. But it can be ridiculous. I bought a pair of pants by Carlisle in a size 0 the other day. Who wears a size 0?" (Dr. Anderson said she usually wears a 4 or a 6.)
The American Apparel Manufacturers Association studies the subject, but Carl Priestland, the association's chief economist, says it's not a simple problem.
"Sizing has been an issue for years simply because there are so many companies out there making garments," he said. "There are seven billion garments consumed in the United states each year that require care labeling. So it's nice and pretty to say we want a standard for each and every piece."
Mr. Priestland said that each year American manufacturers make 200 million dresses, 375 million pairs of jeans, 2.4 billion knit shirts and 45 million dozen pairs of women's panties.
"Can you imagine?" he said. "Forty-five million dozen pairs of women's panties?" To be honest, I can't.
Dr. Anderson explained that American women's sizes originated in the 1940's and 50's, when the Army, confronted with the enormous task of outfitting tens of thousands of women for the first time, tried to create a standard set of sizes. The chart that resulted, outlining the 58 sizes for men and women was published by the Bureau of Standards as Document 454.
Obviously, Document 454 is not taped to the refrigerator of today's designers. Since the 40's, sizes have been shifting as crazily as the San Andreas fault.
The Commerce Department has founded the National Textile Center, a consortium of colleges, to study the subject. The Air Force is conducting a nationwide survey of American bodies to redefine the standard body and size. And as more and more consumers buy clothing in the electronic marketplace -- over the telephone or via the Internet -- Dr. Anderson said, the problem becomes pronounced because consumers rely on sizes, without actually trying on articles of clothing, to determine what will fit them best. Companies like QVC report returns of up to 50 percent on clothing due to poor fit, she said.
But aside from economic difficulties, there are psychological ones. No woman who usually wears a 12 wants to go to her closet and confront a row of stolid, round, pudgy black numbers that read 16 or 18 or 20.
In "The Silence of the Lambs," the drag-queen killer always chose young women who were a size 14 -- and the only moviegoers who remember that piece of trivia are women. And in certain female quarters, the size of Monica Lewinsky's notorious off-the-rack dress continues to inspire guessing games.
I usually call myself a 10. But I've ordered shorts from J. Crew that in a 10, without the tab pocket, are so loose I have to duct-tape them to my body to keep them from falling off. Similar shorts in the same size, with the tab pocket, are so tiny that I have to grease myself like a watermelon on the Fourth of July in order to slide them on.
In noncatalogue clothing, the discrepancies from designer to designer are startling. Tahari makes a jacket that I -- a former competitive swimmer with the shoulders of a Pittsburgh Steeler -- can wear only in a shrinky-dink 6. The skirt, on the other hand, fits in an 8 (svelte), 10 (poochy) or a 12 (nunlike). A benevelont aunt gave me a Mary McFadden couture dress in an 8, and it is broad in the beam. Country Road makes wool pants that fit beautifully in an 8, and viscose pants in the same cut that in a 10 fit like swaddling clothes.
I wear a sample-size 8 in the famously revived Diane Von Furstenburg wrap dress, and a 16 -- gasp! -- in a vintage dress from the 1950's. (Sizes have shifted so much in the last four decades that every woman loves to crow about how Marilyn Monroe wore a 14. But that was back in the days when a 14 was really an 8, so button your lip.)
"It's very upsetting," said one friend who tried on a too-tight skirt in a size 14 at Agnes B. "Even the salesclerk was consoling me."
When men shop, they are not permitted to toy with their psyches the way women do and plot elaborate mazes of self-delusion. Rather than playing a psychological shell game every time they go to the Gap or Brooks Brothers, they simply pick out the pants with a 36-inch waist and the shirt with the 16-inch neck. When they get fat, they are faced with the cold, factual truth -- Got a 44-inch waist, buddy? Well, you're fat! -- and simply scratch off the numbers on the Levi's label.
But every bubble has to burst. That same day on Martha's Vineyard, a friend surprised me with a gift: a blue-and-white polka dot dress, a saucy Marilyn Monroesque number with a halter top and a flared skirt, designed to play fast and loose with a steam grate.
"They didn't have your size," he said, as I twirled in front of the mirror, wearing my new, snug dress.
"Oh? What size is this?" I asked, slowing the gleeful twirling to a more dignified rotation.
"I think it's a, um, a 12," he said, staring down at his hands, clasped humbly in his lap.
I stopped the cake-display twirl. The room was suddenly very quiet. It was a big moment. He looked up, and his terror-filled eyes said: Is she going to hit me now?
"But the lady in the store said the sizes ran really small," he said, painfully nonchalant. "I mean really, really small."
Good answer, I thought, then took a pair of manicure scissors and snipped out the label.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 9; Column 4; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
Three Early Adopters, of Sorts

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL 

 BODY:
WHILE Federal auto choice legislation remains a long shot this year, three states have adopted insurance systems that already give drivers some discretion to limit their right to sue for pain and suffering in exchange for lower premiums.
None offer decisive evidence of how a full-blown auto choice system would play out nationally, but each offers at least modest insights. 

KENTUCKY -- The first state with "choice" -- its changes more than a decade old -- hardly offers one. Those who choose to limit their right to sue for intangible losses like pain and suffering -- the vast majority of drivers -- do not have to impose much discipline on themselves. Lawsuits are permitted as long as the injured party has medical bills of $1,000 -- a low hurdle, indeed.
 The Joint Economic Committee of Congress estimates that a switch to a system that bars virtually all claims for pain and suffering would reduce annual liability insurance costs in Kentucky by an average of $134, or 38 percent. But with premiums already below the national average, rates are not a significant political issue in the state.
 
PENNSYLVANIA -- This state's system looks a lot more like the Federal choice plan. Drivers who choose limited coverage must vault a significant threshold -- having incurred "serious injury" -- to be able to sue for pain and suffering. But full tort coverage remains the default option; to get the limited coverage, drivers must request it from their insurers.
At present, the majority of drivers have full coverage, though David Snyder of the American Insurance Association said that insurers who make the effort find they can sell the limited coverage to about half of their customers. Those who accept limited coverage receive a 20 percent discount on liability insurance.
 
NEW JERSEY -- As in Pennsylvania, drivers can choose limited or full tort coverage. But the default option for those not specifying a preference is the limited coverage. Roughly 85 percent of drivers end up with limited coverage; to sue for pain and suffering, they must meet a threshold that is somewhat weaker than Pennsylvania's. Still, according to Mr. Snyder, drivers opting for the limited coverage in New Jersey's competitive insurance market save 50 percent off the price of unrestricted liability coverage.
New Jersey currently mandates a hefty $250,000 minimum of no-fault personal injury protection. But under pressure from voters to reduce insurance premiums, which remain among the highest in the nation, the state will allow drivers to cut that coverage to just $15,000 beginning next year. That should lure some uninsured drivers back into getting required insurance, but the move could undermine the thrust of the choice system: $15,000 is grossly insufficient to compensate for many injuries, so it is likely that more people will go to court to try to make good their medical costs and lost income.

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
Jobs Look Steady on a Leaner Wall St.

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
STOCKBROKERS often complain that a customer never remembers when their recommendations are proved right, but never forgets when they're wrong.
But if Wall Street, which makes a handsome living from its ingrates, suffers these days from swooning prices, there is little sign that this is putting many jobs at risk. 
 In the 1980's, brokerage houses expanded at breakneck speed as the market and economy emerged from the doldrums, only to contract after the 1987 crash. By contrast, the 1990's have been a time of more sober hiring even as stock prices skyrocketed. "In the past, you've seen hiring booming and busting," said Bill Montgoris, chief operating officer at Bear, Stearns & Company. "Now firms have been much more cautious."
This suggests that even if the stock market plummets, job casualties will remain few, mostly a result of mergers or confined to niches like emerging markets, where disaster has already struck.
Still, there would be a financial pinch -- probably more like a squeeze -- for tens of thousands of people in the securities business for whom annual bonuses have reached lavish proportions in recent years and have driven average industry pay in New York last year to $181,900, almost four times as much as the $48,800 earned by the average city worker. (The securities business includes investment banks as well as brokerage houses.)
An analysis by the office of New York State Comptroller H. Carl McCall concluded earlier this month that in the event of a downturn, "securities firms will likely reduce costs by lowering compensation before resorting to layoffs."
The industry's deliberate pace of hiring can be seen in the fact that it wasn't until June that the New York City economy set a record for the number of Wall Street jobs -- 164,300, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics. That eclipsed the 163,000 total in 1987, before the October crash. The reverberations of that crash led to the loss of one in five securities firms and jobs by 1990, said Jeffrey Schaefer, a consultant to the Securities Industry Association, a trade group.
But why has the latest bull market not led to even more hiring on Wall Street? There is wide agreement on a main reason: Those who run securities businesses today were chastened by 1987.
"They're not staffed as inappropriately as they were back then," said Brian Sullivan, president of his own executive search firm in New York that specializes in financial services. Moreover, he observed, the economy is much stronger now and the industry's "product line" is broader and less reliant on junk bonds and leveraged buyouts.
Other factors that may lessen the industry's vulnerability to a major market drop are vast improvements in technology and efficiencies gleaned through mergers. An industry whose back offices couldn't handle trading volume of 10 million shares a day without expanded hours now breezes through 600 million shares a day and more.
After two companies combine, "you can have two excellent people but you only need one excellent person to do the job," said Vincent T. Pica 2d, a senior official at Prudential Securities. He said Prudential had upgraded its staff by hiring people displaced by mergers.
Still, employment at member firms of the New York Stock Exchange keeps climbing, by 3,384 in the first three months of 1998.
"We're adding pretty much universally," said Sue Weiss, employment vice president at A. G. Edwards & Company, which is based in St. Louis and has 6,352 brokers in 615 offices. She said last week that Edwards expected to add 450 to 550 employees this year.
"The more brokers, the more support they need," Ms. Weiss said. The company has 228 more brokers than it did a year ago.
Despite turmoil in many foreign currencies, officials say that currency trading is one area in which job prospects are probably fading. Mergers are one reason, but even more important is the scheduled introduction in January of the euro, the European currency.
"Lots of firms have shut down their New York trading operation" to leave the field to better-equipped giants, Mr. Sullivan said. "There will be less business across the board -- you're literally eliminating 11 currencies."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "In the past, you've seen hiring booming and busting," said Bill Montgoris of Bear, Stearns. Firms are now more cautious, he said. (James Estrin/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: UPPER EAST SIDE;
Tenants vs. Landlord on Park Ave.

BYLINE: By COREY KILGANNON 

 BODY:
In 1994, shortly after the 13-story building at 823 Park Avenue, near 75th Street, was sold to a developer, Robert Manocherian, tenants of its rent-regulated apartments received notices that he had applied to the state for permission to demolish and rebuild the building's interior. Since then, he has refused to renew the leases on their apartments, whose rents range from $500 to $1,500 a month, far below the market rate for one of the city's most desirable neighborhoods. When Mr. Manocherian took over the building, about 10 of its 37 apartments were vacant, and the number of vacancies has increased to 17, tenants say.
In his demolition application to the State Department of Housing and Community Renewal, Mr. Manocherian, who bought the building from the city after its previous owner had defaulted on property taxes, claims that it is unprofitable. The state may grant a demolition permit and permission to terminate leases if a landlord proves that his building yields a profit of less than 8.5 percent of its assessed value and promises that the rebuilt structure will contain 20 percent more apartments. 
 The tenants, many of whom are elderly, and their lawyer, David Rozenholc, accuse Mr. Manocherian, who owns many properties in Manhattan, of harassing and essentially evicting them so that he can gut the building and construct luxury units. Mr. Rozenholc said harassment had included taking away basement storage space and making unannounced visits to apartments. He said Mr. Manocherian has purposely minimized his profit by leaving apartments vacant and refusing to charge annual rent increases. Once the interior is demolished, he said, the developer would scrap plans for more and smaller apartments. "All this money to build studio apartments on Park Avenue?" Mr. Rozenholc asked. "It makes no real estate sense."
The lawyer claimed that if tenants must give up their apartments, many will be physically and financially unable to find new housing. "It's a building on Park Avenue," he said, "but it's not a Park Avenue building. These people don't have millions stashed away."
Mercia Bross, who has lived at 823 Park Avenue for 27 years and whose husband has cancer, said Mr. Manocherian "told me don't even bother painting my apartment because I'd be out soon."
The Department of Housing and Community Renewal is holding weekly hearings on Mr. Manocherian's application, and neither side expects a quick decision.
Mr. Manocherian's lawyer, Gary Rosenberg, said demolition "was always a possibility." And he insisted that the building was unprofitable. "My client would be delighted to provide all tenants with relocation, not on Park Avenue, but comparable housing with D.H.C.R. standards," Mr. Rosenberg said, adding that Mr. Manocherian had not harassed tenants and fully intended to put in at least 20 percent more apartments.
Anne deSanto said losing her rent-regulated ninth-floor apartment, where she has lived since 1943, would probably force her to enter a nursing home and to give up her china and antiques.
"It's too much trouble to find a new place," said Ms. deSanto, a widow and former schoolteacher, who is in her 90's and testified last week at the state hearings.
A spokesman for the State Department of Housing and Community Renewal, Harry Ryttenberg, called the case "very complex," adding, "We have to be very cautious because you have a sizable amount of tenants whose housing's being threatened, and our goal is to create affordable housing, not demolish it." COREY KILGANNON

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT: FOCUS ON MARKETTURBULENCE;
Shuffling Retirement Deck Is Easier, but Few Play the Game

BYLINE: By MICKEY MEECE 

 BODY:
IN the old days of 401(k) plans, if the market nose-dived the way it did last week, participants who wanted to change their allocations usually had to cool their heels for 30 or even 90 days. Market timing was out of the question.
These days, however, many 401(k) participants can shift assets on the fly, moving money in or out of different categories of stocks and bonds with a single phone call. And they can do so daily if they want, with few restrictions and no penalties or fees. 
 "It's easier than ever for people to switch, and people have more options available," said Tom Schlossberg, president and chief executive of Diversified Investment Advisors in Purchase, N.Y.
Some plans limit the number of times a participant can change their asset allocations to four times a quarter or to once a month. Some also restrict movement in and out of fixed-income investments, to discourage participants from playing interest rates, Mr. Schlossberg said. But the majority of plans allow unlimited changes.
When an investor moves ordinary assets around, capital gains on sales are taxable right away. But gains in 401(k) plans are not taxable until the assets are withdrawn, usually after retirement, so there are significant tax advantages to using 401(k) assets to satisfy any urge to time the market.
While most 401(k) participants now have that opportunity, hardly any are using it.
Ned Burmeister, second vice president of pensions at Principal Financial Group, a 401(k) administration company in Des Moines, offered two possible reasons. Participants are either financially savvy and know they are investing for the long haul, he said, or they are paralyzed by inertia -- not knowing what to do, they do nothing.
During the market gyrations in July, Robert A. Rudell, president of Scudder Kemper Retirement Services in Boston, saw a slight uptick in phone calls, but little shifting of money by its 500,000 participants. "With so little activity in this current market swing," Mr. Rudell said, it shows that "we've done a pretty decent job of education."
In its 401(k) index, which tracks the daily transfers of 1.4 million participants at large companies, Hewitt Associates, a management consulting firm in Lincolnshire, Ill., has found that fewer than 1 percent move money when the market goes haywire.
Last Thursday, for example, when the Dow Jones industrial average fell 4.19 percent, the transfer activity in the index was normal, at 1.22, with a slight shift of assets into fixed-income investments. By comparison, during the market crash of Oct. 27, 1997, the Hewitt index showed four times its normal activity, at 4.0. But even so, just under 1 percent of the participants made changes that day, transferring two-tenths of 1 percent of their $62 billion in assets from stocks to fixed-income investments.
On July 30, when the Dow and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index were both up more than 1 percent, the index showed about twice the average activity -- mostly shifts from bonds to stocks -- said Stacy L. Schaus, a Hewitt consultant. The next day, when both stock indexes tumbled nearly 2 percent, the Hewitt index was at 3.20, with a move into fixed-income investments.
The small minority who do such shifting might not be helping themselves, Ms. Schaus said. Jumping to a different asset category on the day of a plunge means that "they're locking in a loss," she said.
Most people do so thinking that they are timing the market, but they often misunderstand a fundamental difference between changing mutual fund allocations and buying or selling stocks directly.
Karnig Durgarian, managing director and chief of Putnam Investments' defined contribution business in Boston, observed that in a typical plan, where the majority of assets are in mutual funds, transactions do not take place until the end of the day. An investor may call at 10 A.M. hoping to get out of a sliding market early, but the assets must still ride out the day before being transferred, he said. And if things look rosier the next morning, a call at sunup to switch back before prices recover will also be futile.
"You think you got back in early?" he said. "You didn't." Instead, the investor has absorbed all of the bad day's losses and missed all of the good day's gains.

Most investors see 401(k)'s as long-term investments. But there is some movement of assets, as tracked daily by the Hewitt 401(k) Index. A measure of1 means average daily transfer activity over the last 12 months. (Source: Hewitt Associates)

HEADLINE: The Nation: Sample Case;
You Fill Up My Census, Even If I Can't Find You

BYLINE: By STEVEN HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
"OBSCURED by the debate over whether the Census Bureau should use statistical sampling to supplement the next national head count is a little secret: the "actual enumeration" mandated by the Constitution never really takes place.
In the 2000 census, the bureau wants to count everyone who lives in 90 per cent of the households in each of the country's census tracts -- neighborhoods containing about 1,700 dwellings each -- and use the data gathered from them to estimate the remaining 10 per cent who haven't been located. It would then conduct a nationwide survey of 750,000 households and use the results to adjust the official figure up or down. 
 Opponents of sampling say it offends the Constitution, which mandates an "actual enumeration" of the population every 10 years. Their criticism grew even louder last week after a Federal court in Washington said the Census Bureau's plans for using sampling violated statutes governing how the count is to be conducted. When the opponents of sampling say actual enumeration, they mean just that. "You have to go nose-by-nose, head-by-head, neighborhood-by-neighborhood to count people," said Pete Jeffries, spokesman for Representative Dennis Hastert, an Illinois Republican.
 
Dream On
Such comments evoke a dreamy past in which clipboard-toting census-takers troop through communities counting people one at a time. But since the first census in 1790, the traditional head count has been conducted in ways that belie that notion.
"There has never been a kind of line-up-everybody operation where the U.S. Marshals or the census enumerators had to actually see the people," said Margo J. Anderson, author of The American Census: A Social History" (Yale University Press, 1988).
Conducted in a country that has always loathed heavy-handed intrusion by its Federal government, the census has instead relied on the word of whoever in the household chooses to provide the information. If that person gives erroneous information, for whatever reasons -- personal, political, economic -- no one tries to verify it.
In 1970, the Census Bureau moved even further away from the original idea when it began to mail out census questionnaires, and generally relied on the information that was sent back. With mail-back forms, there are no government representatives noting evidence -- bicycles stacked on the porch, raincoats hanging in the hall -- that would look the information-giver in the eye and say, "Are you sure you're the only one living here?"
"In a sense, it was a bigger change in census-taking to go from enumerators going to every house to mailed questionnaires than anything we're talking about now," said Barbara Everitt Bryant, Director of the Census Bureau during the Bush Administration. The fall off in the number of households that mail the questionnaires back -- it dropped to 65 per cent in 1990 from 78 per cent in 1970 -- is a prime reason the Census Bureau wants to change its methods. In the 1990 census, counters went back again and again to households that did not send back the form and to households where no one was home when a census taker came calling. In some cases, households were finally contacted as late as August, a major reason why the 1990 Census had an estimated 26 million errors, including people missed, people counted twice and people counted in the wrong place.
 
Not Just Where, but When
Since census tallies are used for allocating seats in the House of Representative and drawing boundaries for legislative districts, the Census Bureau has to find out not just how many people live in a given neighborhood but how many people live there on the same date, April 1 of the census year. Thus, information gathered in August is barely relevant. And if a census-taker never is able to catch up to the occupants of a household at all, he would ask landlords, neighbors, shop-keepers, even people on the street -- measures that are closer to guesses than actual enumeration.
The Census Bureau has even used a form of sampling to help fill in data. In 1970 Census Bureau statisticians sent counters to a sample of houses that were considered vacant and found that a percentage of them were in fact occupied. This data were then used to extrapolate how many people actually lived in the rest of the nation's presumably vacant dwellings. As a result, a million people were added.
Opponents of sampling say, though, that in the past, those were measures of last resort. This is the first time the census deliberately intends not to at least try to count everyone. What's needed, they say, is for the Census Bureau to merely try harder.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT:FOCUS ON MARKET TURBULENCE;
Flying Through Tumult Of Less-Friendly Skies

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
IN a market that until recently was obsessed with corporate earnings -- and that now seems obsessed with nearly everything -- few sectors have performed as peculiarly as airline stocks.
Business conditions are extraordinarily good for carriers these days, yet airline stock prices have been in a tailspin for weeks. 
 Kevin Murphy, an analyst at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, spent some time late last week, before the outcome of pilot contract negotiations at Northwest Airlines was known, to sort out the conundrum.

Q. Isn't it unusual that airline stocks are getting battered?
 
A. The fundamentals are very strong. We are likely to have record traffic and earnings for all four quarters of 1998. Pricing is good, and fuel prices are at record lows. All of those conditions have made this a very good year.
And yet, since their July highs, the stocks have sold off by more than 20 percent. It seems to me that market sentiment is growing that conditions are as good as they can get and will only get worse.
There were fears that the weak second-quarter gross domestic product number would lead to a real slowdown in the second half. But we are looking for the economy to snap back to more than 3 percent real growth in the last two quarters of the year.
 
Q. Are there other things that investors are not considering as they rush to sell airline stocks?
A. The market seems to be overlooking the fact that interest rates are still low. Traditionally, the up cycle for airline stocks ends when interest rates go up and earnings are disappointing. But interest rates have been steady to lower, and earnings continue to come in as expected or better.
 
Q. Can you give us an example of just how negative investors are at the moment?
 
A. Last month, when American Airlines and Delta announced earnings for the second quarter, both said they would buy back large amounts of stock, a total of more than $1 billion worth.
Stock buybacks in the airline industry are rare because airlines need to spend so much of their cash on new aircraft. And they are doubly rare when the buybacks are of that magnitude. And yet, when they made the announcements, the stocks sold off.
 
Q. What will it take to get a shift in sentiment?
 
A. We have been waiting for the airline group and the market as a whole to stabilize. But until some of the volatility shakes out, it will be difficult for the stocks to mount a sustainable advance.
However, between now and year-end, probably after third-quarter earnings numbers are released, we should get an investible trading rally.
 
Q. What is your outlook for third-quarter earnings?
 
A. Currently, we expect industry earnings will be up about 20 percent in the quarter.
And we are not alone in this regard: The rest of the Street has not lowered any of their earnings estimates. And the companies have been saying that their view of the fundamentals really hasn't changed.
 
Q. Northwest Airlines is one carrier that doesn't seem to have terribly positive fundamentals working in its favor at the moment. They are looking at a major strike, and important Asian routes have been hurt by the economic crisis in the region. The stock is not on your buy list, but you recently gave it an outperform rating. Why?
 
A. Historically, by the time you get to a strike, the bad news is already discounted in the stock. Moreover, times of labor disquiet have usually proved to be a good time to buy a stock.
I think the strike is already priced into Northwest's stock, and I think the Far East is, too.
American Airlines negotiated a contract where costs will go up about 10 percent over the life of the agreement; it was expensive, but the rest of the industry is coming up to where it is.
As long as the economy remains solid, those cost increases in the long run get passed on to the consumer.
 
Q. Are there stocks you like better than Northwest?
 
A. We have outperform ratings on six carriers, including America West, Alaska Airlines and United. And we have strong buy recommendations on American Airlines and Continental Airlines.
 
Q. What makes those two stocks stand out from the crowd?
 
A. American's new computerized reservation system, scheduled to go in place in November, could add upward of $200 million to revenues. Its alliance with U S Airways, were it to go through, would also be beneficial, as would the proposed alliance with British Airways.
Even though much of the turnaround in Continental's fortunes has taken place, we still like the stock. The pending alliance with Northwest should incrementally add to earnings next year.
Continental continues to grow internationally, both across the Atlantic and in Latin America. It is adding new gates in Houston. And its regional jets and commuter flights are a definite opportunity to capture more of the business travel market.
 
Q. Analysts are not supposed to provide advice about which stocks to stay away from. At this point, are there any you would avoid?
 
A. At these prices, there is not much I would stay away from. It is hard to pick a company to short right now.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Kevin Murphy, an airline industry analyst, expects third-quarter earnings to be up 20 percent. He predicts a stock rally by year-end. (Nancy Siesel/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: TURTLE BAY;
Thinking Globally, Acting Locally

BYLINE: By DAVID KIRBY 

 BODY:
Who would have thought that strife between Hindus and Buddhists and a diplomatic dispute between far-off Asian nations could affect an East Side real estate deal? Apparently no one in city government, until a curious City Council aide looked up the proposed buyer, the Kingdom of Bhutan, and raised concerns about its human rights record.
The episode started with a routine meeting on Aug. 20 of the Manhattan Borough Board, which is made up of Manhattan's 12 community boards, its 10 City Council members and the borough president. Among other things, the board votes on the sale of some city-owned property, in this case, the sale of a five-story, $2.3 million building at 763 First Avenue, at East 41st Street, which Bhutan wants for a new consulate. 
 Four members of the board were set to vote in favor of the sale, which had already been approved by the Economic Development Corporation and was supported by Community Board 6. But they were short of the quorum needed. That is when Steve Simon, an aide to Councilman Stanley E. Michels, rushed in, waving a photocopied page from The New York Times Almanac containing the entry for Bhutan.
"We have to stand up to oppression," Mr. Simon explained later. "The City of New York shouldn't be in the business of selling property to an anti-democratic regime." The board is expected to take up the matter again at its next meeting in September.
Since 1991, in the almanac's account, more than 70,000 refugees of Nepalese and Indian descent have fled Bhutan, a remote mountain nation that borders India and China, following anti-Hindu measures passed by the National Assembly, which the almanac calls a "rubber stamp" for the King. Bhutan is predominantly Buddhist.
As Nepal struggles with a refugee problem, Bhutan's National Assembly passed a resolution in July authorizing the forced retirement of relatives of refugees from civil service, according to Amnesty International, which said in its annual report that last year "at least 150 possible prisoners of conscience were detained, many of them incommunicado, without charge or trial." Many of these prisoners, the group said, "were reportedly tortured or ill treated by law enforcement officers."
An employee at Bhutan's Mission to the United Nations said no one was available to comment.
"We are not saying Bhutan has no right to a consulate in New York," Mr. Simon said. "We're simply saying the government itself should not be selling anything to this repressive regime. It gives them very high-profile legitimacy." DAVID KIRBY

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
A Call for 'Auto Choice' and Lower Premiums

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL 

 BODY:
SPENDING more for auto insurance than on car payments? You're not alone.
Although heightened competition, a fraud crackdown and double-digit returns on insurance companies' investment portfolios have kept premiums in check for the last few years, the high cost of auto insurance remains a hot political issue in traffic-choked states from New Jersey to California. And an unusual coalition of would-be reformers, urban officeholders and partisan Republicans is trying to channel that simmering discontent into the most far-reaching changes in auto coverage in a quarter-century. 
 The advocates' motto is "auto choice," which stands for a set of legal changes based on the idea of cutting premiums by limiting the right to sue for damages. Republican Congressional leaders, eager to seize an issue that touches a populist chord, have vowed to bring to the floor in both houses before the November elections a bill that would require states to adopt the auto-choice approach.
Passage of such legislation -- bitterly opposed by trial lawyers and some consumer groups -- would seem unlikely this fall. But hardly anything about the plan, which supporters say would save drivers some $40 billion annually, is following the conventional script.
For example, insurance companies are divided on auto choice, even though they might be expected to stand behind any proposal that clipped the wings of their nemesis, the trial bar. Support for the plan, meanwhile, has come from such disparate political players as the conservative United States Chamber of Commerce and the pragmatic Mayor of Denver, Wellington E. Webb.
The roots of auto choice are found in the wreckage of the last great push for change in auto insurance: the no-fault movement of the early 1970's. In jurisdictions that adopted no-fault coverage -- 13 states, including New York, New Jersey and Connecticut, plus Puerto Rico -- an accident victim's own insurance company pays claims against medical bills and lost wages (called economic losses) up to the policy limits. That contrasts with the "pure tort" arrangement in most other states, in which the injured must collect from those they say were at fault -- typically, the other driver's insurance company.
When states first adopted no-fault plans, it was widely assumed that lawsuits would become rare and that the chunk of insurance premium dollars swallowed by litigation costs would shrink to almost nothing. In fact, legislation in almost every no-fault state left wide loopholes that permitted lawsuits for a range of non-economic losses.
State no-fault laws routinely make exceptions for deliberately caused injuries, injuries caused by drunken drivers and injuries resulting in death. Many states also permit suits for "loss of bodily function," "serious disfigurement" or "temporary disability" -- all examples of injuries serious enough to meet a legal standard, the so-called verbal threshold for litigation. Still others set a dollar threshold; when injuries result in medical bills exceeding some minimum, claims can be brought for pain and suffering.
These dollar thresholds have had a particularly perverse effect, according to Michael Horowitz, a senior fellow at the Hudson Institute, a conservative research group in Washington, who champions auto choice: They encourage the injured to spend extra on physicians and chiropractors to meet the legal minimums -- or even to enlist them in fraud. "It's become a rule of thumb that insurers settle claims for three times the medical costs," Mr. Horowitz said.
 WORSE, perhaps, the tort system rarely compensates those who most deserve to be compensated. In a 1991 study of thousands of accidents in pure tort states, the Rand Institute for Civil Justice estimated that injury victims with economic losses exceeding $100,000 received just 9 cents on the dollar in compensation, on average.
And across the spectrum of tort and no-fault states, a remarkable amount of money is spent just to keep the wheels of justice in motion. According to a study this year by the Joint Economic Committee of Congress, attorney fees absorbed 28 percent of every premium dollar paid for bodily injury coverage in 1996. Fraudulent claims chewed up an additional 13 percent. And agents' commissions and other overhead expenses devoured 25 percent. That left just 34 percent of premiums for compensating accident victims.
Naturally, no-fault seems to work best in minimizing lawsuits in states that set the toughest thresholds for determining whether an accident victim can go to court -- a requirement, for example, that an accident result in "permanent injury or disability." But even in Michigan, widely regarded as the toughest no-fault state, coverage for bodily injuries remains relatively expensive, because the state mandates that every policy provide unlimited coverage of economic losses.
Auto choice aims to accomplish the initial goal of no-fault -- lower premiums and fewer lawsuits -- by putting a price on the right to sue.
Drivers choosing the basic coverage would receive virtually bulletproof no-fault: They would be permitted to sue for pain and suffering only when contending that the injury was intentional or that the other driver was drunk. (They could also sue in the relatively uncommon circumstance in which tangible economic losses exceeded their "personal injury protection" coverage.)
 On the other hand, drivers who wanted the option to sue for pain and suffering could retain it -- but would pay an additional premium.
The price difference would be substantial. According to Stephen Carroll and Allan Abrahamse, researchers at Rand, the cost of providing bodily injury insurance would be 45 percent less, on average, for drivers who opted for basic coverage. And since personal injury protection represents a bit more than half the cost of auto insurance, the cost of a basic policy under the choice system would fall a bit less than 25 percent -- an average of $184 for each car each year, by the Rand calculations.
But there would be new complications. When a driver with full tort coverage was injured by another with a full tort plan, they would be free, of course, to duke it out in court. And when two drivers with basic coverage were in an accident, there presumably would be no suit. But when a driver with tort coverage was hit by a driver with basic coverage, the injured party would have to sue his own insurance company for pain and suffering. That situation -- which could occur often -- "is terribly complicated," said Bob Pike, general counsel for Allstate, one of the nation's biggest auto insurers, which opposes auto choice.
Jeffrey O'Connell, a law professor at the University of Virginia and a proponent of auto choice, says the interaction between tort and basic drivers had a Rube Goldberg feel to it. But, he noted, "it's no worse than the problem with uninsured-motorist coverage, where injured parties must collect from their own insurance company."
 CRITICISM of auto choice comes in many stripes. Some is financially motivated, at least in part. In a membership recruitment letter last year, the Association of Trial Lawyers of America called auto choice "an assault on our livelihood."
Other opposition comes from consumer groups that view any limit on the right to sue as a dangerous constraint on the little guy. Without much thought, the vast majority of drivers would give up their rights in return for premium savings, said Joan Claybrook, president of Public Citizen, the consumer advocacy group founded by Ralph Nader.
Besides, she argued, "drivers who cause grievous harm ought to pay."
Many insurance executives see the whole notion of Federal intervention as the camel's nose under the tent. "How we compensate injury victims should be addressed at the state level," said Mr. Pike of Allstate.
George Priest, a law professor at Yale University, generally sympathizes with initiatives to minimize the cost of settling legal disputes. But he wonders about the justice of auto choice. No-fault plans socialize the cost of accidents, he said, and barriers to suits for pain and suffering reduce the chance of collecting from wrongdoers. Put those together, and auto choice reduces the odds that dangerous drivers will be forced off the road by higher premiums.
 Supporters of auto choice have an answer to each of the objections. To begin with, the state's rights issue is a red herring, argued Mr. O'Connell of the University of Virginia. Under the measure pending in Congress, state legislatures would have the discretion to reject auto choice, while state regulators would have the authority to block its implementation if insurers could not guarantee minimum levels of savings for insured drivers. Indeed, the states' broad discretion to reject or modify auto choice -- included in the bill in order to attract conservatives' votes -- worries some advocates, who remember the process that peppered no-fault insurance with the loopholes that largely nullified its impact.
Supporters also scoff at the idea that auto choice would give a free ride to dangerous drivers. While any no-fault system reduces deterrence of bad driving, Mr. Horowitz argued that the impact would be very modest. "Insurers would still have plenty of reason to charge higher rates to bad drivers," he said, because they are more likely to injure themselves. Besides, he added, drivers who cause injuries could still be sued for economic losses that exceeded the injured person's own coverage.
 However consumers come down on these issues, the potential savings in premiums will win them over, proponents of auto choice say.
They figure that those 25 percent savings in premiums for basic coverage would prove attractive across the board. And the potential savings for two influential groups are even more striking.
Premiums for commercial vehicles would fall by about 28 percent, according to the Joint Economic Committee. Corporations, which now face a disproportionate share of suits for pain and suffering, would enjoy bigger-than-average savings as the number of suits declined; injuries to their own drivers, moreover, are already covered by workmen's compensation.
"It's real simple," said Larry Kraus, a spokesman for the Chamber of Commerce. "Our three million members would save $8 billion annually."
City dwellers are also in a position to reap exceptional savings from restrictions on suits, because urban accident victims are exceptionally litigious. In Washington, D.C., for instance, auto accident rates fell 22 percent between 1985 and 1995, but the number of lawsuits over auto accidents rose 137 percent, according to the Joint Economic Committee. In 1994, the committee found, people involved in auto accidents in Los Angeles were 2.2 times as likely to claim they were injured as people in the rest of the state. In Philadelphia, the multiple was 3.5; in New York City, 2.7; in Newark, 2.4.
 BY no coincidence, a 1991 survey by Robert Klein, then the director of research for the National Association of Insurance Commissioners, showed that the average liability premium was 32 percent higher for drivers in large cities than in other parts of the same states. Hence the support for the overhaul proposals from some big-city politicians. "I favor auto choice for one reason," said Mr. Webb, the Denver Mayor. "My constituents would be a lot better off."
 The numbers help explain the eclectic band of supporters drawn to auto choice. In the Senate, they include Mitch McConnell, Republican of Kentucky; Daniel Patrick Moynihan, Democrat of New York, and Joseph I. Lieberman, Democrat of Connecticut. Other supporters are Representative James P. Moran of Virginia, a centrist suburban Democrat; Republican Governors Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey and John Engler of Michigan; Mayor Rudolph W. Guiliani of New York, and past Presidential candidates like Michael S. Dukakis and Steve Forbes.
To deepen this coalition, Republican Congressional leaders say they intend to cast their insurance proposals in populist terms, just in time for the November election. "Auto choice is for the moms and dads who sit at the kitchen table every month with the family checkbook trying to balance the family needs with the family budget," said Dick Armey of Texas, the House majority leader.
 Two of the biggest auto insurers, USAA and State Farm, support auto choice, on the grounds that it would cut the portion of premium dollars burned up by fraud and litigation. But other major insurance companies are either opposed or are watching from the sidelines.
Trial lawyers are investing heavily in the defeat of auto choice. "We're focusing like a laser beam on this issue," said Richard D. Hailey, the past president of the trial lawyers association.
Hopes for passage rest on a combination of pressure from the Congressional leadership to keep Republicans in line and small-business support drummed up by the Chamber of Commerce. But swing votes will have to come from Democrats.
And legislators might very well feel pressure from their constituents on the topic. While auto insurance is not on voters' minds in much of middle America, it is a volatile issue in densely populated parts of the East and West Coasts. High premiums almost toppled Governor Whitman last year, when her Democratic opponent, James E. McGreevey, made the cost of auto insurance a centerpiece of his campaign.
 Will Washington politicians remember this episode? That depends on how much consumers care about the issue. "Auto choice would give city residents a much needed break on insurance," said Mr. Moran of Virginia. "The big question is whether they'll get the message and explain their feelings to Congress."

GRAPHIC: Illustration/Chart: "Screech, Crash, Claim"
Suppose John and Mary meet the hard way at an intersection: Their cars collide and each driver sustains a mild to moderate injury. Whether they, or more likely their insurance companies, will meet again in court depends on which insurance system is in use in their state. Here is a simplified summary.
 
IN A TORT STATE
Whose insurer pays for the accident depends on who is found to be at fault -- an issue that often must be settled in court. John and Mary may sue each other's insurers for pain and suffering as well as economic losses. They sue their own insurer if the other driver was uninsured.
 
IN A NO-FAULT STATE
John's insurer pays for his economic losses (medical bills and lost wages), and Mary's insurer pays hers, regardless of who is at fault. John could sue Mary, or vice versa, for pain and suffering only if the injuries meet a test of severity or the medical bills exceed a dollar threshold; limits vary from state to state.
 
UNDER AUTO CHOICE
If both John and Mary have chosen full tort coverage, the situation is the same as in a tort state. If both chose the cheaper, "limited" option, the situation is the same as in a no-fault state, except that they can only sue for pain and suffering if the other driver was drunk or deliberately caused harm. If one driver has limited coverage and the other full tort, the full tort driver, only, can sue his or her own insurer for pain and suffering.
(Illustration by Chris Murphy)(pg. 9)

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Without a Splash, a Tiny Fund Stays Ahead

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
WHILE many mutual funds spend a lot of their shareholders' money on splashy magazine ads and TV commercials, Meridian Value budgets zero for marketing to investors. "Our philosophy is to focus on investment performance with the idea that good performance will attract assets to the fund," said Kevin C. O'Boyle, the fund's lead manager.
As a marketing strategy, that clearly hasn't worked. Meridian Value, started in 1994, holds a puny $12 million. Throw in the value of stock grants, and that's what some of Mr. O'Boyle's peers take home in a year. 
 But the focus on performance has worked quite well for investors. The fund has returned an average of 20.4 percent annually for the three years ended Thursday, compared with just 10.7 percent for its peers in the small-capitalization value category, according to Morningstar Inc., the Chicago financial publisher. This year, Meridian Value managed a 2 percent return through Thursday, while its peers lost 13.9 percent.
While Morningstar classifies him as a value manager, Mr. O'Boyle has built his track record at the fund by identifying what he calls "busted growth stocks," shares of fast-growing companies that stumble. He became lead manager in June 1995.
"When a company with a strong balance sheet and a history of generating strong returns from capital fails to meet its earnings estimates for a year, the stock prices tend to decline dramatically," he said. "We find this happens even if the company's three- to five-year business outlook remains strong."
Meridian Value can invest in companies of any size but tends to favor smaller ones; the median market capitalization of the companies in the portfolio is $400 million. Mr. O'Boyle aims to spread the fund's assets evenly among about 40 stocks -- he currently has 38.
He will consider any company in any industry except biotechnology. "Those companies generally have no profit history and often no revenues," he said. "So we have a hard time valuing them."
Otherwise, he looks for companies with rock-solid balance sheets and for industries with strong growth prospects. The fund has more than 14 percent of its assets in retail stocks and hefty amounts in consumer products, telecommunications, health services and technology.
Finding a busted growth stock is one thing, but before he buys, Mr. O'Boyle also identifies a catalyst that will let the company increase its earnings by more than 15 percent a year over the next two years.
One catalyst might be a shift in one or more industry trends. For example, the fund owns shares of Tyson Foods and Sanderson Farms, two poultry processors. This industry suffered from excess capacity for years, Mr. O'Boyle said; shares of Tyson lagged behind the market from 1993 through 1997, while those of Sanderson fell from $19.25 in early 1997 to less than $12 in the fall of that year. But starting last year, consolidation reduced industry capacity, pushing up retail prices; meanwhile, the producers' feed prices declined.
He began buying both stocks last May, Tyson at $20 a share and Sanderson for between $12 and $13. Mr. O'Boyle expects Tyson to earn $1.25 in its 1999 fiscal year; the stock now sells for $17.625, just over 14 times those projected earnings. Sanderson, at $12.25, sells for under 14 times his 1998 fiscal-year earnings estimate of 90 cents a share.
A switch to a more effective business strategy can be a catalyst, too, especially when new competition has beaten down a company's stock. Mr. O'Boyle cited Discount Auto Parts, a do-it-yourself car parts retailer in the Southeast.
The company implemented a "store clustering" strategy, putting many stores close together to fend off competitors like Auto Zone and Pep Boys. The strategy reduced sales at individual stores and cut earnings growth for the company in 1996 and 1997, but sales started picking up earlier this year. The company is also starting a commercial parts delivery service.
Mr. O'Boyle expects Discount Auto to earn $1.90 a share in fiscal year 1999; At $21.50, it trades for just 11 times those projected earnings.
 ANOTHER example is Shoe Carnival, a 100-outlet discount retailer that does business in the Midwest and parts of the South. It more than doubled the number of its stores in 1993 and 1994, but had problems: it sold too many low-margin private-label shoes and struggled with a bloated inventory.
By mid-1996, the company had eliminated its excess inventory by cutting prices and used the resulting cash to pay down debt. The stock traded at $5, just above tangible book value.
But Mr. O'Boyle began buying shares only after Shoe Carnival rolled out a strategy in the second half of 1996 to sell quality-brand shoes at a discount. Sales in the company's established stores began rising shortly thereafter, and Mr. O'Boyle held on, recently adding to his holdings at $11 a share. The stock now trades at $9.25. He expects the company, which trades at just over 11 times his estimate of this year's earnings, to earn 75 cents a share in 1998 and 90 cents a share in 1999.
 Mr. O'Boyle keeps in shape by swimming two miles a day, four days a week after work. "Swimming gives me time to think and relieves the stress from work," he said. He swims competitively with the masters' team at the University of San Francisco.
His proclivity for staying active shows itself on the business side of his life as well. His fund's turnover rate is 144 percent, versus an industry average of about 90 percent. He is quick to dump companies that do not improve their operating results, or whose stock price outstrips their business prospects.
Mr. O'Boyle aims to stay fully invested, but right now, cash makes up a steep 17 percent of the fund.
"I've been having trouble finding companies with prospect of improved operating results because the Asian situation has slowed economic growth and the strong U.S. dollar has slowed export growth," he said. "The economy has been strong in the U.S. for so long that few companies fall into our category of having operating problems for one year."

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT:FOCUS ON MARKET TURBULENCE;
Seeing a Powerful Correction, but No Bear Market

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
BACK in mid-January, the stock market faltered, investors fretted, and Byron R. Wien, chief United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, stepped up to the plate, telling clients that the Dow Jones industrial average, helped by declining interest rates, could hit 9,000 during the first half of the year. It did. But in early August, with the market down sharply from its July 17 high, some people wondered whether to prepare for the next leg of the bull market or to sell. Mr. Wien advised the latter and those who did are glad they did.
On Friday, Mr. Wien shared his opinions about the market turmoil. 

Q. First, what has prompted all this?
 
A. I don't think that Russia, in and of itself, is the factor that has got everybody concerned. It's that Russia is the latest development. What you have is a situation where Europe and the United States were doing well and one by one the rest of the world was getting into trouble.
 
Q. Is there a problem of further contagion?
 
A. The people who are going to be hurt are the people who have lent money to Russia or the people who have had investments there. That's primarily the major German banks and some hedge funds and they've already been hurt. I think the German banks can deal with this and I don't think the hedge funds are a critical economic factor.
 
Q. How is this likely to play out in the United States markets?
 
A. I think we began a topping process in June where the number of stocks performing well was becoming increasingly narrow. Now we're suffering the down side of this enormously favorable first half that we had and my view is that we'll erase all of the gains of this year.
But I don't think we're in a serious bear market because the economic fundamentals of the United States are still quite favorable. The level of employment is still high. Interest rates are low. Housing starts, durable goods orders are still strong. So my belief is that we're going to suffer the worst correction that we've had since October of 1990, but I don't think we're in a bear market.
 
Q. Corporate profits are starting to skid, though.
 
A. One of the factors that could make me wrong is that profits are coming through worse than I and others expected. But they are still positive. In spite of all the things that have happened, the year-over-year profits are still above a year ago. And I think they will be better in the second half than they were in the first half.
 
Q. Is the Dow catching up with the erosion that's been taking place elsewhere?
 
A. Well, I think that the rest of the market, beyond the top 100 stocks in the S.& P. 500, has been hitting a bear market for a while now. More than 60 percent of the stocks on the New York Stock Exchange were down more than 20 percent as of the end of last week. Stocks like Coca-Cola are beginning to suffer along with the rest of the market. Part of my idea of how this would unwind is that some of these large-capitalization multinationals would suffer along with the rest of the market and that would provide the final flush-out that would end this phase of the decline.
 
Q. Is there some flight to quality?
 
A. There were some high-technology stocks like Microsoft, Dell Computer and Cisco Systems that were certainly doing well. Liquidity is playing a big factor in this market. One, if you're a little uncertain about the market, you want to buy something you'll be able to sell if you change your mind. And, secondly, many mutual funds now have grown to the size where they need large-capitalization stocks to put certain amounts of money to work.
 
Q. How confident are you that shareholders will hang in there if the market continues to sell off?
 
A. I'm not confident at all about that. We've seen a few weeks of poor markets and net redemptions. It's possible if we have another bad week that August could turn out to be a net negative month as far as withdrawals. The last time that happened was in the fall of 1990. This won't be over until there are some mutual fund redemptions.
 
Q. Should investors look for stocks that have held up, or been beaten up?
 
A. If you look at the pattern of the market over the last decade or so you'll see that out of every major correction new leadership has emerged. For example, from 1988 to 1990, large-capitalization stocks did extremely well. Then the market corrected as a result of the Iraq invasion of Kuwait and, coming out of that, small- and medium-capitalization stocks did well. Those are the ones I would focus on when the final bottom is reached.
 
Q. So, is your advice to sit tight or to lighten up?
 
A. My advice has been to sell on the rallies because this is going to get worse. But we're probably two-thirds of the way toward the bottom. And next year is going to be a good year. So, if you have stocks that you have long-term confidence in, I wouldn't sell them at distressed prices here. On the other hand, if you have some marginal holdings, where the fundamentals have changed, and you don't have long-term conviction if the market does rally, I would sell those.
 
Q. And the bond market?
 
A. You do have a flight to quality in the bonds. So you could see long-term rates perhaps go to 5 percent, but my view is that if the economy stays strong and these are stabilized, and the Russian situation doesn't infect the rest of Europe, long-term rates will head higher next year.
 
Q. Are there any particular stocks you would recommend?
 
A. The carnage in the bank stocks has been extreme. The airlines have suffered excessively. Those are the only two.
 
Q. Where will the market end this year?
 
A. My feeling is that the market will end the year plus or minus 5 percent of its starting point.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 6; Column 5; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
One Amway Product Your Aunt Won't Sell

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

DATELINE: GRAND RAPIDS, Mich.

 BODY:
AFTER decades of peddling soap, detergent and toothpaste to Middle America, the Amway Corporation, the direct-marketing juggernaut, wants to clean up in another line of business: mutual funds.
Merrill Lynch and Fidelity Investments shouldn't be too concerned. Amway's army of more than one million distributors won't be hawking fund shares at neighborhood coffee klatches or at the office water cooler. Nor will they seek to sign up their friends and neighbors to sell the fund in the type of multilevel marketing plan for which the company is famous. 
 For now, at least, Amway distributors are the target buyers, not sellers, of shares of the Amway Mutual fund. "This is not soap, where we market to our distributors and they sell to customers and receive bonuses based on sales," said James J. Rosloniec, an Amway vice president who oversees the company's mutual fund efforts. "But we would like to make the fund more noticeable."
In short, Amway wants its $162 million stock fund to grow and possibly multiply into a family of mutual funds. That, in turn, could let Amway funds expand into corporate retirement plans and, thus, reach more investors outside the Amway family circle.
Amway, whose $7 billion in worldwide sales makes it one of the country's largest privately held companies, is not a newcomer to the mutual fund game. The fund was started in 1971, making it older than 90 percent of all diversified equity funds. And it is by no means a clunker. While it has never finished at the top of the fund rankings, it has rarely strayed far from the middle of the pack of the large-capitalization funds tracked by Morningstar Inc., the financial publisher in Chicago.
Over the 10 years ended Thursday, the fund returned an average of 14.6 percent a year, less than the 17.8 percent of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index but ahead of the 13.3 percent of its large-cap value peers.
The fund has remained small, Mr. Rosloniec said, because Amway has never really tried to attract investors. "It was started as a sort of paternal thing on the part of the company's owners, a kind of savings program," he said.
Certain Amway distributors, in addition to receiving an annual cash bonus based on product sales, can also receive a profit-sharing bonus, which is paid in fund shares. In January, for example, Amway bought $14.1 million in fund shares, equivalent to about 10 percent of the fund's assets, which it transferred to distributors as bonuses.
Therefore, while the Amway fund is open to any investor who inquires, Amway employees and distributors own the vast majority of the fund's shares. The largest holder, with 15 percent of the shares, is a partnership and foundation affiliated with Jay Van Andel, one of Amway's two co-founders.
The sales expansion effort, at least initially, is likely to be low-key. "I don't anticipate that we'll be taking out an ad in the newspaper next to Fidelity," Mr. Rosloniec said. Instead, the company is likely to include information about the fund in monthly advertising packages mailed to distributors.
Amway is also looking for a "national marketing organization" to help it expand its reach, Mr. Rosloniec said. In addition, shareholders recently approved several changes intended to make the fund "more marketable," he said. Among other things, they eliminated the fund's 3 percent sales charge and added a continuing distribution, or 12b-1, fee, of 0.25 percent; the fee can be used to compensate financial advisers or others who might eventually sell shares of the fund.
So why would a giant direct marketer use intermediaries to sell its mutual fund? Fund shares, like those of ordinary companies, are securities, and to sell them an individual is required to hold a license -- something that most Amway distributors do not have.
Amway argues that the expansion will bolster the fund's assets, which, through economies of scale, may result in a lower expense ratio. Last year, fund shareholders paid 0.90 percent of assets in expenses, not including the 3 percent sales load. This year, including the 12b-1 fee, they will pay 1.15 percent of assets.
Eventually, Amway hopes to reach even more investors by participating in retirement programs. But to crack that market, the company will probably have to expand its menu of offerings.
"It's probably logical for us to have an array of different types of funds" to serve the retirement market, Mr. Rosloniec said, including a fixed-income fund. Also under consideration is a charitable gift trust, similar to one offered by Fidelity, which allows investors to make charitable donations in the form of appreciated shares of stock.
 THE question of who will manage or be the sub-adviser for any new offerings will depend on the funds' investment objectives. The portfolio of the existing fund is guided by Ark Asset Management, a New York money manager that has served as sub-adviser since 1995. At the end of June, the fund's assets were spread across about 80 stocks, mostly blue chips.
Not among them is Procter & Gamble, which has sued Amway twice, and certain of its distributors at least seven times since the early 1980's, contending that the company encouraged its distributors to spread false statements about the company, including rumors linking Procter & Gamble to Satanism. Amway has denied the accusations, contending that Procter & Gamble has engaged in a false public relations campaign of its own.
Prospective investors should be aware of one key difference between Amway Mutual and the company's nonfinancial products. Unlike, say, Amway's Glister toothpaste, the fund does not come with a money-back guarantee.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1330 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT: FOCUS ON MARKET TURBULENCE;
The Bear Draws Blood

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN 

 BODY:
FROM the standpoint of the United States economy, Russia does not matter."
That was the line coming from American stock market optimists until late last week. Their research reports and market comments painted a benign picture of Russian turmoil, at least as it concerned Americans. Of all United States exports, the reasoning went, less than 1 percent were to Russia. The Russian economy was no larger than that of the Netherlands. Few people invested their money there and those who did knew they faced high risks. 
 Investors ignored that logic and panicked anyway.
Russia's financial and political crisis, however removed from the day-to-day business of most American companies and from the portfolios of most investors, prompted the largest one-day selloff of stocks so far this year, sending the Dow Jones industrial average down 357.36 points, or 4.19 percent, on Thursday. A further loss of 114.31 points in a volatile session on Friday left the Dow at 8,051.68, to close the week down 13.8 percent from its peak in July. Most other stock markets also fell, and a flight of capital to the safety of Treasury bonds drove the yield on the benchmark 30-year bond down to just 5.34 percent.
If the current correction in stock prices turns into a full-scale bear market, meaning a retreat of more than 15 percent in the Dow, historians will almost certainly see Russia as the trigger.
The reason, to use the buzzword of the mid-1990's, is globalization: the ugly version.
The good version was peddled right up until the current crisis erupted. It held that capitalism was spreading through the places where socialism once reigned. Tapping new markets and billions of new consumers, multinationals had already begun to earn big money abroad.
The ugly version is of more recent vintage. It goes like this: Currency crises can zip from one changing economy to the next, spreading deflation around the world and leaving recessions in their wake. No one, not the United States or the International Monetary Fund, has the power, or perhaps the will, to do much about it.
From that perspective, Russia is the latest to succumb to a contagion that first broke out in Thailand in July 1997 and shows no sign of slowing its global march. South Korea has more economic heft. Thailand and Malaysia have more foreign investment. But Russia was a vital experiment in capitalism that until very recently was thought to be too important, politically and militarily, to fail.
"Any one of these countries in isolation is not big potatoes in the world economy," said Jeffrey E. Garten, dean of the Yale School of Management, "but they tell a story about the downside of globalization."
When he served in the Commerce Department during the first Clinton Administration, Mr. Garten was a leading proponent of America's global push. Today, he sees the dark side of the trend. "Nobody ever said that globalization was just a great thing, that it only means economic progress for everyone," he said.
Russia's fall makes clear that capitalism -- floating currencies, open borders and free markets -- cannot exist without institutions to support it, Mr. Garten said. The United States has a Federal Reserve Board to act as a backstop in a bank crisis. The Securities and Exchange Commission serves as a cop for the stock market. Bank deposits are federally insured. Global capitalism has no such bulwarks.
"This is one reason we had a global depression in the 1930's," Mr. Garten said. "No one took responsibility. We have been fooling ourselves that the I.M.F. would act as a lender of last resort. They have not. They failed, in Asia and now in Russia."
Russia's decline also shows the power of global price trends. Some see Russia as having aggravated an already troubling downward spiral of prices, or deflation, that is sweeping the world through ever-cheaper manufactured goods.
Prices for these goods began to tumble in Asia in the mid-1990's. Many countries in that region now have full-scale deflation in consumer prices, a phenomenon not seen on such a scale since the Depression. With the devaluation of the ruble, prices of Russian exports like oil and metals, which were already sagging, are likely to slump further.
Falling prices on a broad scale can be insidious because companies earn steadily less for the products they make and because consumers tend to wait before they buy. But while most economists recognize the evil of deflation, little is being done to stop the spread, said Chen Zhao, a managing editor of Bank Credit Analyst, a research and publishing concern in Montreal.
In fact, real interest rates, which take inflation into account, are at record highs in many countries, as governments and central banks try to defend their currencies against speculators. Shortly after Russia devalued the ruble, for example, Canada and Mexico raised rates to defend their currencies. High real rates slow economic growth and put fresh downward pressure on prices.
"Russia is part of the deflation problem," Mr. Chen said. "We don't think there are any major forces that can put a stop to it." He suggests that the United States Federal Reserve should lower interest rates to reduce the relative appeal of the dollar. But he sees few signs that the Fed is prepared to do so unless domestic economic fundamentals take a turn for the worse. "The U.S. does not comprehend the risk," he said.
So far, Russia's internal problems have had only a marginal effect on earnings of companies in the United States. Republic New York, BankAmerica and Lehman Brothers are three financial institutions that have already acknowledged some Russia-related losses. Several high-risk hedge funds took serious hits and others may follow. Steel companies, paper producers, chemical producers and others dependent on commodity prices are also made vulnerable by Russian deflation.
But if the direct losses are relatively minor, the indirect cost, including the toll on investor psychology, is immeasurably large. Investors have already begun to treat "global" as a bad word.
Until recently, many of the largest companies in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index advertised their world ties, and it was not just hype. Over the last decade, the foreign share of sales of S.& P. 500 companies have risen to 34 percent from 25 percent.
Of Exxon's total sales, 88 percent are foreign, according to I.B.E.S. International; Coca-Cola sells two-thirds of its soft drinks abroad. Motorola is 60 percent foreign. Intel and Dow Chemical both sell more than half of their goods overseas. Because of the turmoil in emerging markets and the strong dollar, which reduces the dollar value of foreign sales, I.B.E.S. now projects that all of those companies' earnings will fall this year. Merrill Lynch downgraded Coca-Cola shares last week, citing emerging-markets woes.
After faring relatively well in the summer selloff, shares of large, internationally oriented companies are getting slaughtered. "Globalization is starting to work against us," said Byron R. Wien, chief United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter. "The international luster is tarnished."
James Grant, editor of Grant's Interest Rate Observer and a longtime bear, said Russia's woes and the poor short-term outlook for the global economy left Wall Street bulls with little ground to stand on.
"Globalization means the knee bone is connected to the thigh bone," Mr. Grant said. "When there were these great developments abroad, it was a whole new world of opportunity. It could explain some extraordinary valuations of stocks. Then when it's not working, Wall Street decides that the rest of the world doesn't count for much."
The idea that the American market alone can justify stock prices that were once predicated on world expansion is an exercise in wishful thinking, Mr. Grant said. "You could call it rank cynicism or hopeless optimism," he added. "I don't think anyone will believe it."

GRAPHIC: Photos: As markets dropped on Friday, a crowd gathered at the Australian Stock Exchange in Sydney. Australia's dollar hit an all-time low the day before. (Associated Press)(pg. 5); It was a week when the world's financial markets shared a common bond: high anxiety. A trader at the Tokyo Stock Exchange sought support as the Nikkei index fell on Thursday to its lowest intraday level in 12 years. On Friday, at the Chicago Board of Trade, Thomas Weston tried to keep pace with frenzied trading in options on the Dow Jones industrials. And a Credit Suisse employee watched as European markets continued to drop. (Photographs by Associated Press (Tokyo,Chicago); Reuters (Zurich); illustration by James M. Needham)(pg. 1)
 
Graph: "A Coney Island of the Wallet"
In the last two weeks, nearly every morning seemed to bring American investors dire headlines about spasms in overseas markets, and every trading day a volatile, turbulent stock market. Put the charts together, and the roller coaster that equity investors rode stands out starkly, with the unhappy twist that the plunges often grew steeper, not tamer, as the ride went on. The Dow Jones industrial average, plotted here at five-minute intervals during each trading day, bounced as high as 8,753.78 and as low as 8,011.52 before closing on Friday at 8,051.68, off 373.32, or 4.43 percent over the two-week period. (Source: Bloomberg Financial Markets)(pg. 5)
