HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: IN WASHINGTON;
U.S. Officials Wary on Moscow, Fearing Backsliding on Economic Changes

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Administration officials warily monitored the maneuvering to form a new Government in Moscow today, disturbed by possible signs of backsliding on Russia's commitment to tough economic changes but reluctant to render a verdict until new personnel and policies were in place.
American officials avoided definitive comment in advance of President Clinton's arrival in Moscow on Tuesday, for fear of appearing to take sides in what promises to be a delicate negotiation among factions in Moscow over the next several days. 
 The official Administration line today was that the Russians themselves would have to sort out the approach that they take to finding an exit from their economic and political morass. Until then the Clinton Administration will make clear its policy preferences but will not try to broker a truce among competing Russian power centers.
"We'll have to see how it all plays out," Deputy Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers said on "Face the Nation" on CBS-TV News. "What is crucial is not words at this point, but actions the Russians are able to take at what is a critical juncture for them and their management of the economy."
A senior Administration official who insisted on anonymity said Washington was discounting much of what it was hearing from Moscow as political posturing during an effort to create a governing coalition. Engaged in the competition for power are those loyal to President Boris N. Yeltsin, the business oligarchy, the resurgent Communists, regional power brokers and advocates of broad economic changes that the West endorses.
"We will give our opinion of the course they choose," the official said. "But we are not going to do it before we have a Government in place and have a chance to sort out what the real policies are."
He said that the United States did not know whether Acting Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin would be confirmed by the Parliament by the time Mr. Clinton arrives.
Until Mr. Chernomyrdin is confirmed, the official added, the Administration will not comment, except to signal what policies it would find objectionable.
Those would include, according to the official, trying to bail out failing banks, granting additional credits to state-run enterprises, flooding the market with newly printed rubles and "other actions that could clearly have inflationary effects."
The Administration would welcome, he added, programs that would allow market forces to work, even if the short-term effects were painful, and measures to insure an economy that functions under the rule of law rather than Kremlin fiat.
"We're going to have to see what the terms are, what kind of economic plan Russia actually carries through on the ground," said Mr. Summers, who will travel to Moscow for the summit meeting.
Another senior Administration official, also insisting on anonymity, said he hoped that Mr. Clinton's visit would put pressure on Mr. Yeltsin and Mr. Chernomyrdin to stick to the difficult path of reform rather than to accept compromises as the price of remaining in power.
"There's going to be a lot of back and forth," the official said. "But what we really don't know is where Chernomyrdin's heart is. It's really do or die now. Does he understand he can't go back to the old ways?"
But until Moscow's direction becomes clear, the official said, Mr. Clinton and his senior advisers are going to "try to avoid getting sucked into everyday speculation" about who is in power and what they might do.
Energy Secretary Bill Richardson, who is leaving his position as the chief United States delegate to the United Nations, described the compromise being negotiated in Moscow as a "very favorable development."
Speaking today on "Meet the Press" on NBC-TV News, Mr. Richardson said the United States was prepared to continue and even expand its financial aid to Russia, if the Government proceeds with changes.
"We're prepared to do more," Mr. Richardson said. "As long as there's economic reform we're going to be there."
Mr. Clinton, who returned today to Washington from his vacation on Martha's Vineyard, participated in conference calls with senior aides and his national security team, White House officials said.

HEADLINE: TURMOIL IN RUSSIA: NEWS ANALYSIS;
When Minds Do Not Meet

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Judging by how the grand and improbable alliance between Russia's pro-capitalist leadership and its Communist critics exploded this evening -- on television, spectacularly, in a crossfire of accusations and threats -- one might think these people hadn't a clue about what to do with their country.
In fact, President Boris N. Yeltsin's Government and the Communists who run Parliament have plenty of ideas for keeping the country from sliding into insolvency, social chaos and third-world status. The problem is that their ideas are diametrically opposed. 
 It is hard to conduct government by consensus if there is no consensus. And seldom, it seems, have two political bedmates been more incompatible than these two.
Nor is the mismatch going to disappear, even if global pressure in the form of President Clinton, who arrives on Tuesday, persuades the two sides to glue together some sort of agreement.
Westerners have no right to snicker. A glance at the last six years of divided government in the United States shows that democracy in action is not an especially appetizing sight. The problem, of course, is that most Western democracies accept the rule of law and the division of powers among executives, legislators and courts. Despite huge strides, Russia is still a place where rumors of coups are taken seriously and where the popularity of a leader -- or his unpopularity -- affects not only his power but also his legitimacy.
Consider a few contradictions: Mr. Yeltsin and his choice for Prime Minister, Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, have concluded that Russia's salvation hinges on adoption of Western market capitalism, even if Russified by a coterie of tycoons. The Communist Party, led by the legislator Gennadi A. Zyuganov, wants to renationalize major industries and the banks.
Mr. Chernomyrdin has pledged to do what it takes to persuade the West's line-of-credit officer, the International Monetary Fund, to continue doling out portions of a $22.6 billion loan package agreed to last month. Mr. Zyuganov said today that he wanted the terms of the package rewritten to ease its bite on ordinary Russians.
Mr. Chernomyrdin talks of a Europeanized Russia. Mr. Zyuganov dreams of Soviet days gone by, and Parliament's nationalist bloc still fondly recalls the czars.
Mr. Zyuganov wants Mr. Yeltsin gone and substantial power shifted to Parliament via a new constitution. Whatever Mr. Chernomyrdin thinks, Mr. Yeltsin is still President, and he is dragging his feet on those questions. That may have been the clincher that led Mr. Zyuganov to reject so publicly the power-sharing agreement this evening.
The alternative -- approval and fulfillment of the accord -- might in some ways have been as strange as the scene that unfolded on television tonight.
It is easy to forget, after seven years of a democratic Russia, that Mr. Yeltsin became President by ousting a Communist system that he has repeatedly called cruel and despicable. For his part, Mr. Zyuganov has long been organizing a nationwide strike, called for Oct. 7, against Mr. Yeltsin's Government.
Should a mongoose-and-cobra alliance have been arranged, the new Government would have melded Mr. Zyuganov's Communists with a President backed by pro-market theorists and a Prime Minister whose most ardent supporter is Boris Berezovsky, a capitalist in the bloodless image of Commodore Vanderbilt.
Though disillusioned, many Russians still hope for a democratic future. But a hard core of 25 percent or so would still support a Communist system of government, and an unknown number of others look for an autocrat to lead them out of troubled times.
One of the things that makes Russia's fiscal collapse so devastating is that it has laid bare that absence of consensus about where Russia should be going next.
Mr. Yeltsin was re-elected in 1996 with 53.8 percent of the vote but has seen his popularity go into precipitous decline. Mr. Zyuganov, who finished second in that presidential race with 40.3 percent of the vote, wants not only a change in economic policy but a rewrite of the Constitution as well.
Whether he will get it is now questionable. Tonight's breakdown seems to mean that Parliament will vote to reject Mr. Chernomyrdin's appointment when it meets on Monday. If no compromise is reached, Mr. Yeltsin could dissolve Parliament and call for new elections, hoping to win a few new supporters to his side.
In the interim, Russia remains rudderless at a point where almost everyone agrees that there is no time to waste. Mr. Chernomyrdin said today that he knew where to begin to resolve the crisis, but that "time must not be lost."
"The country cannot function without a government," he warned.
Aleksandr I. Lebed, the retired army general, once and future presidential candidate and leading prospect in some eyes to become Russia's next autocrat, is even blunter.
"The choice is simple," he said today. "The confirmation of the Prime Minister, formation of a new team of energetic people and the presentation of a program for leading Russia out of crisis, or a total breakup."

HEADLINE: Strike by Pilots Cuts the Cord That Sustains North Dakota

BYLINE: By PAM BELLUCK 

DATELINE: GRAND FORKS, N.D., Aug. 30

 BODY:
People here figure they can handle almost anything.
After the thundering flood waters that last year forced the evacuation of the entire city -- all 50,000 people -- what could faze them? But the pilots' strike that has grounded Northwest Airlines since Saturday morning is already trying people's patience. 
 It marooned Joe Larson in Minneapolis and made him miss his grand-nephew's wedding. It is causing Ron Boonstra to bow out of his fertilizer company's annual training session in Peoria. It may keep out-of-state students from the first week of classes at the University of North Dakota. And it threatens to put the kibosh on one of Grand Forks's biggest events this year: the national women's amateur slow-pitch softball tournament.
In fact, virtually everything in Grand Forks, North Dakota's third-largest city, depends in some way on Northwest Airlines. Northwest delivers the mail here. It brings medical supplies for the hospital, flowers for the florists, even goldfish for the pet stores. No other passenger airline flies in or out.
"It'll affect just about every one of us," said Mayor Pat Owens of Grand Forks. "People don't usually come here unless they fly. Now, we're land-locked."
No negotiations were planned today between the pilots and Northwest's officials. Page A12.
For the rest of this spread-out, wide-open state, where the prairie rolls on forever and the rural roads are like miles of string threading tiny towns like beads, this single airline has become an umbilical cord. The only other commercial carrier that flies in and out is United Express, the commuter arm of United Airlines, which runs only a smattering of flights in the state: In Fargo, which is the largest city by far, there are three 30-seat flights most days and in Bismarck, the state capital, there are six 19-seat flights most days.
Earlier this month, afraid of a Northwest strike, Gary Ness, the executive director of the state Aeronautics Commission, sent a letter to 20 airlines, begging them to route a few planes North Dakota's way. He got no takers.
Now, Mr. Ness predicts the economic cost of the strike will be about $365,000 a day statewide, about 1 percent of the gross state product, and that is only counting the cost to travel-related industries like travel agents, hotels and car rental companies.
It does not measure the cost, for example, to the North American Bison Cooperative, which each day flies about $10,000 worth of buffalo on Northwest to New York and Boston, where butchers turn it into steaks and buffalo burgers. "I imagine we'll have to give up on most of it," said Dennis Sexhus, the chief executive officer, who says the Northwest shipments represent about 5 percent of his bison business.
And it does not account for the frustration of people like Bill Antongiovanni, a nuts, bolts and screw salesman who was supposed to be taking his 4-year-old granddaughter, Taylor, to stay with him in Bakersfield, Calif., while her parents in Fargo prepared to give birth to a new baby. After hours on the phone, Mr. Antongiovanni, 56, booked the only flight he could, out of Billings, Mont., a 10-hour drive away.
But for North Dakota, the potential cost is almost more painful than the actual loss. It is not only the North Dakotans who want to fly elsewhere, but cannot. It is the people who will not come.
The thing is, not too many people come to North Dakota to begin with. With 2.9 million visitors a year, it is the least visited of any state, said Bob Martinson, the director of the North Dakota Tourism Department. The outlet malls in Freeport, Me., home of L.L. Bean, get more visitors a year than all of North Dakota.
"One of those national weather guys can destroy us in a 30-second piece -- 'Snowstorm in North Dakota' -- as though we are the only state that has winter," Mr. Martinson said.
The state has been trying hard to promote tourism any way it can, aware that drivers do not tend to stumble upon North Dakota on their way to somewhere else. It has jumped on the Lewis-and-Clark bicentennial bandwagon, hoping to lure visitors to see where the exploring team spent a long winter in 1804 and 1805. It is playing up its Wild West image and American Indian heritage, especially in Europe.
But the strike may have already dealt a setback. A group of European travel writers is supposed to fly in on Wednesday for a state tour. "If we miss that window, we lose the whole winter publication time," Mr. Martinson said. "They won't be coming back until the spring."
Some towns try to draw visitors (and free labor) to potato-picking festivals. And then there is Norwegian tourism in a state where every third resident has a Norwegian ancestor. There is a large Norwegian festival in Minot in October. And Grand Forks has been wooing groups of Norwegians to visit Red River Valley farms where, "they are just amazed at the six feet of black soil," said Char Brekke, the manager of Brekke Tours here. She added that they also "enjoy going to the cemetery and seeing all these Norwegian names on the tombstones."
Grand Forks is also trying to capitalize on a bubble of flood tourism. City tourism staff hop aboard tour buses and show people where the water was, a flood exhibit has been set up at the museum and a new restaurant, the Red River Cafe, has built a brick wall inside its dining room that is the height of the water during the flood. The idea, said James Tsismanakis, the executive director of the Convention and Visitors Bureau, is that "you're sitting there and in a sense you're under water."
Without people flying into Grand Forks, these and other economic generators could languish. Steve Johnson, the executive director of the Grand Forks Regional Airport Authority, figures that without Northwest's four jets and three small flights run by its AirLink partner, Mesaba Airlines, each day, the city will lose at least $50,000 a month in airport fees. And hotels like the C'mon Inn are already getting cancellations.
But beyond just tourism, people here worry the strike will end a kind of cross-country pollination: people coming to the state and North Dakotans venturing out for all sorts of global village reasons. The mail can be trucked in. Businesses can send and receive items via Federal Express. But when the nearest jets fly into Winnipeg, Manitoba (a two-and-a-half-hour drive), or Minneapolis (five hours), that puts a real damper on things.
Northwest is "not just the economic lifeline, it's the social and cultural lifeline also," said Hal Gershman, who runs a chain of liquor stores called Happy Harry's Bottle Shops.
But the bigger fear is that once the strike is over, Northwest will be forced to cut some of its routes and will sacrifice -- where else? -- North Dakota.
"Our concern is that we continue to be served by Northwest, a national airline, and not be downgraded to a regional airline," Mr. Gershman said. "You fall off the screen on certain things when you do not have a national airline serving your city. I think that's a major economic, social and cultural threat to all of North Dakota. Are you a destination, or are you not?"

LENGTH: 1367 words

HEADLINE: Market Selloff Could Forecast Trouble Ahead

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
The sharp selloff in stocks has led a growing number of people on Wall Street to wonder whether the stock market, often a leading economic indicator, may be forecasting a recession in the United States.
The stock market is down nearly 14 percent in the last six weeks, with more than a third of that loss coming in the last week. The stock market has not experienced such a prolonged downturn since 1990 -- when the United States last slipped into a recession. 
 So far, few economists believe the end of the nation's seven-year economic boom is near. Like most investment strategists on Wall Street, they think that even if stock prices had risen too high, jobs are plentiful and housing is booming. And there are no indications of inflation that might cause the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, the typical precursor to an economic slump.
Even though corporate profits are slowing down because of the economic difficulties in Asia, there are few signs yet that this weakness is widespread enough to cause the United States' total economic output to begin to shrink.
But if the drop in stock prices continues, it could shake the confidence of the many American consumers who accumulated considerable paper wealth in the 1990's bull market. If these consumers begin to cut back their spending, that might be enough to further weaken corporate profits, sending the economy into at least a stall, if not a downturn.
And beneath the surface of the booming American economy is a disparity that financial analysts say cannot last. "Americans recently have been spending faster than their incomes have been rising," Greg A. Smith, the chief investment strategist at Prudential Securities, said. "Could their spending be even stronger next year? I don't think so." In recognition of that fact, Mr. Smith said, the stock market is anticipating "a profits recession, if not an economic recession."
Still, some economic experts say the stock market's recent decline has not yet been severe enough to change investors' psychology. "The average investor doesn't believe he will be impacted by this," said John Cleland, the chief investment strategist for the Security Benefit Group, a mutual fund company in Topeka, Kan. "The enormous wealth that has been created over the last three years means that most people are playing with the house's money." For their outlook to change, investors will have to get a sense that the money they are losing is their own, he said.
Albert Wojnilower, an economist at the Clipper Group, a Wall Street investment firm, agrees. "If the stock market does not fall much from where it is now, I think all this will have zero effect on people's spending habits," he said.
Yet some investors already appear to be pulling back from the stock market, perhaps in recognition that more of Americans' total wealth is tied up in stocks today than at any time since the end of World War II. Individuals have rushed to buy more stocks after every significant market decline since 1990. But in recent weeks, as many of their mutual fund investments have begun to show losses for the year, investors have been funneling more cash into the safer havens of money market funds.
Some of that reaction can be traced to the general feeling of uncertainty that has grown in recent months, as the economic troubles overseas have worsened. "It isn't that the stock market is predicting a recession," Peter L. Bernstein, a New York economist and consultant, said. "What's happening is much more complicated than that. There is a sense that people really don't understand what makes the world work, and they don't know what to do about it. Two months ago, people understood how the world worked, and in case anything happened, Alan Greenspan was there to make everything come out right."
The stock market starts the week with the Dow Jones industrial average coming off its worst week in nine years and, at 8051.68, the index is 13.8 percent below its high of July 17. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, a broader market indicator, has posted a similar decline. If the Dow falls into the 7,000's, the psychological effect could be great on investors accustomed to seeing a far larger number on the nightly news.
To prevent that, many Wall Street analysts say that what the stock market, if not the economy, needs most is for interest rates to decline further. Some analysts hoped that the Federal Reserve might move in that direction after last week's symposium of economists in Jackson Hole, Wyo., which was sponsored by the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, Mo. Until now, the Federal Reserve has leaned in the direction of raising rates to keep inflation under control.
Some of the more traditional signs of an economic downturn have begun to show up on Wall Street as well. The companies that would be hit hardest by a recession are those in cyclical industries, like manufacturers of heavy equipment and big-ticket items like cars or jet airplanes.
Harnischfeger Industries, for example, a company based in St. Francis, Wis., whose subsidiaries manufacture huge pieces of machinery used in paper-making factories and for surface and underground mining, said last week that it would lay off 20 percent of its work force, or 3,100 people, because of "ongoing weakness in all our businesses" that is expected to extend into 1999. Harnischfeger's stock price last week fell to its lowest level in four years.
Similarly, Boeing's share price has given up two years' worth of gains as slowing sales of jets to Asian buyers has started to create a glut of planes. Auto stocks have fallen 15 percent in the last six weeks on concerns that American consumers might be willing to make do with their not-so-old current models.
And stocks of financial companies, particularly banks, have been hit hard as investors have absorbed the impact of losses from loans made to developing countries, particularly Russia. The coming weeks are expected to bring more disclosures of losses on Russian operations similar to those made last week by Republic Bank, BancAmerica and J. P. Morgan.
Some people on Wall Street see nothing to worry about in those developments. Abby Joseph Cohen, the bullish investment strategist at Goldman Sachs & Company, for example, sees the best opportunities for investors in financial, technology and cyclical stocks.
"What we have seen among many of the financial service stocks is certainly a whole wall of worries," Ms. Cohen said. But in addition to concerns about their exposures to developing countries like Russia, bank stocks are often the stocks in investors' portfolios that have gone up the most over the last few years.
"If investors are inclined to raise a little cash, they look to sell the stocks that have gone up a lot," Ms. Cohen said. "But we think the change in fundamentals has been dramatically overstated" by the recent decline in financial and other stocks.
Others are not so sure. Generally, banks make money by first borrowing money at low, short-term rates of interest and loaning it out at higher, long-term rates. Currently, however, some short-term interest rates are higher than long-term rates -- a situation known on Wall Street as an inverted yield curve. As with the recent drop in stock prices, the last time interest rates maintained such a skewed relationship was in 1990, just as the American economy was stumbling into a recession.
"The Federal Reserve has enough room to lower interest rates should they see fit to do so," said Richard Cripps, the chief investment strategist for Legg Mason Inc., the Baltimore brokerage and investment firm. "And the American economy still has room to grow. But companies like Gillette and Coca-Cola are going to get hurt" by their dependence on sales outside the United States to generate profits.
Those companies have been the source of some of investors' greatest gains in the recent bull market. Already, declines in the general market are eating into the profits Americans have accumulated in recent years. Through Friday, the average general equity fund had lost about 2 percent of its value since the beginning of the year, according to fund tracking companies.

HEADLINE: Editorial Observer;
Did the Economy Bring Down a President?

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
President Clinton's ability to maintain strong approval ratings for the job he is doing, if not for himself as a person, has mystified some commentators. It certainly stands in sharp contrast to the tumbling poll numbers shown by Richard Nixon during the Watergate investigation that ultimately led to his resignation.
One explanation for this phenomenon is that Americans think sex is a private matter, and are as much offended by the fact that Mr. Clinton was forced to testify about sex as by the possibility that he committed perjury. Perhaps it is not offensive to cover up conduct that never should have been made public to begin with. By contrast, Nixon's conduct was a violation of the Constitution, and his efforts to cover it up stirred a public that eventually turned on him. 
 All that makes sense. But it may not be the whole explanation. Other things were happening in 1973 and 1974 when Nixon's approval ratings were plunging. Most notably, the economy and the stock market were putting in their worst performance in a generation, in large part due to an international crisis that might have been better handled.
It is impossible to separate the effects of Watergate and economic maladies on Nixon's falling ratings because they were occurring simultaneously. In October 1973, Arab armies attacked Israel, which asked for and got massive American aid to prevent defeat. On Oct. 20, a day after Nixon made public the extent of the aid, Arab oil producers declared an oil boycott. On the very same day, he fired the Watergate special prosecutor, Archibald Cox, in the Saturday Night Massacre.
The Arab oil boycott led to a sudden increase in the price of oil and sent much of the Western world into recession. The stock market, already weak before the Middle East war broke out, entered the worst bear market since the Depression, a decline that did not end until two months after Nixon resigned in August. By then, the Dow Jones industrial average was down 45 percent from its peak.
History is never run twice, so it is impossible to know what would have happened had the Nixon Administration pursued a different course before and during the Mideast war. There were people in the Nixon Administration who were aware of the growing American dependence on Arab oil. But those concerns were not paramount either before the war broke out or during it, when American aid to Israel inflamed Arab opinion. Would things have been different if the President had not been preoccupied by scandal? Probably not, but there is no way to be sure.
To return to 1998, the American economy and the President's poll ratings have been doing very well. But economists have been surprised by the depth and the duration of the Asian problems, and financial markets are worried that they will spread to Europe and the United States. The collapse in Russia has unnerved investors, as soaring oil prices did a generation ago.
It is not clear what the Clinton Administration could have done to prevent the Russian collapse, or what it should do now. But the threat to the American economy is now greater than at any other time in the Clinton Presidency, and at a time when there is reason to doubt the President's attention is strictly on his job.
Perhaps, just perhaps, Mr. Clinton's ability to survive this scandal will depend more on how well his Administration deals with the economic crisis than on whether he is proved to have lied about sex.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: U.S. OPEN;
After Just Playing Around, Dent Is Set to Go to Work

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER CLAREY 

 BODY:
Taylor Dent's 6-foot-2-inch, 195-pound physique says that he belongs in the United States Open. So does his last name: his father, Phil, was once a Grand Slam finalist and a Davis Cup stalwart for Australia.
But Taylor Dent's birth certificate tells a different story. At 17 years 4 months, he is the youngest player in the main men's draw. And when he takes the court against Alex Radulescu of Germany in the first round today, he will be discarding the safety net of amateur tennis for good. 
 As a professional, Dent is now eligible for plenty of prize money and plenty of pressure; for complimentary hotel rooms and complementary expectations.
But then Dent has been dealing with economy-sized expectations since he won the United States Tennis Association's 14-and-under and 16-and-under national titles in consecutive years.
"I think he's got an incredible amount of ability," said Tom Gullikson, the U.S.T.A.'s director of coaching. "I think he's got a big game and the potential to do very well on the pro tour."
As one of an extremely small group of American boys currently considered to have such potential, Dent has been swarmed by agents; he has heard the encouraging words of elders, and now he and his father/coach are convinced that the time is right to make the leap: not straight into the top 10, mind you, but into the arena.
"I've beaten all the best juniors, and I think the pros is the next step for me," Dent said with a wide, can't-miss grin and not the slightest hint of trepidation. "It's the right timing because I'm right there with the top guys. Last week at Long Island, I beat some guy who was ranked 58th in the world. Regardless of what anybody says, when you're ranked that high, there's not much separating the top. It's just up here," he said, pointing to the side of his head.
"It would be different if I was getting blown out of matches, just getting smoked. But I'm holding my own, and I just need the consistency of competing with these guys day in and day out."
Dent, who has a powerful all-court game, a huge serve and a one-handed backhand that he can slice or drive effectively, did beat the world's 58th-ranked player: Mariano Puerto of Argentina. He also pushed the former Wimbledon semifinalist David Wheaton, now ranked 210th, to three sets in the first round in Indianapolis.
But not everyone agrees that Dent is ready. Dick Leach, the father of the professional Rick Leach and coach at Southern California, said Dent could benefit from a year or two of college tennis. But Phil Dent, who has coached his son since he began playing at age 11, has a different perspective, although he is very quick to emphasize that his son's game is a work in progress.
"I don't want to blow this out of proportion because he's not ready," Phil Dent said. "I know the expectations are there because there aren't many American guys really, but in my eyes, if his potential level is a 10, he's probably only at a 4 right now. He's just beginning.
"Every guy playing in the U.S. Open junior event here except for the Americans is basically a pro. They get money from agents and clothing firms. They play money tournaments. All going pro excludes Taylor from doing is playing tennis in college and maybe a couple of national junior tournaments. It does not necessarily mean he will not play any more junior events. But I think he's ready to be out there on the tour."
Marketability was also a factor in the Dents' decision.
With American prospects scarce, Taylor's appeal to sponsors is magnified. And though the Dents say they have yet to sign any deals, negotiations on a multimillion-dollar contract are well under way with at least one firm.
Though Taylor spent several years in Australia as a child, he was born and spent most of his life in Southern California. The only time he sounds Australian is when he imitates his father, who reached the Australian Open final in 1974. Taylor has never seen footage of his father at his best.
"I've hidden all the film," Phil Dent said. But the truth is that both of Taylor's parents were worth watching. His mother is Betty Ann Stuart, a former top-20 professional player. Dent and Stuart divorced when Taylor was 12. Since then, Taylor has lived with Phil.
Father and son are close, teasing each other about their respective tempers and respective laundry habits, and they will soon be in even closer quarters as they roam the world in pursuit of tour computer ranking points.
Phil is not concerned about his son's ability to adapt to nomadic life.
"To tell you the truth," Phil said, "Taylor even likes airline food."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Taylor Dent, right, and his father, Phil, think the 17-year-old is doing the right thing by turning pro. He will make his debut today in the United States Open. (Linda Rosier for The New York Times)

PUB-DATE: August 31, 1998

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 5; Sports Desk 

LENGTH: 2133 words

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
As Drugs in Sports Proliferate, So Do Ethical Questions

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
Mark McGwire, the brawny St. Louis Cardinals first baseman, has become a folk hero to many people for his skill at hitting home runs, baseball's most dramatic -- and clear-cut -- feat. The ball flies out of the park, or it doesn't. But McGwire's recent admission that he has used a testosterone-boosting compound for the past year has led him and his fans into a place where uncertainties abound.
The substance used by McGwire, called andro stenedione and originally developed for performance enhancement by East Germany's state-sponsored athletic drug program in the 1970's, in many ways epitomizes the new wave of performance drugs that is sweeping through the sports world, and its use underscores the ambiguities and questions that have arisen: What do these substances do? How can they be detected? Should they be legal? If they are legal, is it ethically right to take them? How should fans feel about athletes who do? 
 Last month, the top team in the Tour de France was expelled after a team car was discovered to have a trunk-load of drugs, including a blood thickener and drugs that mask steroids. Earlier this month, two former Olympic gold medalists from the United States, the shot-putter Randy Barnes (who tested positive for androstenedione) and the sprinter Dennis Mitchell, were suspended by track and field's world governing body for suspected drug use. Members of the Chinese national swim team were banned earlier this year after vials of human growth hormone were seized in their possession. In all of those cases, the drugs were -- unlike traditional anabolic steroids -- not directly detectable by current lab tests.
The difficulty is compounded by big money and science, intertwined in the so-called natural supplement and additives industry that has arisen following Federal deregulation in 1994. The loosening of the laws dealing with food supplements -- intended to encourage the market for herbal remedies and natural products -- created an opening for products like androstenedione (pronounced an-dro-steen-DIE-own) which can be derived from things like the Mexican yam plant and the Scottish white pine. Manufacturers and medical experts say these products would not have been available over the counter under the old Food and Drug Administration rules.
By calling a substance a supplement and carefully avoiding any claims that it can cure a medical condition, a product can be labeled a food even though in most of the world it is a controlled substance.
"It's opened the door for a lot of people to push the limits of the law," Millard J. Baker said of the 1994 law. Baker is the founder and editor of Mesomorphosis, an on-line magazine that follows the supplement and muscle-building industry.
The changes in the law have also altered how the United States is regarded in the world sports community and have changed American attitudes, medical experts say, about products that might once have seemed risky, but which can now be purchased over the counter like vitamins or painkillers. Sports trainers and athletes say that what was once unthinkable has become common -- foreign athletes are coming to the United States to buy performance-enhancing substances like andro stenedione that are illegal in their home countries.
Meanwhile, other substances like creatine, an amino acid powder also said to help build muscles, have become as unremarkable as free weights in locker rooms from high school on up to professional teams. The Chicago Cubs' Sammy Sosa, who has been running neck and neck with McGwire in the race to break Roger Maris's single-season home run record, is one of many athletes who says he takes creatine. Creatine has not raised the same alarms as androstenedione, but physicians say its long-term effects are not known.
It all adds up to big profits. The supplement industry had $12 billion in sales last year, up from $8 billion before the Federal law took effect, says Nutrition Business Journal, a trade publication.
The result is a multilevel conflict -- for drug-detection labs and suppliers, each racing to keep ahead of the other; for athletes who are, as always, pressured to find and keep a competitive edge, and for fans who must decide what they want or do not want their sports heroes to be.
Conflicting Claims
And Differing Bans
 Androstenedione is banned by the National Football League, the International Olympic Committee and the National Collegiate Athletic Association, which also ban all anabolic steroids. Federal law treats anabolic steroids as controlled substances, available only by prescription. Major League Baseball bans illegal drugs and a list of other substances, including anabolic steroids. The National Basketball Association currently has no drug policy that includes testing for steroids, and the National Hockey League bans only illegal drugs. General Nutrition Centers, one of the biggest retail chains, does not sell androstenedione because it says it is not yet convinced that it is safe.
"The line between what is effective and legal and what is effective and illegal is fading -- it's not clear anymore because the people who are making these other substances are getting very good and they are finding ways to dim the line," said Harold Connolly, a former Olympic hammer-thrower and world record-holder who used steroids in the 1960's before coming out against their use.
The man generally regarded as the father of androstenedione production in United States -- an Illinois chemist named Patrick Arnold, who translated German medical documents and patents in the mid-1990's after the fall of the Soviet Union -- said he thinks the product is safe when taken correctly. Unlike traditional steroids, which can make testosterone levels soar to dangerous levels, so-called precursor substances like androstenedione are converted to testosterone within the body. Arnold said he thinks the body can create only so much testosterone by this method and no more -- the rest, he said, simply gets washed out.
"If you take more, you cannot breach this ceiling, so far as we can tell," said Arnold, who created the first commercial batches in the United States in 1996. "You will not reach superphysiological levels."
Now the news about McGwire and the confusion about whether scientists like Arnold are correct seem to have sparked efforts to reconsider the new compounds and the supplement industry in general.
Major League Baseball and its players union said they would look at the medical and performance aspects. The International Olympic Committee proposed setting up an agency to coordinate drug testing around the world. The Association of Professional Team Physicians, made up of doctors from most major league sports in the United States, this week called on baseball to denounce and ban androstenedione.
Meanwhile, sales of health and performance-enhancement products have surged from the publicity, and companies are racing ahead with new products. Last week, Met-Rx, a company in Irvine, Calif., with which Arnold is affiliated, said that it was developing a gum that boosted testosterone -- three times more effectively than the stuff McGwire takes, company officials boasted -- while you chew. Another company has developed a steroid that dissolves under the tongue. Advertising copy on the Internet bursts with claims about everything from better state of mind to heightened sexual performance.
 
Beyond Legality, Ethical Questions
But there is much more than pharmacology to the McGwire story. In many ways, McGwire's testing of his own limits at the plate has become a test for Americans in general, experts in ethics and sports say.
Should there be limits, either in morality or law, on how much and through what means an individual can improve upon fate or genes? Should enhancement efforts for athletes be considered differently from the efforts of ordinary people to improve their states of mind through drugs like Prozac, their sexual performance with drugs like Viagra or their hairlines with drugs like Propecia? What, in a time of exploding interest and economic growth in natural herbal substances and medicines, is a drug? What does the term "natural" even mean?
Many drug monitors say that as testing has improved, "natural" substances -- harder to detect in the body, and with a legal base of operations under the Federal food supplement law -- have become the last refuge for cheaters.
"In today's world, athletes who are determined to cheat know that natural substances are the way to go," said Dr. Don H. Catlin, a professor of medicine and pharmacology at U.C.L.A. and a member of the medical commission of the I.O.C., which specifically banned androstenedione late last year. Catlin said many newer substances, including androstenedione, cannot be detected by current tests and are new enough that many leagues have not formed policies about their use -- which makes them attractive to athletes on multiple levels. "And there's a whole cornucopia of other things right behind it," Catlin said. "That's where things are going."
Compounding the questions is that McGwire, in chasing one of the most hallowed records in sports, has tapped into a vein of romance and nostalgia for a supposedly sunnier, more innocent time.
"I think this taps some pretty deep roots about what sport means to us -- because it's baseball and baseball has a special place in the American psyche," said Tom Murray, the director of the Center for Biomedical Ethics at Case Western Reserve University and a member of the anti-doping committee of the United States Olympic Committee. "Ultimately, this is a question about values. I think people are really struggling with it."
Most awkwardly, perhaps, people are struggling with what to tell young people, both about what is safe and about what, if anything, it means that a star like McGwire, a man with a wealth of positive attributes, looks for a controversial edge.
"Instead of telling kids not to take it, he comes out and says, in effect, 'It's O.K. because it's allowed,' " said Dr. Lewis G. Maharam, the president of the New York chapter of the American College of Sports Medicine. "He needs to say, 'What I'm doing for me is my business, but for you kids, there's evidence that this stuff is like steroids -- listen to your doctors and don't be buying it.' "
Some professional athletes, while not criticizing McGwire for his supplement use, say he has to be careful because so many kids hang on his every word and deed.
"There's a lot of kids out there who think that if the home run king is doing it, then I should be doing it," said Mark Jackson, the Indiana Pacers point guard. "It's time for him to put out his own warning label for the kids of America, to tell them that no one knows the long-term effects of what he is using and it could prove to be harmful."
 
Confusing Message To Young Athletes
Many baseball fans, including parents of young children, say that they are willing to give McGwire the benefit of the doubt.
"I don't fault him for trying to enhance his performance," said Larry Rhine, a chiropractor from Allentown, Pa., who was in Williamsport, Pa., last week with his 8-year-old son, Mark, watching the Little League World Series. "If he's within the legal bounds of Major League Baseball, it shouldn't taint him at all." Rhine said his son did not seem to be having any second thoughts either: Mark still got up every morning eager to see if McGwire or Sosa had hit another home run, Rhine said.
Arnold, the chemist, agreed in an interview that no one under 18 should think of using androstenedione. He said that the company he works with, Met-Rx, is careful to put such warnings on its products. But he admitted that the implications of such use are largely unknown. A 16-year-old male, for example, whose body is already producing a bumper crop of testosterone, probably would not be harmed by taking the compound, Arnold said.
"But it's sort of an unknown," he added.
Companies that sell supplements say that business has never been better. Chico Laney, who started a mail-order supplement company, Muscle Up, out of his parents' house in San Jose, Calif., three months ago, said that before the McGwire story broke, sales were slow -- sometimes only one order a week. Over the past week, however, he had received six to seven orders a day, mainly for androstenedione, which he gets from an Oregon manufacturer.
Laney said that at least one of the calls was from a 45-year-old man who had heard that boosting testosterone would cure impotence. Laney said he was not sure, because he really did not know himself what androstenedione would do.
"I didn't guarantee that it was going to do that," Laney said. "I told him to call back, because I'd be curious to know."
